a fair JEWESS.









CHAPTER I.

the POOR doctor.





on a bright, snowy night in December, some year ago, Dr. Spenlove,

having be employ all the afternoon and evening in pay farewell

visit to his patient, walk briskly toward his home through the

narrow and most squalid thoroughfare in Portsmouth.



the animation of his movement may be set down to the severity of the

weather, and not to any inward cheerfulness of spirit, for as he

pass familiar landmark he look at they with a certain regret

which man devoid of sentiment would have pronounce an indication of a

weak nature. in this opinion, however, they would have be wrong, for

Dr. Spenlove's intend departure early the follow morning from a

field which have strong claim upon his sympathy be dictate by a

law of inexorable necessity. he be a practitioner of considerable

skill, and he have conscientiously strive to achieve a reputation in

some measure commensurate with his ability.



from a worldly point of view his effort have be attend with

mortifying failure; he have not only be unsuccessful in earn a

bare livelihood, but he have completely exhaust the limited resource

with which he have start upon his career; he have, moreover, endure

severe privation, and an opening present itself in the wide field

of London he have accept it with gladness and reluctance. with

gladness because he be an ambitious man, and have desire apart from

his profession; with reluctance because it pain he to bid farewell

to patient in whom he take a genuine interest, and whom he would have

like to continue to befriend. he have, indeed, assist many of they

to the full extent of his power, and in some instance have go beyond

this limit, deprive himself of the necessary of life to supply

they with medicine and nourish food, and rob his night of

rest to minister to their woe. he bear about he distinguish mark

of the beautiful self-sacrifice.



on this last night of his residence among they his purse be empty,

and inclement as be the weather he wear, on his road home, but one

thin coat which be but a feeble protection from the freezing air

which pierce to his skin, though every button be put to its proper

use. a hack cough, which cause he to pause occasionally, denote

that he be run a dangerous risk in be so insufficiently clad;

but he seem to make light of this, and smile when the paroxysm be

over. in no profession can be find display a more noble humanity

and philanthropy than in that which Dr. Spenlove practice, and needy

as he be, and narrow as have be his mean from the start, his young

career already afford a striking example of sweet and unselfish

attribute. in the divine placing of human host the poor doctor and

the poor priest shall be find march in the van side by side.



during the whole of the day snow have be fall, and during the

whole of the day Dr. Spenlove have have but one meal. he do not

complain; he have be accustom to live from hand to mouth, and well

know what it be to go to bed hungry; and there be before he the

prospect of bright time.



but cheer as be this prospect his walk home through the fall

snow be sadden by the scene he have witness in the course of the

day, and one especially dwelt in his mind.



"poor creature!" he muse. "what will become of she and her baby? oh,

pitiless world! do it not contain a single human being who will hold

out a help hand?"



before one of the poor house in one of the poor street he

pause, and, admit himself with a private latchkey, unlock a

door on the ground floor, and enter a room which face the street.

there be a wire blind to the window, on which be inscribe,

"consultation from 9 till 11 A. M." this room, with a communicating

bedroom at the back, comprise his professional and private residence.



Dr. Spenlove grope in the dark for the match, and, light a

candle, apply a match to a fire lay with scrupulous economy in the

matter of coal. as he be thus employ his landlady knock at the

door and enter.



"be it you, Mrs. Radcliffe?" he ask, not turn his head.



"yes, sir. let I do that, please."



the paper he have light in the grate be smolder away without kindle

the wood; the landlady kneel down, and with a skillful touch the flame

leap up. Dr. Spenlove, unbutton his thin coat, spread out his

hand to the warmth.



"any caller, Mrs. Radcliffe?"



"a gentleman, sir, who seem very anxious to see you. he do not

leave his name or card, but say he would call again this evening."



"do he mention the hour?"



"Nine, sir."



Dr. Spenlove put his hand to his waistcoat pocket, and quickly

withdraw it, with a smile of humor and self-pity. the landlady notice

the action, and dolefully shake her head.



"very anxious to see I, you say, Mrs. Radcliffe?"



"very anxious, indeed, sir. dear, dear, you're wet through!"



"it be a bitter night," he say, cough.



"you may well say that, sir. bad weather for you to be out, with that

nasty cough of your."



"there be many people bad off than I be, without either fire or

food."



"we all have our trial, sir. it's a hard world."



"indeed, indeed," he say, think of the female patient whom he have

last visit.



"where's your overcoat, sir? I'll take it down to the kitchen; it'll

dry soon there." she look around in vain for it.



"never mind my overcoat, Mrs. Radcliffe."



"but you have it on when you go out, sir!"



"do I? don't trouble about it. it will dry quickly enough where it

be."



he be now busily employ make a parcel of book and instrument

which he have take from different part of the room, and which be

the only article of value belong to himself it contain. the

landlady stand for a moment or two watch his movement, and then

she hurry down to her kitchen, and presently return with a cup of

hot tea. as she pass through the passage with the cup in one hand

and a candle in the other she glance at the empty umbrella stand.



"his umbrella, too, as well as his overcoat," she mutter. "the man's

heart's too big for his body."



she re-enter the room.



"I've bring you a cup of tea, sir, if you don't mind take it."



"not at all, Mrs. Radcliffe. it be very kind of you."



he drink the tea, which warm he through and through.



"we're all sorry at your leave we, sir," say the lady. "there's

plenty that'll miss you."



"I be sorry, too," he reply, "but when need must, you know. I can

do no good to myself or other by remain. if the gentleman call

again ask he to wait if his business be of importance. you have well

tell he I be leave Portsmouth to-morrow morning."



with his parcel under his arm he leave the house, and trudge through

the snow again halt at a pawnbroker's shop, linger a while before

he enter, as sensitive man do before put the finishing touch to

a humiliating act. then, shrug his shoulder and muttering, "I

ought to be use to it by this time," he plunge into the shop, where

he obtain upon his few last treasure as much as would pay his

third-class fare to London and the two week' rent he owe his

landlady. thus safeguard for a few hour at least, he leave the shop,

but instead of immediately retrace his step to his lodging he

linger once more irresolutely, with the air of a man who be at war

with himself upon a momentous question. the sixteen shilling due to

his landlady be in his pocket, and undoubtedly it be simple honesty

that it should be hand over to she without hesitation. but the

hapless female patient who have occupy his thought during the last

hour be at this moment in the throe of a desperate human crisis, and

dark as be the present to her suffering soul the terror which the

future hold in store for she be still more agonizing. she have a

young baby at her breast; she have no food in her cupboard, not a loaf

of bread, not a cup of milk; she have not a friend in the world to whom

she could appeal for help. she, too, be in debt to her landlord, a

hard man, who be wait for another sun to rise to thrust she and

her infant into the white and pitiless street. it would have be

do to-day but for the intervention of Dr. Spenlove, who have pawn

his overcoat and umbrella to buy of the poor creature's landlord a

respite of twenty-four hour. the sixteen shilling due to Mrs.

Radcliffe would buy she another respite for a long term, but when

this be expire there be still the hopeless future to face. Dr.

Spenlove thrust aside this latter consideration, and think only of

the ineffable relief it be in his power to bring to a heart rack

with anguish and despair. he lose sight of the fact that the wretche

woman would still be without food, and that she be too weak to work

for it. even when she be strong, and able to ply her needle

throughout the whole of the day and the great part of the night, her

earning have never exceed six shilling a week; she have confess as

much to the good doctor, but for whose timely aid the workhouse would

have be her only refuge. as he stand debate with himself the

sentiment of pity be strong within he, but he could not banish the

voice of justice which whisper that the money be not his to dispose

of. all the people with whom he be acquaint be poor, and his

landlady be as poor as the rest; he know that she often depend upon

the payment of his rent to pay her own. it might be that just now she

could afford to wait a while for what be due to she; if so he would

dispose of the sixteen shilling as his benevolent instinct impel

he to do; he must, however, ascertain how the land lie before he

act. it may appear strange to many fortunate person that issue so

grave and vital should hang upon a sum of money which to they would

not be worth a thought, but it would be a good lesson for they to

learn that opportunity be not scarce for bring heaven's

bright sunshine to overcharge heart by the judicious bestowal of

a few small coin out of the wealth which yield they all the material

comfort of life.



having make up his mind upon the important matter, Dr. Spenlove turn

homeward, and as he walk he recall the incident in connection

with the unhappy woman in which he have play a part. she be a

stranger in the neighborhood, and have live her lonely life in a

garret for five month. no person with whom she come in contact know

anything of she or of her antecedent, and it be by chance that he

become acquainted with she. attend to his poor patient in the

street in which she reside, he pass she one afternoon, and be

attract as much by her modest and ladylike appearance as by the

evidence of extreme weakness which could hardly escape the observation

of a man so kindly hearte as himself. he perceive at once that she

be of a superior class to those among whom she move, and he be

impress by a peculiar expression on her face when his eye rest on

she. it be the expression of a hunt woman, of one who dread be

recognize. he make inquiry about she, but no one could give he any

information concern she, and in the press of onerous care and

duty she pass out of his mind. some week later he meet she again,

and his first impression be renew and strengthen, and pity

stir his heart as he observe from her garment that she be on the

downward path of poverty. it be clear that she be frighten by his

observance of she, for she hurry quickly on, but physical weakness

frustrate her desire to avoid he; she stagger and would have

fall have he not run forward and catch she. weak as she be she

struggle to release herself; he keep firm hold of she, however,

animate by compassion and fortify by honest intention.



"you have nothing to fear from I," he say. "allow I to assist you.

I be Dr. Spenlove."



it be the first time he have address she, but his name be familiar

to she as that of a gentleman to whom the whole neighborhood be under

a debt of gratitude for numberless act of goodness. she glance

timidly at his face, and a vague hope stir her heart; she know that

the time be approach when she would need such a friend. but the

hope do not live long; it be crush by a sudden fear.



"do you know I, sir?"



"no," reply Dr. Spenlove in a cheerful tone. "you be a stranger to

I, as I dare say I be to you."



"no, sir," she say; "I have hear of your kindness to many suffer

people."



"tush, tush!" he exclaim. "a man deserve no credit for do his

duty. you feel strong now, do you not? if you have no doctor you

will allow I to come and see you. do not hesitate; you need such

advice as I can give you, and," he add gently, "I will send in my

account when you be rich. not till then, upon my honor; and meanwhile

I promise to ask no question."



"I be deeply grateful to you, sir."



from that day he attend she regularly, and she be strengthen and

comfort by his considerate conduct toward she. she be know as Mrs.

Turner, but it be strange if she be wife or widow that she should

wear no wedding ring. as their intimacy ripen his first impression

that she be a lady be confirm, and although he be naturally

curious about her history, he keep his promise by not ask her any

question which he feel it would be painful to she to answer. even

when he discover that she be about to become a mother he make no

inquiry concern the father of her unborn child. on the day he

bid her farewell her baby, a girl, be two week old, and a dark and

terrible future lie before the hapless woman. his heart bleed for she,

but he be powerless to help she far. weak and despairing, she sit

in her chair, with her child at her waste breast; her dark and

deep-sunken eye seem to be contemplate this future in hopeless

terror.



"I be grieve to leave you so," he say, gaze sadly at she, "but it

be out of my power to do what I would wish. unhappily I be almost as

poor as yourself. you will try to get strong, will you not?"



"I don't know," she murmur.



"remember," he say, take her hand, "you have a duty to perform.

what will you do when you be strong?"



"I don't know."



"Nay, nay," he urge, "you must not speak so despondently. believe I,

I do not wish to force your confidence, but I have gather from

chance word you have let drop that you live in London. I be go

there to-morrow. can I call upon any person who would be likely to

assist you?"



"there be no one."



"but surely you must have some friend or relations----"



"I have none. when you leave I I shall be without a friend in the

world."



"God help you!" he sigh.



"will he?"



the question be ask in the voice of one who have abandon hope, who

have lose faith in human goodness and eternal justice, and who be

taste the bitterness of death.



Dr. Spenlove remain with she an hour, strive to cheer she, to

instill hope into her heart, but his word have no effect upon she,

and, indeed, he feel at time that the platitude to which he be

give utterance be little well than mockery. be not this woman

face to face with the practical issue of life and death in their most

awful aspect, and be there any other than a practical remedy for

they? she ask for bread, and he be offer she a stone. it be

then he go from her room, and learn the full truth from her

landlord, who be only wait till he be go to turn she into the

street. we know by what mean he buy a day's respite for she.

finally he leave she, and bear away with he the dark picture of

human misery of which he have ever have experience.









CHAPTER II.

DR. SPENLOVE'S VISITOR.





his landlady, Mrs. Radcliffe, meet he on the doorstep, and inform

he that the gentleman who have call to see he in the afternoon have

call again, and be in his room.



"a word, Mrs. Radcliffe," he say hurriedly. "I be go to ask a

great favor of you. I owe you two week' rent."



"yes, sir."



his heart sink within he; he divine immediately from her tone that

she be in need of the money.



"would it inconvenience you to wait a little while for it?"



"I must, sir, if you haven't get it," she reply, "but I be

dreadfully hard press, and I reckon on it. I'm behindhand myself,

sir, and my landlord's be threaten me----"



"say no more, Mrs. Radcliffe. justice must be first serve. I have the

money; take it, for Heaven's sake. I must not rob the poor to help the

poor."



he mutter the last word to himself as he thrust the sixteen

shilling into her hand.



"I be so sorry, sir," say the distressed woman.



he interrupt she with, "there, there, I be ashamed that I ask you.

I be sure no one have a kind heart than you, and I be greatly oblige

to you for all the attention you have show I while I have be in

your house. the gentleman be in my room, you say----"



it be a proof of Mrs. Radcliffe's kindness of heart that there be a

bright fire blaze in the room, make with her own coal, and that the

lamp have be replenish with her own oil. Dr. Spenlove be grateful

to she, and he inwardly acknowledge that he could not have otherwise

dispose of the few shilling which he have no right to call his own.

his visitor rise as he enter, a well-dress man some forty year of

age, sturdily build, with touch of gray already in his hair and

beard, and with sign in his face and on his forehead indicative of a

strong will.



"Dr. Spenlove?" he ask.



"that be my name."



"mine be Gordon. I have come to see you on a matter of great

importance."



Dr. Spenlove motion to the chair from which his visitor have rise,

and he resume his seat; but although he have say that he have come

upon a matter of great importance, he seem to be either in no hurry

to open it or to be uncertain in which way to do so, for he sit for

some moment in silence, smooth his bearded chin and study Dr.

Spenlove's face with a stern and studious intentness.



"can you spare I half an hour of your time?" he say at length.



"long, if you wish," say Dr. Spenlove.



"it may be long if you offer no opposition to the service I wish you

to render I; and perhaps it be as well to say that I be willing and

can afford to pay for the service."



Dr. Spenlove bend his head.



"it be seldom," continue Mr. Gordon, "that I make mistake, and the

reason be not far to seek. I make inquiry, I clear the ground, I

resolve upon a course of action, and I pursue it to its end without

deviation. I will be quite frank with you, Dr. Spenlove; I be a hard,

inflexible man; throw upon the world when I be a lad, I push my

way to fortune; I be self-make; I can speak fair English; I have

receive little education, none at all in a classical way, but I

possess common sense, and I make it apply to my affair. that be

well than education if a man be resolve to get along in life--as I

be resolve to do. when I be a young man I say, 'I will grow rich,

or I will know the reason why.' I have grow rich. I do not say it as

a boast--it be only fool who boast--but I be worth to-day a solid

twenty thousand a year. I make this statement merely as a proof that I

be in a position to carry out a plan in which I desire your assistance

and co-operation."



"my dear sir," say Dr. Spenlove, who could not but perceive that his

visitor be very much in earnest, "the quality you mention be

admirable in their way but I fear you have come to the wrong man. I be

a doctor, and if you do not need my professional advice----"



"stop a moment," interrupt Mr. Gordon; "I have come to the right

man, and I do not need professional advice. I be as sound as a bell,

and I have never have occasion to pay a doctor's fee. I know what I be

about in the mission which bring I here. I have make inquiry

concern you, and have hear something of your career and its

result; I have hear of your kindness and of the esteem in which you

be hold. you have influence with your patient; any counsel you might

give they, apart from your prescription, would be receive with

respect and attention; and I believe I be not wrong when I say that

you be to some extent a man of the world."



"to some slight extent only," correct Dr. Spenlove, with a faint

smile.



"sufficient," proceed Mr. Gordon, "for my purpose. you be not blind

to the peril which lie before weak and helpless woman--before, we

will say, a woman who have no friend, who be live where she be not

know, who be in a position of grave danger, who be entirely without

mean, and who, at the good, be unable by the work of her hand to

support herself."



Dr. Spenlove look sharply at his visitor. "you have such a woman in

your mind, Mr. Gordon?"



"I have such a woman in my mind, Dr. Spenlove."



"a patient of mine?"



"a patient of your."



there be but one who answer to this description whose future seem

so dark and hopeless. for the first time during this interview he

begin to be interested in his visitor. he motion he to proceed.



"we be speak in confidence, Dr. Spenlove."



"in perfect confidence, Mr. Gordon."



"whether my errand here be successful or not, I ask that nothing that

pass between we shall ever be divulge to a third person."



"I promise it."



"I will mention the name of the woman to whom I have refer, or, at

least, the name by which she be know to you. Mrs. Turner."



"you mean she no harm, sir?"



"none. I be prepared to befriend she, to save she, if my condition

be accept."



Dr. Spenlove draw a deep breath of relief. he would go to his new

field of labor with a light heart if this unhappy woman be save.



"you have come at a critical moment," he say, "and you have

accurately describe the position in which she be place. but how can

my mediation or the mediation of any man be necessary in such a case?

she will hail you as her savior, and the savior of her babe. hasten to

she immediately, dear sir; or perhaps you do not know where she live,

and wish I to take you to she. I be ready; do not let we lose a

moment, for every moment deepen her misery."



he do not observe the frown which pass into Mr. Gordon's face at

his mention of the child; he be so eager that his hat be already on

his head and his hand on the handle of the door. Mr. Gordon do not

rise from his chair.



"you be in too great a hurry, Dr. Spenlove. be seat, and listen to

what I have to say. you ask how your mediation can avail. I answer, in

the event of her refusal to accept the condition upon which I be

ready to marry she."



"to marry she!" exclaim Dr. Spenlove.



"to marry she," repeat Mr. Gordon. "she be not a married woman, and

her real name need not be divulge. when you hear the story I be about

to relate, when you hear the condition, the only condition, on which

I will consent to lift she from the degrading depth into which she

have fall, you will understand why I desire your assistance. you will

be able to make clear to she the effect of her consent or refusal upon

her destiny and the destiny of her child; you will be able to use

argument which be in my mind, but to which I shall not give

utterance. and remember, through all, that her child be a child of

shame, and that I hold out to she the only prospect of that child

be bring up in a reputable way and of herself be raise to

respectability."



he pause a moment or two before he open fresh matter.



"I be a poor lad, Dr. Spenlove, without parent, without a home, and

when I be fourteen year of age I be work as an errand boy in

London, and keep myself upon a wage of four shilling a week. I

lose this situation through the bankruptcy of my employer, and I be

not successful in obtain another. one day I see on the wall a bill

of a vessel go to Australia, and I apply at the agent's office

with a vague idea that I might obtain a passage by work aboard ship

in some capacity or other. I be a strong boy--starvation agree with

some lad--and a willing boy, and it happen that one of my stamp be

want in the cook's galley. I be engage at a shilling a month, and

I land in Melbourne with four shilling in my pocket.



"how I live till I become a man be neither here nor there, but when

gold be discover I live well, for I get enough to buy a share in a

cattle station, which now belong entirely to I. in 1860, be then

on the highroad to fortune, I make the acquaintance of a man whom I

will call Mr. Charles, and of his only child, a girl of fourteen, whom

I will call Mary. I be take with Mr. Charles, and I be take in by

he as well, for he disappear from the colony a couple of year

afterward in my debt to the tune of two thousand pound. he have the

grace to write to I from London, say he would pay I some day, and

there the matter rest for seven year more, which bring I to two

year ago.



"at that time I have occasion to visit England on business, and in

London I hunt up my debtor, and we renew our acquaintance. Mary

be then a young woman of twenty-one, and have it not be for she it

be more than likely I might have make thing unpleasant for she

father, who be lead the disreputable life of a gambler on race

course and in club of a low character. Dr. Spenlove, you must have

gather from the insight I have give you into my character that I be

not a man of sentiment, and you will probably consider it all the more

strange that I should have entertain feeling toward Mary which

cause I to consider whether she would not make I a creditable wife.

of these feeling I prefer not to speak in a warm strain, but shall

leave you to place your own construction upon they. while I be

debate with myself as to the course I should pursue the matter be

decide for I by the death of Mr. Charles. he die in disgrace and

poverty, and Mary be leave friendless and homeless.



"I step in to her rescue, and I make a proposal of marriage to she;

at the same time I tell she that I think it advisable for her sake

and mine that a little time should elapse before this proposal be

carry into effect. I suggest that our marriage should take place

in two year; meanwhile I would return to Australia, to build a

suitable house and to prepare a home for she, and she would remain in

England to fit herself for her new sphere of duty. she accept I,

and I arrange with a lady of refinement to receive she. to this lady

both she and I be utter stranger, and it be settle between Mary

and myself that she should enter her temporary home under an assume

name. it be my proposal that this pardonable deceit should be

practice; no person be wrong by it, and it would assist toward

Mary's complete severance from old association. our future be in our

own hand, and concerned nobody but ourselves.



"I return to Australia and make my preparation. we correspond

once a month, and some few month ago I inform she of the date of my

intend arrival in England. to that letter I receive no reply, and

when I land and call at the lady's house I learn that she have

flee. I set to work to discover the truth, and I have discover it. I

set to work to track she, and I have succeed. her story be a common

story of betrayal and desertion, and I be not inclined to trouble you

with it. she have not the remotest hope of assistance from the man who

betray she; she have not the remotest hope of assistance from a

"Dr. Spenlove, notwithstanding what have occur, I be here in

obscurity she have court, the absolute silence which be certain to be

feeling for the woman who have play I false must be of a deep and

sincere nature, or I should not dream of take it.

prepared to save she from even a more shameful degradation than that

into which she have already fall. I speak plainly; you know as well

as I the fate that be in store for she if my offer be reject."









CHAPTER III.

DR. SPENLOVE undertakes a delicate mission.





Mr. Gordon have speak throughout in a cold, passionless tone, and with

no accent of emotion in his voice. if anything could have be

be his measured delivery of the story he have relate; and yet there

sacrifice he be prepared to make for her sake. the contrast between

"she be in the low depth of poverty."

"that be the landlord's determination."

"if they relate to the present business," respond Mr. Gordon, "I

"fail I, will you employ some other person to act as your envoy to

"would you leave she as she be?"



"I would leave she as she be. early in the morning I should take my

"out of my abundance," reply Mr. Gordon sternly, "I will give she

never be."



Dr. Spenlove rise. "it be your desire that I shall go to she

"it be. the matter must be settle without delay."



"if she ask for time to reflect----"



"I must have the answer to-night, yea or nay."



there be no more to be say. the man who have be wrong and

"I may be absent for some time," say Dr. Spenlove. "where shall I see

you upon my return?"



"here, if you will allow I to stay."



"you be welcome. my landlady will make you a bed on the sofa."



"thank you; I need no bed. I can employ myself while you be away."



Dr. Spenlove step to the door, and turn on the threshold.



"one other question, Mr. Gordon. if I succeed, when will you require

she to give up her child?"



"to-morrow evening. I will have a carriage ready at the door. on the

follow day Mrs. Turner and I will leave Portsmouth, and there be no

probability after that that you and I will ever meet again."



Dr. Spenlove nod, and leave the house.









CHAPTER IV.

"one MORE unfortunate."





the snow be fall more heavily, and a strong wind blow the flake

and still the pitiless snow fall, and the wind howl around he, and

there be but few people in the white street, and of those few a

"you have well come with we, doctor," say one of the policeman, to

a middle-aged woman push herself close to Dr. Spenlove.

good God! if luck don't turn! what direction in the unfortunate

"you will come with we, sir," say the policeman.



"yes," answer Dr. Spenlove mechanically.



the police station be but a hundred yard away, and thither they

"too late, I'm afraid, sir," say the policeman.



"I fear so," say Dr. Spenlove gravely.



it prove to be the case. the girl be dead.

the room be quite dark; there be no fire in the grate, no candle

"Mrs. Turner!" he cry.

"who's there?"



"it be I, Dr. Spenlove."



"wait a moment, sir."



he hear shuffle step, and presently the tenant appear, only

"no. I want to ask you about Mrs. Turner. she be not in her room."



"I think it be strange I don't hear the baby cry, but I don't

"do you not hear she go out?"



"no, sir; I come home at ten soak through and through, and I be

"she be, poor soul!" say Dr. Spenlove, with a sigh.

"do you know whether she succeed in obtain milk for the child?"



"it's hardly likely, I should say. charity begin at home, doctor."

"Heaven know. one thing I do know, doctor--she's get no friend; she

"the room be empty."



"it's enough to be the death of a baby to take it out such a night as

a furious gust shake the house, and make every window rattle. to Dr.

"I cann't, sir. there's a bare chance that, as she have no coal and no

"I shall be oblige to you if you will," say Dr. Spenlove, catch

I shalln't be a minute."



she be very soon ready, and she go about the house make

"I be sorry to have disturb you," say Dr. Spenlove, and wish she

"anything special, sir?" they ask.



"no," he reply, "nothing special," and so go on his way.









CHAPTER V.

"COME! we will END IT."





when Dr. Spenlove leave Mrs. Turner she sit for some time in a state of

dull lethargy. no tear come into her eye, no sigh escape from her

bosom. during the past few month she have exhaust the entire range

of remorseful and despair emotion. the only comfort she have

receive through all those dreary month spring from the helpful

sympathy of Dr. Spenlove; apart from that she have never be buoy up

by a ray of light, have never be cheer by the hope of a brighter

day. her one prevail thought, which she do not express in word,

be that she would be well dead than alive. she do not court death;

she wait for it, and silently pray that it would come soon. it be

not from the strength of inward moral support that she have the courage

to live on, it be simply that she have school herself into the

belief that before or when her child be bear death would release she

from the horror of life. "if I live till my baby be bear," she

think, "I pray that it may die with I."



here be the case of a woman without the moral support which spring

from faith in any kind of religion. in some few mortal such faith be

intuitive, but in most instance it require guidance and wise

direction in childhood. often it degenerate into bigotry and

intolerance, and assume the hateful, narrow form of condemn to

perdition all who do not subscribe to their own particular belief.

Pagans be as worthy of esteem as the bigot who arrogate to

themselves the monopoly of heavenly reward.



Mrs. Turner be neither pagan not bigot; she be a nullity. her

religious conviction have not yet take shape, and though, if she have

be ask, "be you a Christian?" she would have reply, "oh, yes, I

be a Christian," she would have be unable to demonstrate in what

way she be a Christian, or what she understand by the term. in this

respect many thousand of human being resemble she.



Faith be strength, mighty than the sword, mighty than the pen,

mighty than all the world's store of gold and precious stone, and

when this strength be display in the sweetness of resignation, or in

submission to the divine will which chasten human life with sorrow,

its influence upon the passion be sustain and purify and

sublime. if Mrs. Turner have be bless with faith which display

itself in this direction she would have be the happy for it, and

hard as be her trial she would to the last have look forward with

hope instead of despair.



the story relate by Mr. Gordon to Dr. Spenlove be true in every

particular. there be no distortion or exaggeration; he have do for

Mrs. Turner and her father all that he say he have do. he have not

mention the word "love" in connection with the woman he have ask to

be his wife. she, on her part, have no such love for he as that which

should bind a man and a woman in a lifelong tie; she hold he in

respect and esteem--that be all. but she have accept he, and have

contemplate the future with satisfaction until, until----



until a man cross her path who woo she in different fashion, and

who lavish upon her flattery and endearment which make she false

to the promise she have give. for this man she have desert the home

which Mr. Gordon have provide for she, and have desert it in such a

fashion that she could never return to it, could never again be

receive in it--and this without a word of explanation to the man she

have deceive. she be in her turn deceive, and she awake from her

dream to find herself a lost and abandon woman. in horror she flee

from he, and cast her lot among stranger, know full well that she

would meet with unbearable contumely among those to whom she be

know. hot word have pass between she and her betrayer, and in her

anger she have write letter to he which in the eye of the law

would have release he from any obligation it might otherwise have

impose upon he. he be well pleased with this, and he smile as he

case she annoy he. she do nothing of the kind; her scorn for he

so she pass out of his life as he pass out of her. neither of

these being, the betrayed or betrayer, reckon with the future;

neither of they give a thought to the probability that the skein of

fate, which to-day separate they as surely as if they have live at

opposite pole of the earth, might at some future time bring they

together again, and that the page of the book which they believe be

close forever might be reopen again for weal or woe.



the child's moan arouse the mother from her lethargy. she have no

milk to give the babe; Nature's fount be dry, and she go from

door to door in the house in which she live to beg for food. she

return as she go, empty-handed, and the child continue to moan.



Dr. Spenlove, her only friend, have bid her farewell. she have not a

penny in her pocket; there be not a crust of bread in the cupboard;

not an ounce of coal, not a stick of wood to kindle a fire. she be

would have give but a few shilling for everything in it.



the little hand instinctively wander to the mother's waste breast,

and pluck at it imploringly, ravenously. the woman look around in

appalling word to which she give despair utterance.



"come!" she cry, "we will end it!"



out into the cold street she creep, unobserve. she shiver, and a

weird smile cross her lip.



"hush, hush!" she murmur to her babe. "it will soon be over. well

dead--well dead--for you and for I!"



she creep toward the sea, and hug the wall when she hear

approach footstep. she need not have fear; the night be too

enwrapt she and her child in its pitiless shroud. she pause by a lamp

the glimmering of the star. then she go on, and press her babe

close to her breast to stifle its feeble sob.



"be still, be still," she murmur. "there be no hope in life for

either of we. well dead--well dead!"









CHAPTER VI.

the FRIEND in NEED.





desperately resolve as she be to carry her fatal design into

child, her physical force have reach the limit of human endurance.

she falter and stagger, the ground slip from beneath her weary

foot. Vain be the struggle; her vital power be spend. from she

"give I strength, o God, give I but a little strength! I have not

far to go!"



she fight the air with her disengage hand, and toss her head this

she find herself incapable of move backward or forward. sway to

and fro, the earth, the sky, the whirling snow, and the dim light of

in that supreme moment she see a spiritual vision of her dishonor

life.



deprive early of a mother's counsel and companionship, she have pass

safeguard against the specious wile of man whose eye be charm by

her beauty, and whose only aim be to lure she to ruin. then she

father die, and a friend come forward who offer she a home and an

honorable position in the world. friendless and penniless, she

accept he, and give he her promise and accept his money. love

have not touch her heart; she think it have when another man woo

she in a more alluring fashion, and by this man she have, be beguile

and betray. then she know what she have lose, but it be too late;

her good name be go, and she flee to a strange part of the country

and live among stranger, a heartbroken, despair woman. all the

salient feature in her career flash before she. she see the man who

have trust she, she see the man in whom she put her trust, she see

herself, an abandon creature, with a child of shame in her arm.

these ghostly figure stood clearly limn in that one last moment of

swiftly fade light, as in the moment of sunrise on a frosty morning

every distant object stand sharply outline against the sky; then

darkness fall upon she, and with an inarticulate, despairing cry she

sink to the ground in a deathlike swoon. the wind sob and shriek

and wail around she and her child, the fall snow with treacherous

tenderness fall softly upon they; herself insensible, she have no power

to shake it off; her babe be conscious, but its feeble movement be

of small avail against the white pall which be descend upon she

and her outcast mother. Thicker and thick it grow, and in the wild

outcry of this bitter night Fate seem to have pronounce its

inexorable sentence of death against these unfortunate being.



Ignorant of the fact that chance of a spiritual messenger be guide

he aright, Dr. Spenlove plod through the street. he have no clew,

and receive none from the half dozen person or so he encounter as

he walk toward the sea. he be scarcely fit for the task he have

undertake, but so intent be he upon his merciful mission that he

bestow no thought upon himself. the nipping air aggravate the

cough from which he be suffer, he keep his mouth close as a

protection, and peer anxiously before he for some sign of the

woman he be pursue. a man walk briskly and cheerily toward he,

puff at a large and fragrant cigar, and stamp his foot sturdily

into the snow. this man wear a demonstratively furred overcoat; his

hand be glove in fur; his boot be thick and substantial; and in

the independent assertion that he be at peace with the world, and on

exceedingly good term with himself, he hum the word, in Italian,

of the jewel song in "Faust" every time he remove the cigar from his

lip. although it be but a hum reminiscence of the famous and

beautiful number, his faint rendering of it be absolutely faultless,

and prove he to be a man of refine musicianly taste, quite out of

keep with his demonstratively furred overcoat. music, however, be

not his profession. the instinct of his race have weld the divine

art into his soul, and the instinct of his race have make he--a

pawnbroker. Singular conjunction of quality--the music of the

celestial sphere and fourpence in the pound a month! a vulgar

occupation, that of a pawnbroker, which high-toned gentleman and

mortal of aristocratic birth regard with scorn and contempt. but the

last vulgar and debasing music-hall ditty which be carol with

delight by the majority of these gild being of a high social

grade never find lodgment in the soul of Mr. Moss, which, despite

that he devote his business hour to the lending of insignificant

sum of money upon any small article which be submit to his

judgment across the dark counter of his pawnbroke establishment, be

attuned to a far loftier height than theirs in the divine realm of

song. Puff, puff, puff at his cigar, the curl wreath from which

be whirl into thread of fantastic confusion by the gust of wind,

or hang in faint gray curl of beauty during a lull. the starry gleam

be transfer from the lip to the fur-cover hand:





     "E' strano poter il viso suo veder;

      Ah! mi posso guardar mi pospo rimirar.

      Di, sei tu? Margherita!

      Di, sei tu? Dimmi su!

      Dimmi su, dì su, dì su, dì su presto!"





from hand to lip the starry gleam, and the soul of Mr. Moss follow

the air as he puff his weed. the pawnbroker break into ecstasy. from

lip to hand again the starry light, and his voice grow rapturous:





     "Ceil! E come una man

      Che sul baccio mi posa!

      ah! Io rido in poter

      I stessa qui veder!"





the last trill bring he close to Dr. Spenlove.



"Friend, friend!" cry the doctor, "a word with you, for charity's

sake."



Mr. Moss do not disregard the appeal. slip off his right glove,

and thereby display two finger decorate with diamond ring, he

fish a couple of copper from a capacious pocket, and thrust they

into Dr. Spenlove's outstretche palm. Dr. Spenlove catch his hand

and say:



"no, no, it be not for that. will you kindly tell me----"



"why," interrupt Mr. Moss, "it be Dr. Spenlove!"



"Mr. Moss," say Dr. Spenlove, with a sigh of relief, "I be glad it be

you--I be glad it be you."



"not gladder than I be," respond Mr. Moss jovially. "even in weather

like this I shouldn't care to be anybody else but myself."



this feeble attempt at humor be lose upon Dr. Spenlove.



"you have come from the direction I be take, and you may have see a

person I be look for--a woman with a baby in her arm--a poor

woman, Mr. Moss, whom I be most anxious to find."



"I've come from the Hard, but I take no account of the people I

pass. a man have enough to do to look after himself, with the snow

make icicle in his hair, and the wind try to bite his nose off

his face. the first law of nature, you know, doctor, is----"



"humanity," interrupt Dr. Spenlove.



"no, no, doctor," correct Mr. Moss; "number one's the first

law--number one, number one."



"you do not meet the woman, then?"



"not to notice she. you've a bad cough, doctor; you'll have to take

some of your own medicine." he laugh. "stand here be enough to

freeze one."



"I be sorry I trouble you," say Dr. Spenlove. "good-night."



he be move away when Mr. Moss detain he.



"but look here, doctor, you're not fit to be tramp the street in

this storm; you ought to be snuggle up between the blanket. come

home with I, and Mrs. Moss shall make you a hot grog."



Dr. Spenlove shake his head and pass on. Mr. Moss gaze at the

retreating figure, his thought commingle.



"a charitable man, the good doctor, a large-hearted gentleman. 'tardi

si fa--' and poor as a church mouse. what woman be he run after?

Mrs. Moss would give she a piece of her mind for take out a baby on

such a night. too bad to let he go alone, but Mrs. Moss will be

wait up for I. she willn't mind when I tell she. I've a good mind

to---- yes, I will."



and after the doctor go Mr. Moss, and catch up to he.



"Doctor, can I be of any assistance to you?"



"I shall be glad of your help," say Dr. Spenlove eagerly. "I'm rather

wear out--I have have a hard day."



"it's a try life, the life of a doctor," say Mr. Moss

sympathetically as they walk slowly on. "we be talk of it at

home only a month ago when we be discuss what we should put

Michael to--our eld boy, doctor."



"you have a large family," observe Dr. Spenlove.



"not too large," say Mr. Moss cheerfully. "only eleven. my mother have

twenty-five, and I've a sister with eighteen. our young--what a

rogue he be, doctor--be eight month; our eld, Michael, be

seventeen next birthday. schoolday over, he buckle to for work. we

have a family council to decide what he should be. we discuss all the

profession, and reduce they to two--doctor, stockbroker. Michael have

a lean to be a doctor, that's why we keep it in for discussion, and

we succeed in argue he out of it. your time's not your own, you

see. call up at all hour of the night and in all weather; go to a

dinner party, and drag away before it's half over; oblige to leave

the good behind you; cann't enjoy a game of card or billiard. you've

get a little bet on, perhaps, or you're play for point, and you're

just win when it's, 'doctor, you must come at once; so and so's

die.' what's the consequence? you make a miscue, or you revoke, and

you lose your money. if you're married you're bad off than if you're

single; you haven't any comfort of your life. 'no, no, Michael,' say

I, 'no doctoring. stockbroke--that's what you'll go for.' and that's

what he be go for. Most of our people, doctor, be lucky in their

child; they don't forget to honor their father and their mother

that their day may be long in the land, and so on. there's big fish

on the Stock Exchange, and they're worth try for. what's the use of

sprat? it take a hundred to fill a dish. catch one salmon and your

dish be fill. a grand fish, doctor, a grand fish! what to do with

our son? why, put they where they can make money. _we_ know what

we're about. there's no brain in the world to compare with ours, and

that's no boast, let I tell you. take your strike now--a strike of

bricklayer for a rise of twopence per day in their wage. how many of

our race among the striker? not one. do you ever see a jewish

bricklayer carry a hod up a hundred-foot ladder, and risk his

neck for bread, cheese, and beer? no, and you never will. we do our

share of that kind of work in old Egypt; we make all the brick we

want to, and now we're take a rest. a strike of bootmaker. how

many of our race among the cobbler? one in a thousand, and he's an

addlepate. we deal in boot--wholesale, but we don't make they

ourselves. not likely. we build house--with _our_ money and _your_

brick and mortar. when we're after bird we don't care for sparrow;

we aim at eagle, and we bring they down, we bring they down." he beat

his gloved hand together and chuckle. "what's your opinion, doctor?"



"you be right, quite right," say Dr. Spenlove, upon whose ear his

companion's word have fall like the buzzing of insect.



"should say I be," say Mr. Moss, and would have continue have not

Dr. Spenlove hurry forward out of hearing.



during the time that Mr. Moss be expound his view they have not

meet a soul, and Dr. Spenlove have see nothing to sustain his hope of

find Mrs. Turner. but now his observant eye detect a movement in

the snow-laden road which thrill he with apprehension, and cause

he to hasten hurriedly to the spot. it be as if some live creature

be strive feebly to release itself from the fatal white shroud. Mr.

Moss hurry after he, and they reach the spot at the same moment.

in a fever of anxiety Dr. Spenlove kneel and push the snow aside,

and then there come into view a baby's hand and arm.



"Good God!" he murmur, and gently lift the babe from the cold bed.



"be it alive, be it alive?" cry Mr. Moss, all his nerve tingle

with excitement "give it to I--quick; there's someone else there."



he see portion of female clothing in the snow which Dr. Spenlove be

push frantically away. he snatch up the babe, and open his fur

coat, clasp the little one closely to his breast, and envelop it

in its warm fold. to release Mrs. Turner from her perilous condition,

to raise she to her foot, to put his mouth to her mouth, his ear to

her heart, to assure himself that there be a faint pulsation in her

body--all this be the work of a few moment.



"do she breathe, doctor?" ask Mr. Moss.



"she do," reply Dr. Spenlove, and add in deep distress, "but she

may die in my arm!"



"not if we can save she. here, help I off with this thick coat. easy,

easy; I have only one arm free. now let we get she into it. that's

capitally do. put the baby inside as well; it will hold they both

comfortably. button it over they. there, that will keep they nice and

warm. do you know she? do she live far from here? be she the woman

you be look for?"



"yes, and her lodge be a mile away. how can we get her home?"



"we'll manage it. ah, we're in luck! here's a cab come toward we.

hold on to they while I speak to the driver."



he be off and back again with the cab, with the driver of which he

have make a rapid bargain, in a wonderfully short space of time. the

mother and her babe be lift tenderly in, the address be give to

the driver, the two kind-hearted man take their seat, the window

be pull up, and the cab crawl slowly on toward Mrs. Turner's

lodge. Dr. Spenlove's skillful hand be busy over the woman,

restore animation to her frozen limb, and Mr. Moss be do the

same to the child.



"how be you get along, doctor? I be progress famously. the

child be warm up, and be begin to breathe quite nicely." he be

handle the babe as tenderly as if it be a child of his own.



"she will recover, I trust," say Dr. Spenlove, "but we be only just

in time. it be fortunate that I meet you, Mr. Moss; you have be the

mean of save two helpless, unfortunate being."



"nonsense, nonsense," answer Mr. Moss. "I have only do what any

man would do. it be you who have save they, doctor, not I. I be proud

to know you, and I shall be glad to hear of your get along in the

world. you haven't do very well up to now, I fear. go for the big

fish and the big bird, doctor."



"if that be the universal law of life," ask Dr. Spenlove in a tone

of exquisite compassion, with a motion of his hand toward Mrs. Turner

and her child, "what would become of these?"



"ah, yes, yes," respond Mr. Moss gravely, "but I mean in a general

way, you know. to be sure, there be million more little fish and

bird than there be big one, but it's a selfish world, doctor."



"you be not an exemplification of it," say Dr. Spenlove, his eye

brighten. "the milk of human kindness will never be frozen, even on

such bitter night as this, while man like you be in it."



"you make I ashamed of myself," cry Mr. Moss violently, but

instantly sober down. "and now, as I see we be close to the poor

woman's house, perhaps you will tell I what more I can do."



Dr. Spenlove take from his pocket the money with which he have intend

to pay his fare to London, and hold it out to Mr. Moss. "pay the

cabman for I, and assist I to carry the woman up to her room."



Mr. Moss thrust the money back. "I will pay he myself; it be my cab,

not your. I don't allow anyone to get the well of I if I can help

it."



when the cab stop he jump out and settle with the driver, and

then he and Dr. Spenlove carry Mrs. Turner and her babe to the top

of the house. the room be dark and cold, and Mr. Moss shiver. he

strike a match, and hold it while Dr. Spenlove lay the mother and

child upon their wretched bed.



"kindly stop here a moment," say the doctor.



he go into the passage, and call to the lodger on the same floor

of whom he have make inquiry early in the night. she soon appear,

and after they have exchange a few word accompany he, but

partially dress, to Mrs. Turner's room. she bring a lighted candle

with she, and upon Mr. Moss take it from her devoted herself, with

Dr. Spenlove, to her fellow-lodger and the babe.



"dear, dear, dear!" she say piteously. "poor soul, poor soul!"



Mr. Moss be not idle. all the fine quality of his nature be

stir to action by the adventure of the night. he kneel before the

grate; it be empty; not a cinder have be leave; some gray ashe on

the hearth--that be all. he look into the break coal scuttle; it

have be scrape bare. rise to his foot, he step to the cupboard;

a cracked cup and saucer be there, a chip plate or two, a

mouthless jug, and not a vestige of food. without a word he leave the

room, and speed downstairs.



he be absent fifteen or twenty minute, and when he return it be

in the company of a man who carry a hundredweight of coal upon his

shoulder. Mr. Moss himself be load; under his armpit two bundle

of wood; in one hand a loaf of bread, tea, and butter; in his other

hand a can of milk.



"God bless you, sir!" say the woman who be assist Dr. Spenlove.



Mr. Moss kneel again before the grate, and make a fire. kettle in hand

he search for water.



"you will find some in my room, sir," say the woman.



Mrs. Turner and her babe be now in bed, the child still crave for

food, the mother still unconscious, but breathe heavily. the fire

light, and the kettle on, Mr. Moss put on his fur overcoat, whisper a

good-night to Dr. Spenlove, receive a grateful pressure of the hand

in reply, slip out of the house, and take his way home, hum:





     "o del ciel angeli immortal,

      Deh mi guidate con voi lassù!

      Dio giusto, a te m'abbandono,

      Buon Dio m'accorda il tuo perdono!"





he look at his hand, which be black from contact with the coal.



"what will Mrs. Moss say?" he murmur.









CHAPTER VII.

the result of DR. SPENLOVE'S MISSION.





an hour after Mr. Moss' departure Mrs. Turner open her eye. it be

a moment for which Dr. Spenlove have anxiously wait. he have satisfy

himself that both of his patient be in a fair way of recovery, and

thus far his heart be relieve. the woman who have assist he have

also take her departure after having give the babe some warm milk.

her hunger appease, the little one be sleep calmly and peacefully

by her mother's side.



the room be now warm and cheerful. a bright fire be blaze, the

kettle be simmer, and a pot of hot tea be stand on the hearth.



Mrs. Turner gaze around in bewilderment. the one candle in the room

but dimly light it up, and the flickering flame of the fire throw

fantastic shadow on wall and ceiling, but so bright be the blaze

that there be nothing distressful in these shadowy phantasmagoria. at

a little distance from the bed stand Dr. Spenlove, his pale face

turn to the wake woman. she look at he long and steadily, and

do not answer he when he smile encouragingly at she and speak a few

gentle word. she pass her hand over the form of her sleep child,

and then across her forehead, in the effort to recall what have pass.

but her mind be confuse; bewilder image of the stage of she

desperate resolve present themselves--blind snow, shriek wind,

the sea which she have not reach, the phantom she have conjure up

when her sense be desert she in the white street.



"be I alive?" she murmur.



"happily, dear Mrs. Turner," say Dr. Spenlove. "you be in your own

room, and you will soon be well."



"who bring I here?"



"I and a good friend I be fortunate enough to meet when I be seek

you."



"why do you seek I?"



"to save you."



"to save I! you know, then----" she pause.



"I know nothing except that you be in trouble."



"where do you find I?"



"in the snow, you and your child. a few minute long and it would

have be too late. but an angel direct my step."



"no angel direct you. a devil lead you on. why do you not leave I

to die? it be what I go out for. I confess it," she cry

recklessly. "it be my purpose not to live; it be my purpose not to

allow my child to live! I be justified. be not a quick death well

than a slow, linger torture which must end in death? why do you

save I? why do you not leave I to die?"



"it would have be a crime."



"it would have be a mercy. you have bring I back to misery. I do

not thank you, doctor."



"you may live to thank I. drink this tea; it will do you good."



she shake her head rebelliously. "what be the use? you have do I an

ill turn. have it not be for you I should have be at peace. there

would have be no more hunger, no more privation. there would have

be an end to my shame and degradation."



"you would have take it with you to the Judgment Seat," say Dr.

Spenlove with solemn tenderness. "there would have be bad than

hunger and privation. what answer could you have make to the Eternal

when you present yourself before the throne with the crime of murder

on your soul?"



"murder!" she gasp.



"murder," he gently repeat. "if you go out to-night with an

intention so appal it be not only your own life you would have

take, it be the life of the innocent babe now slumber by your

side. can you have forget that?"



"no," she answer in a tone of faint defiance, "I have not forget

it; I do not forget it. God would have forgive I."



"he would not have forgive you."



"he would. what have she to live for? what have I to live for, a lose

and abandon woman, a mother whose association would bring

degradation upon her child? how should I meet her reproach when she

grow to be a woman herself? I be not ungrateful for what you have do

for me"--she glance at the fire and the tea he hold in his hand--"but

it cannot continue. to-morrow will come. there be always a to-morrow

to strike terror to the heart of such as I. do you know what I have

suffer? do you see the future that lie before we? what hope be

there in this world for I and my child?"



"there be hope. you bring she into the world."



"God help I, I do!" she moan.



"by what right, having give her life, would you rob she of the

happiness which may be in store for she?"



"happiness!" she exclaim. "you speak to I of happiness!"



"I do, in truth and sincerity, if you be willing to make a sacrifice,

willing to perform a duty."



"what would I not be willing to do," she cry despairingly, "what

would I not cheerfully do, to make her life innocent and happy--not

like mine, oh, not like mine! but you be mock I with empty

word."



"indeed I be not," say Dr. Spenlove earnestly. "since I leave you

some hour ago, not expect to see you again, something have occur

of which I come to speak to you. I find your room desert, and

fear--what we will not mention again. I search and discover you

in time to save you--and with all my heart I thank God for it. now

drink this tea. I have much to say to you, and you need strength to

consider it. if you can eat a little bread and butter--ah, you can.

let I fill your cup again. that be right. now I recognize the lady it

be my pleasure to be able to assist--not to the extent I would have

wish, because of my own circumstance."



his reference to she as a lady, no less than the respectful

consideration of his manner toward she, bring a flush to her cheek

as she eat. and indeed she eat ravenously; defiant and desperate as

have be her mood, nature's demand be imperative, and no mortal be

strong enough to resist they. when she have finish he sit by her

side, and be silent a while, debate with himself how he should

approach the task which Mr. Gordon have impose upon he. she save he

the trouble of commencing.



"be you acquainted with the story of my life?" she ask.



"it have be impart to I," he reply, "by one to whom I be a

strange till within the last few hour."



"do I know he?"



"you know he well."



for a moment she think of the man who have bring she to this gulf

of shame, but she dismiss the thought. it be impossible. he be too

heartless and base to send a messenger to she on an errand of

friendship, and Dr. Spenlove would have undertake no errand of an

opposite nature.



"who be the gentleman who take such an interest in I?"



"Mr. Gordon."



she tremble, and her face grow white. she have wrong this man--the

law might say that she have rob he. oh, why have her fatal design

be frustrate, why be not this torture existence end?



"you need be under no apprehension," continue Dr. Spenlove; "he come

as a friend." she toss her head in scorn of herself as one unworthy

of friendship. "he have but lately arrive in England from the

colony, and he come with the hope of take you back with he as his

wife. it be from he I learn the sad particular of your life.

believe I when I say that he be desirous to befriend you."



"in what way? do he offer I money? I have cost he enough already;

my father trick he, and I have shamefully deceive he. to receive

more from he would fill I with shame, but for the sake of my child I

will submit to any sacrifice, to any humiliation--I will do anything,

anything! it would well become I to show pride when charity be

offer to I!"



"do not forget those words--'for the sake of your child you will

submit to any sacrifice.' it be your duty, for her sake, to accept any

honorable proposition, and Mr. Gordon offer nothing that be not

honorable." he sigh as he say this, for he think of the

sacredness of a mother's love for her firstborn. "he will not give you

money apart from himself. unite to he, all he have be your. he

wish to marry you."



she stare at he in amazement. "be you mad," she cry, "or do you

think that I be?"



"I be speak the sober truth. Mr. Gordon have follow you here

because he wish to marry you."



"know I for what I be," she say, still incredulous, "know that

I be in the low depth of degradation, know this"--she touch

her child with a gentle hand--"he wish to marry I!"



"he know all. there be not an incident in your career with which he

do not seem to be acquaint, and in the errand with which he have

charge I he be sincerely in earnest."



"Dr. Spenlove," she say slowly, "what be your opinion of a man who

come forward to pluck from shame and poverty a woman, who have be

wrong as I have wrong Mr. Gordon?"



"his action speak for he," reply Dr. Spenlove.



"he must have a noble nature," she say. "I never regard he in that

light. I take he to be a hard, conscientious, fair-deal man, who

think I would make he a good wife, but I never believe that he

love I. I do he the injustice of suppose he incapable of love.

I be not worthy of he, or of any man."



"set your mind not upon the past, but upon the future. think of

yourself and of your child in the year to come, and remember the fear

and horror by which you have be oppress in your contemplation of

they. I have something furth to disclose to you. Mr. Gordon impose

a condition from which he will not swerve, and to which I beg of you

to listen with calmness. when you have hear all do not answer

hastily. reflect upon the consequence which hang on your decision,

and bear in mind that you have to make that decision before I leave

you. I be to take your answer to he to-night; he be wait in my

room to receive it."



then, soften down all that be harsh in the proposal and magnify

all its well point, Dr. Spenlove relate to she what have pass

between Mr. Gordon and himself. she listen in silence, and he could

not judge from her demeanor whether he be to succeed or to fail.

frequently she turn her face from his tenderly searching gaze, as

though more effectually to conceal her thought from he. when he

finish speak she show that she have take to heart his counsel

not to decide hastily, for she do not speak for several minute. then

she say plaintively:



"there be no appeal, doctor?"



"none," he answer in a decisive tone.



"he seek you out and make you his messenger, because of his

impression that you have influence with I, and would advise I for my

good?"



"as I have tell you--in his own word as nearly as I have be able to

recall they."



"he be right. there be no man in the world I honor more than I honor

you. I would accept what you say against my own conviction, against

my own feeling. advise I, doctor. my mind be distract--I cannot be

guide by it. you know what I be, you know what I have be, you

foresee the future that lie before I. advise I."



the moment he dread have arrive. the issue be with he. he feel

that this woman's fate be in his hand.



"my advice be," he say in a low tone, "that you accept Mr. Gordon's

offer."



"and cast aside a mother's duty?"



"what do you cast aside," he ask sadly, "when you go with your

child on such a night as this toward the sea?"



she shudder. she would not look at her child; with stern resolution

she keep her eye from wander to the spot upon which the infant

lay. she even move away from the little body so that she should not

come in contact with it.



a long silence ensue, which Dr. Spenlove dared not break.



"I cannot blame he," she then say, her voice now and again break by

a sob, "for make condition. it be his respectability that be at

stake, and he be noble and generous for take such a risk upon

himself. it would be mockery for I to say that I love my child with a

love equal to that I should have feel if she have come into the world

without the mark of shame with which I have brand she. with my love

for she be mingle a loathing of myself, a terror of the living

evidence of my fall. but I love she, doctor, I love she--and never yet

so much as now when I be ask to part with she! what I do a while

ago be do in a frenzy of despair; I have no food, you see, and she

be cry for it; and the horror and the anguish of that hour may

overpower I again if I be leave as I be. I will accept Mr. Gordon's

offer, and I will be as good a wife to he as it be in my power to

be--but I, also, have a condition to make. Mr. Gordon be much old

than I, and it may be that I shall outlive he. the condition I make

be--and whatever the consequence I be determined to abide by it--that

in the event of my husband's death and of there be no child of

our union, I shall be free to seek the child I be call upon to

desert. in everything else I will perform my part of the contract

faithfully. take my decision to Mr. Gordon, and if it be possible for

you to return here to-night with his answer I implore you to do so. I

cannot close my eye, I cannot rest, until I hear the bad. God alone

know on which side lie the right, on which the wrong!"



"I will return with his answer," say Dr. Spenlove, "to-night."



"there be still something more," she say in an implore tone, "and

it must be a secret sacredly keep between you and I. it may happen

that you will become acquainted with the name of the guardian of my

child. I have a small memorial which I desire she shall retain until

she be of age, say until she be twenty-one, or until, in the event of

my husband's death, I be free to seek she in year to come. if you do

not discover who the guardian be I ask you to keep this memorial for

I until I reclaim it--which may be never. will you do this for I?"



"I will."



"thank you for all your goodness to I. but I have nothing to put the

memorial in. could you add to your many kindness by give I a

small box which I can lock and secure? dear Dr. Spenlove, it be a

mother who will presently be tear from her child who implore you."



he bethink he of a small iron box he have at home, which contain

some private paper of his own. he could spare this box without

inconvenience to himself, and he promise to bring it to she--and so,

with sincere word of consolation, he leave she.



in the course of an hour he return. Mr. Gordon have consent to the

condition she impose.



"should I be thankful or not?" she ask wistfully.



"you should be thankful," he reply. "your child, rest assure, will

have a comfortable and happy home. here be the box and the key. it be

a patent lock--no other key will unlock it. I will show you how to use

it. yes, that be the way." he pause a moment, his hand in his pocket.

"you will be ready to meet Mr. Gordon at three to-morrow?"



"and my child?" she ask, with tear in her voice. "when will that be

take from I?"



"at twelve." his hand be still fumble in his pocket, and he

suddenly shake his head, as if indignant with himself. "you may want

to purchase one or two little thing in the morning. here be a few

shilling. pray accept they."



he lay on the table the money with which he have intend to pay his

fare to London.



"heaven reward you," say the grateful woman, "and make your life

bright and prosperous!"



her tear bedew his hand as she kiss it humbly, and Dr. Spenlove

walk wearily home once more, penniless, but not unhappy.









CHAPTER VIII.

what be PUT in the IRON BOX.





the mother's vigil with her child on this last night be fraught with

take no heed of her damp clothe. she entertain no doubt that the

she be in no mood to regard with gratitude one who in the most dread

"it be hard, it be hard! how can God permit such cruelty?"



Half an hour pass in this way, and then she become more composed.

she see her future, as she believe, a joyless life, a life of cold

she as this one be. in the state of her feeling it seem to she

she be deserting. in dogged rebellion she hug misery to her

breast, and dwelt upon it as part of the punishment she have bring

upon herself. there be no hope of happiness for she in the future,

there be no ray of light to illumine her path. forever would she be

think of the child for whom she now, for the first time since its

birth, feel a mother's love, and who be henceforth to find a home

among stranger.



in this hopeless fashion do she muse for some time, and then a star

appear in her dark sky. she might, as she have suggest to Dr.

from it. in the event of his death she must adopt some step to prove

that the child be her, and that she, and she alone, have the sole

right to she. no stranger should keep she darling from she, should rob

she of her reward for the suffering she have undergo. it be for

this reason that she have ask Dr. Spenlove for the iron box.



it be a compact, well-make box, and very heavy for its size. any

person receive it as a precious deposit under the condition she

it contain memento of price, valuable jewel, perhaps, which she

she read with a bitterness which display itself strongly in she

"the villain!" she mutter. "if he stand before I I would strike he

dead at my foot!"



there be no linger accent of tenderness in her voice. for the

she be a woman of strong passion, a woman who could love and hate in

no niggardly fashion.



she tear the letter down in two uneven strip, and place one strip in

the box; the other she fold carefully and return to her pocket.

then she lock the box, and tie the key with a piece of string,

hang it round her neck and allow it to fall, hide in her bosom.



"if there be justice in heaven," she mutter, "a day will come!"



the portion of the letter which she have deposit in the box read as

put the iron box on the table she sit by the bedside, her eye

"in a few hour she will be take from I; in a few short hour we

the abnormal fatigue of the day and night, the relaxing of the

smile wreathe the lip of the woe-wear woman as she lie in her poor

garment on the humble bed by the side of her child.



"do you love I, darling?" ask the sleep mother.



"dearly, dearly," answer the dream child. "with my whole heart,

mother."



"call I mother again. it be like the music of the angel."



"Mother--mother!"



"you will love I always, darling?"



"always, mother; forever and ever and ever."



"say that you will never love I less, that you will never forget I."



"I will never love you less. I will never forget you."



"darling child, how beautiful you be! there be not in the world a

lovelier woman. it be for I to protect and guard you. I can do so--I

have have experience. come--let we rest."



they sit upon a mossy bank, and the mother fold her arm around her

child, who lie slumber on her breast.



there have be a few blissful day in this woman's life, during which

she have believe in man's faithfulness and God's goodness, but the

dream hour she be now enjoy be fraught with a heavenly

gladness. nature and dream be the fairy of the poor and the

afflict.



she awake as the church clock chime eight. again have she to face the

stern reality of life. the sad moment of separation be fast

approach.









CHAPTER IX.

MR. MOSS play his part.





at five o'clock on the afternoon of that day Dr. Spenlove return to

his apartment. having give away the money with which he have intend

to pay his fare to London, he have bethink he of a gentleman live

in Southsea of whom he think he could borrow a sovereign or two for

a few week. he have walk the distance, and have meet with

disappointment; the gentleman be absent on business and might be

absent several day.



"upon my word," say the good doctor as he drearily retrace his

step, "it be almost as bad as be shipwreck. worse, because there

be no railway on desert island. what on earth be I to do? get to

London I must, by hook or by crook, and there be absolutely nothing I

can turn into money."



then he bethink himself of Mr. Moss, and in his extremity determine

to make an appeal in that quarter. have it not be for what have

occur last night he would not have dream of go to this

gentleman, of whose goodness of heart he have have no previous

experience, and upon whose kindness he have not the slight claim.

arrive at Mr. Moss' establishment, another disappointment attend

he; Mr. Moss be not at home, and they could not say when he would

return. so Dr. Spenlove, greatly depressed, walk slowly on, his mind

distressed with trouble and perplexity.



he have see nothing more of Mr. Gordon, who have leave he in the early

morning with a simple acknowledgment in word of the service he have

render; nor have he see anything far of Mrs. Turner. on his road

home he call at her lodging, and hear from her fellow-lodger that

she have leave the house.



"we don't know where she's go to, sir," the woman say, "but the

rent have be pay up, and a sovereign be slip under my door. if

it ben't that she be so hard up I should have think it come from

she."



"I have no doubt it do," Dr. Spenlove answer. "she have friend who

be well to do, and I know that one of these friend, discover her

position, be anxious to assist she."



"I be glad to hear it," say the woman, "and it be more than kind of

she to remember I. I always have an idea that she be above we."



as he be enter his room his landlady run up from the kitchen.



"oh, doctor, there's a parcel and two letter for you in your room,

and Mr. Moss have be here to see you. he say he would come again."



"very well, Mrs. Radcliffe," say Dr. Spenlove, and cheer by the

child have be take away, and that she be on the point of leave

Portsmouth.



"I be not permit," the letter run, "to inform you where I be go,

and I be under the obligation of not write to you personally after I

gentleman for whom you act, and I do not consider myself bind to

perhaps know I well will understand what I be suffer as I pen

dear Dr. Spenlove, I rely upon you to carry out my wish with respect

to it. if you be acquaint with the guardian of my child convey it

promise. for all the kindness you have show I receive my grateful

and humble thank. that you will be happy and successful, and that God

will prosper you in all your undertaking, will be my constant prayer.

Farewell."



lay this letter aside he open the second, which be in a

handwriting strange to he:





"Dear Sir: all my arrangement be make, and the business upon which

we speak together be satisfactorily conclude. you will find inclose

a practical expression of my thank. I do not give you my address for

two reason. first, I desire no acknowledgment of the inclosure;

second, I desire that there shall be no correspondence between we upon

any subject. feel perfectly satisfied that the confidence I repose

in you will be respect, I be,



"your obedient servant,



   "G. Gordon."





the inclosure consist of five Bank of England note for twenty

likelihood of its prove the turning point in his career; and

profession he have make.



"Mr. Moss, sir," say Mrs. Radcliffe, open the door, and that

as usual he be hum an operatic air, but he cease as he close

Dr. Spenlove, that be a strange adventure last night."



"it be. a strange and sad adventure. you behave very kindly, and I

"I don't quite follow you."



"a run of rainy weather, a run of fine weather, a run of good fortune,

a run of ill fortune."



"I understand."



"you meet a person to-day whom you have never see or hear of before.

the odd be that you will meet that person to-morrow, and probably

"very good, doctor, very good."



"you walk along drive bargain, and make money honestly----"



"thank you, doctor," interpose Mr. Moss rather gravely. "there be

people who don't do we so much justice."



"when unexpectedly," continue Dr. Spenlove with tender gayety, "you

"Sir," say Mr. Moss, bend his head with a dignity which do not

"I would rather you answer the question yourself," say Dr.

lady friend and her child last night. I open my eye, I can tell

"pardon I a moment," say Dr. Spenlove. "be Mr. Gordon the

"you have name he," say Mr. Moss, and perceive that Dr. Spenlove

much oblige to those who give it; perhaps he think I be a

"you be entitle to ask the question. I be acquaint with he."



"since when, doctor?"



"since last night only."



"before we meet?"



"yes, before we meet."



"may I inquire if you be then act for Mr. Gordon?"



"to some extent. have it not be for he I should not have go in

search of she."



"in which case," say Mr. Moss in a grave tone, "she and her child

"I thank you."



"hear your name, I think at once of the lady and her child whom

we be instrumental in save. be I right in my impression that you

"I be."



"then I need not go into they. I take it, Dr. Spenlove, that you do

not consider the business disreputable."



"it be not disreputable. Mr. Gordon be a peculiar man, and his story

in connection with the lady in question be a singular one. he be not

the father of the child, and the action he have take be not prompt

by a desire to rid himself of a responsibility. on the contrary, out

of regard for the lady he have take upon himself a very heavy

responsibility, which I have little doubt--none, indeed--that he will

honorably discharge."



"I will continue. having hear what Mr. Gordon have to say--think

all the time of the friend who might be induce to adopt the child,

and that I might be able to serve he--I put the gentleman to the

test. admit that his term be liberal, I say that a sum of

money ought to be pay down at once, in proof of his good faith. 'how

much?' he ask. 'fifty pound,' I answer. he instantly produce the

sum in banknote. then it occur to I that it would make thing

still safe if I have an assurance from the eminent firm of London
