lawyer that the business be honorable, and meet with their approval;

and if I also have a notification from they that they be prepared to

pay the money regularly. 'send they a telegram,' suggest Mr. Gordon,

'and make it full and complete. I will write a short one, which you

can send at the same time. let the answer be address here, and open

they both yourself when they arrive, which should be before twelve

o'clock.' the telegram write, I take they to the office; and before

twelve come the reply, which be perfectly satisfactory. everything

appear to be so straightforward that I undertake the business. a

singular feature in it be that Mr. Gordon do not wish to know with

whom the child be place. 'my lawyer will make inquiry,' he say,

'and they will be content if the people be respectable.' Dr.

Spenlove, I think it right that you should be inform of what I

have do; you have express your approval, and I be satisfied. don't

you run away with the idea that I have act philanthropically.

nothing of the kind, sir; I have be pay for my trouble. and now, if

you would like to ask any question, fire away."



"be no condition of secrecy impose upon you?"



"yes, but I say I be bind to confide in one person. he may have

think I mean Mrs. Moss, but it be you I have in my mind. I promise

that it should go no far, and I do not intend that it shall. Mrs.

Moss will be none the bad for not be let into the secret."



"where be the child now?"



"in the temporary care of a respectable woman who be provide

suitable clothing for it, Mr. Gordon having give I money for the

purpose."



"he have not spare his purse. when do you propose take the child to

her new home?"



"to-night."



"they be good people?"



"the good in the world. she cannot help be happy with they."



"do they live in Portsmouth?"



"no, in Gosport. I think this be as much as I have the right to

disclose."



"I agree with you. Mr. Moss, you can render I an obligation, and you

can do a kindness to the poor child's mother. she have implore I to

endeavor to place this small iron box in the care of the guardian of

her child, to be retain by they for twenty-one year, or until the

mother claim it, which she will be free to do in the event of her

husband die during her lifetime. I do not know what it contain, and

I understand it be to be give up to no other person than the child or

her mother. will you do this for I or for she?"



"for both of you, doctor," reply Mr. Moss, lift the box from the

table. "it shall be give into their care, as the mother desire. and

now I must be off. I have a busy night before I. do you go to London

to-morrow?"



"a train leave in a couple of hour; I shall travel by that."



"well, good-night, and good luck to you. if you want to write to I

you know my address."



they part with cordiality, and each take his separate way, Dr.

Spenlove to the City of Unrest, and Mr. Moss to the peaceful town of

Gosport.









CHAPTER X.

the VISION in the CHURCHYARD.





some twelve month before the occurrence of the event record in the

precede chapter a Jew, bear the name of Aaron Cohen, have come to

reside in the ancient town of Gosport. he be accompany by his wife

Rachel. they have no family, and their home be a home of love.



they be comparatively young, Aaron be twenty-eight and Rachel

twenty-three, and they have be marry five year. Hitherto they have

live in London, and the cause of their take up their residence in

Gosport be that Aaron have conceive the idea that he could establish

himself there in a good way of business. one child have bless their

union, whom they call Benjamin. there be great rejoicing at his

birth, and it would have be difficult to calculate how many

macaroon and almond and butter cake, and cup of chocolate, and

glass of aniseed be sacrifice upon the altar of hospitality in

the happy father's house for several day after the birth of his

firstborn. "Aaron Cohen do it in style," say the neighbor, and as

both he and Rachel be hold in genuine respect by all who know they,

the encomium be not mere empty praise. seldom even in the locality in

which the Cohens then reside--the east end of London, where charity

and hospitality be proverbial--have such feasting be see at the

celebration of a circumcision. "if he live in bayswater," say the

company, "he couldn't have treat we well." and when the father

lift up his voice and say, "blessed art thou, the Eternal, our God,

king of the universe, who hath sanctify we with his commandment,

and command we to introduce our son into the covenant of our father

Abraham," there be more than usual sincerity in the response, "even

as this child have now enter this covenant, so may he be initiate

into the covenant of the law, of marriage, and of good work." perhaps

among those assemble there be some who could not have translate

into English the hebrew prayer they read so glibly, but this reproach

do not apply to Aaron, who be an erudite as well as an orthodox Jew,

and understand every word he utter. on this memorable day the

feasting commence in the morning, and continue during the whole day.

"I wish you joy, Cohen, I wish you joy"--this be the formula, a

hundred and a hundred time repeat to the proud father, who really

believe that a prince have be bear among Israel; while the

pale-face mother, press her infant tenderly to her breast, and who

in her maidenhood have never look so beautiful as now, receive in

her bedroom the congratulation of her intimate female friend. the

poor people in the neighborhood be welcome, and if the seed of

good wish could have blossom into flower a rose-strew path of

life lie before the child.



"he shall be the son of my right hand," say Aaron Cohen; and Rachel,

as she kiss her child's mouth and taste its sweet breath, believe

that Heaven have descend upon earth, and that no mother have ever be

bless as she be bless. this precious treasure be the crowning of

their love, and they lay scheme for baby's youth and manhood before

the child be out of long clothe--scheme destine not to be

realize.



for sixteen month Benjamin fill the heart of his parent with

ineffable joy, and then the Angel of Death enter their house and

bear the young soul away. how they mourn for the dear one who be

nevermore on earth to rejoice they with his beautiful way need not

here be relate; all parent who have lose their firstborn will

realize the bitterness of their grief.



but not for long be this grief bitter. in the wise and reverent

interpretation of Aaron Cohen their loss become a source of

consolation to they. "let we not rebel," he say to his wife, "against

the inevitable and divine will. give praise unto the Lord, who have

ordain that we shall have a child in heaven wait to receive we."

fraught with tenderness and wisdom be his word, and his counsel

instill comfort into Rachel's heart. Benjamin be wait for they,

and would meet they at the gate. Beautiful be the thought, radiant

the hope it raise, never, never to fade, nay, to grow bright even

to her die hour. their little child, dead and in his grave, bring

they near to God. Heaven and earth be link by the spirit of

their beloved, who have go before they; thus be sorrow sweeten,

and happiness chasten by faith.



sit on their low stool during the day of mourning, they speak,

when they be alone, of the peace and joy of the eternal life, and

thereby be draw spiritually close to each other. the lesson they

learn in the darken room be more precious than jewel and gold;

it be a lesson which come to all, high and low alike, and rich indeed

be they who learn it aright. for some time thereafter, when the

mother open the drawer in which her most precious possession be

keep, and kiss the little shoe her child have wear, she would murmur

amid her tear:



"my darling be wait for I--my darling be wait for I!"



God send to all sorrow mother a comfort so sweet!



Aaron Cohen have select a curious spot in Gosport for his habitation.

the window of the house he have take overlook the quaint, peaceful

churchyard of the market town. so small and pretty be this resting

place for the dead that one might almost have imagine it to be a

burial ground for child's break toy. the headless wooden

soldier, the battered doll, the maim content of cheap Noah's

ark, the thousand and one treasure of childhood, might have be

inter there, glad to be at rest after the ruthless mutilation they

have undergo. for really, in the dawn white light of a frosty

morning, when every object for mile around sharply outline itself in

the clear air and seem to have lose its rotund proportion, it be

hard to realize that, in this tiny churchyard, man and woman whose

breast once throb with the passion and sorrow of life should be

crumble to that dust to which we must all return. no, no; it could

be nothing but the last home of plain and paint shepherd, and

bald-headed pet, and lamb devoid of fleece, and mayhap--a high

flight which we all hope to take when the time come for we to claim

our birthright of the grave--of a dead bullfinch or canary, carry

thither on its back, with its leg stick heavenward, and bury

with grow-up solemnity, and very often with all the genuineness of

grief for a mortal bereavement. have you not attend such a funeral,

and have not your overcharged heart cause you to sob in your dream as

you lay in your cot close to mamma's bed?



but these fantastic fancy will not serve. it be a real human

churchyard, and Rachel Cohen know it to be so as she stand look out

upon it from the window of her bedroom on the first floor. it be from

no feeling of unhappiness that her sight became dim as she gaze

upon the tombstone. shadow of child rise before she, the

pattering of whose little foot be once the sweetest music that ever

fall on parent's ear, the touch of whose little hand carry with it

an influence as powerful as a heart-stir prayer; child with

golden curl, child with laugh eye, child with wistful

face--but there be one, ah! there be one that shine as a star amid

the shadow, and that rise up, up, till it be lose in the solemn

cloud, send therefrom a divine message down to the mother's heart:

"Mamma, mamma, I be wait for thee!"



Quiet as be everything around she, Rachel hear the word; in the

midst of the darkness a heavenly light be shine on she.



she wipe the tear from her eye, and steal down to the room in which

her husband be sit.









CHAPTER XI.

MR. WHIMPOLE introduce himself.





it be the front room of the house on the ground floor which Aaron

Cohen have convert into a shop. the small parlor window have be

replace by large one, a counter have be put up, behind which be

shelf fit into the wall. these shelf at present be bare, but

Aaron hope to see they fill. under the counter be other shelf,

as empty as those on the wall.



when Rachel enter her husband be engage count out his money,

like the king in his counting house. there be a studious expression

on his face, which be instantly replace by one of deep tenderness as

he look up and see trace of tear in her eye. he gather his

money together, banknote, silver, gold, and copper, and motion she

into the room at the rear of the shop. this be their living room, but

a large iron safe in a corner denote that it be not to be devote

entirely to domestic affair. in another corner be the symbol of his

business, which be to be affix to the front of the premise, over

the shop door--the familiar device of three golden ball.



let his money fall upon the table, he draw his wife to his side,

and pass his arm round she.



"the house," he say, "be almost in order."



"yes, Aaron; there be very little to do."



"I be also ready for business. I have the license, and to-morrow those

glitter ball will be put up and the name paint. they be rather

large for so small a shop, but they will attract all the more

attention." he gaze at her anxiously. "do you think you will be

contented and happy here?"



"contented and happy anywhere with you," she reply in a tone of the

deep affection.



"in this town especially, Rachel?"



"yes, in this town especially. it be so peaceful."



"but," he say, touch her eye with his finger, "these?"



"not because I be unhappy," she say, and her voice be low and sweet.

"I be look out upon the churchyard from our bedroom window."



"ah!" he say, and he kiss her eye.



he divine the cause of her tear, and there be much tenderness in

his utterance of the monosyllable and in the kiss he give she, man

and wife for five year, they be still the fond of lover.



"my dear," say Aaron presently, "the spirit of prophecy be upon I.

we shall lead a comfortable life in this town; we shall prosper in

this house. it be a piece of real good fortune my hit upon it.

when I hear by chance that the man who live here own the lease and

wish to dispose of it I hesitate before part with so large a sum

as a hundred pound for the purchase. it be nearly half my capital,

but I like the look of the place, and a little bird whisper that we

should be lucky in it, so I make the venture. I be certain we shall

not regret it. there be a knock at the street door."



"who can it be?" ask Rachel anxiously. "we know no one in Gosport,

and it be night."



"which be no excuse for our not open the door," say Aaron Cohen,

sweep the money off the table into a small chamois leather bag,

which he tie carefully at the neck, and put into his pocket. "true we

believe we be not know here, but there may nevertheless be an old

acquaintance in Gosport who have hear of our arrival, and come to

welcome we; or Judah Belasco may have tell a friend of his we be

here; or it may be an enterprise baker or grocer who wish to

secure our custom. no," he add as the knock be repeat, "that be

not a tradesman. let we see who it be that express himself so

impatiently."



Aaron go to the street door, and Rachel follow he into the

passage, carry a candle. the night be dark, and Rachel stand a

little in the rear, so that Aaron could not distinguish the feature

of his visitor. he be a big man, and that be all that be apparent

to the Cohens.



"Mr. Cohen?" query the visitor.



"yes," say Aaron.



"Mr. Aaron Cohen?"



"that be my name."



"can I speak with you?"



"certainly." and Aaron wait to hear what the stranger have to say.



"I be not accustomed to be keep wait on the doorstep. I should

prefer to speak to you in the house."



Rachel, who be naturally timid, move close to her husband, who take

the candle from her hand, and hold it up in order to see the face of

the stranger.



"step inside," he say.



the stranger follow Aaron and Rachel into the little parlor, and

without take off his hat, look at Aaron, then at Rachel, and then

into every corner of the room; the last object upon which his eye

rest be the device of the three golden ball, and a frown gather

on his feature as he gaze. Aaron note these movement and sign

with attention and amusement.



"do you detect any blemish in they?" he ask.



"I do not understand you," say the stranger.



"in those ball. there be an expression of disapproval on your face

as you gaze on they."



"I disapprove of they altogether," say the stranger.



"I be sorry, but we cannot please everybody. I be not responsible for

the insignia; you will find the origin in the armorial bearing of the

Medici. that be a beautiful hat you have on your head." the stranger

stare at he. "really," continue Aaron blandly, "a beautiful hat; a

fine protection against the hot ray of the sun; a protection, also,

against the wind and rain. but in this room, as you may observe, we

have neither wind nor rain nor sun." the stranger, redden slightly,

remove his hat, and place it on the table. "my wife," then say

Aaron.



the stranger incline his head, with the air of a man acknowledge an

introduction to one of a low station. the manner of this

acknowledgment be not lose upon Aaron.



"my wife," he repeat courteously, "Mrs. Cohen."



"I see," say the stranger, glance again at Rachel with

condescension. "with your permission I will take a seat."



it be distinctly at variance with the hospitable instinct of Aaron

Cohen that he do not respond to this request.



"you have the advantage of we," he say. "I have have the pleasure of

introduce my wife to you. afford I the pleasure of introduce you

to my wife."



somewhat stiffly the stranger hand Aaron a visit card, upon which

be inscribe the name of Mr. Edward Whimpole, and in a corner the

word, "Churchwarden." Mr. Whimpole's movement be slow, and intend

to be dignify, but Aaron exhibit no impatience.



"my dear, Mr. Edward Whimpole, churchwarden."



Rachel bow gracefully, and Aaron, with an easy motion of his hand,

invite Mr. Whimpole to a chair, in which he seat himself. then

Aaron place a chair for his wife, and take one himself, and prepare

to listen to what Mr. Whimpole have to say.



Mr. Whimpole be a large-framed man with a great deal of flesh on his

face; his eye be light, and he have no eyebrow worth speak of.

the good feature in his face be his mouth, and the most insignificant

his nose, which be really not a fair nose for a man of his build. it

be an add injury inflict upon he by nature that it be very thin

at the end, as though it have be plane on both side. but then, as

Aaron have occasion to remark, we don't make our own nose. a distinct

contrast present itself in the two nose which, if the figure of

speech may be allow, now face each other.



Mr. Whimpole have not disclose the nature of his visit, but he have

already make it clear that he be not graciously dispose toward the

Jew; the only effect this have upon Aaron be to render he exceedingly

affable. perhaps he scent a bargain, and be aware that mental

irritation would interfere with the calm exercise of his judgment in a

matter of buy and selling.



"may I inquire," he say, point to the word "churchwarden" on the

card, "whether this be your business or profession?"



"I be a corn-chandler," say Mr. Whimpole.



"Churchwarden, my dear," say Aaron, address his wife in a pleasant

tone, "_and_ corn-chandler."



for the life of he Mr. Whimpole could not have explain to the

satisfaction of those not directly interested why he be angry at the

reception he be meet. that Aaron Cohen be not the kind of man he

have expect to meet would not have be accept as a sufficient

reason.



"I be not mistaken," say Mr. Whimpole, with a flush of resentment,

"in believe you to be a Jew?"



"you be not mistaken," reply Aaron with exceed urbanity. "I be a

Jew. if I be not proud of the fact it would be folly to attempt to

disguise it, for at least one feature in my face would betray I."



"it would," say Mr. Whimpole, deal a blow which have the effect of

cause Aaron to lean back in his chair, and laugh gently to himself

for fully thirty second.



"when you have quite finish," say Mr. Whimpole coldly, "we will

proceed."



"excuse I," say Aaron, draw a deep breath of enjoyment. "I beg

you will not consider I want in politeness, but I have the

instinct of my race, and I never waste the small trifle, not even

a joke."



a little tuft of hair which run down the center of Mr. Whimpole's

head--the right and leave bank of which be devoid of

verdure--quiver in sympathy with the proprietor's astonishment. that

a man should make a joke out of that which be generally consider to

be a reproach and a humiliation be, indeed, matter for amazement,

nay, in this instance, for indignation, for in Aaron Cohen's laughter

he, Mr. Whimpole himself, be make to occupy a ridiculous place.



"we be loath," continue Aaron, "to waste even the thin joke. we

be at once both thrifty and liberal."



"we!" exclaim Mr. Whimpole in hot repudiation.



"we Jews, I mean. no person in the world could possibly mistake you

for one of the choose."



"I should hope not. the idea be too absurd."



"make your mind easy, sir; you would not pass muster in a synagogue

without exciting remark. yes, we be both thrifty and liberal, waste

nothing, and in the free spending of our money see that we get good

value for it. that be not a reproach, nor be it a reproach that we

thoroughly enjoy an agreeable thing when we get it for nothing. there

be so many thing in life to vex we that the opportunity of a good

laugh should never be neglect. Proceed, my dear sir, proceed; you

be say that you believe you be not mistake in take I for a

Jew."



"be it your intention," ask Mr. Whimpole, come now straight to the

point, "to reside in Gosport?"



"if I be permit," reply Aaron meekly.



"I hear, Mr. Cohen, that you have purchase the lease of this house."



"it be true, sir. the money have be pay and the lease be mine."



"it have twenty-seven year to run."



"twenty-seven year and three month. who can tell where we shall be,

and how we shall be situate at the end of that time?"



Mr. Whimpole wave the contemplation aside. "you give a hundred pound

for the lease."



"the precise sum; your information be correct."



"I have some intention, Mr. Cohen, of buy it myself."



"indeed. it be a case of the early bird, then."



"if it gratify you to put it that way. I have, therefore, no option

but to purchase the lease of you."



"Mr. Whimpole," say Aaron after a slight pause, "I be agreeable to

sell you the lease."



"I think as much." and Mr. Whimpole dispose himself comfortably in

his chair.



Rachel's eye dilate in surprise. their settlement in Gosport have not

be make in haste, and all arrangement for commence business be

make. she could not understand her husband's willingness to give up

the house.



"I do not expect you to take what you give for it," say Mr. Whimpole;

"I be prepared to give you a profit, and," he add jocosely, "you

will not be backward in accept it."



"not at all backward. you speak like a man of sense."



"how much do you want for your bargain? how much, Mr. Cohen? don't

open your mouth too wide.



"if you will permit I," say Aaron, and he proceed to pencil down a

calculation. "it be not an undesirable house, Mr. Whimpole?"



"no, no; I don't say it be."



"it be compact and convenient?"



"fairly so, fairly so!"



"I will accept," say Aaron, having finish his calculation, "five

hundred pound."



"you cannot be in earnest!" gasp Mr. Whimpole his breath fairly

take away.



"I be quite in earnest. be you aware what it be you would buy of I?"



"of course I be aware; the lease of this house."



"not that alone. you would buy my hope for the next twenty-seven

year; for I declare to you there be not to my knowledge in all

England a spot in which I so desire to pass my day as in this

peaceful town; and there be not in all Gosport a house in which I

believe I shall be so happy as in this. you see, you propose to

purchase of I something more than a parchment lease."



"but the--the thing you mention be of no value to I."



"I do not say they be. I be speak from my point of view, as all

man be bind to do. there be no reason why we should bandy word. I

be not anxious to sell the lease; wait till it be in the market."



"a most unhealthy situation," observe Mr. Whimpole.



"it concern ourselves, and we be content."



"I cannot imagine a more unpleasant, not to say obnoxious, view."



"the view of the churchyard? the spot have already acquire an

inestimable value in my eye. God rest the soul of those who lie in

it! the contemplation of the peaceful ground will serve to remind I

of the vanity of life, and will be a constant warning to I to be fair

and straightforward in my dealing. the warning may be need, for in

the business I intend to carry on there be--I do not deny it--many

dangerous temptation."



"tush, tush!" exclaim Mr. Whimpole petulantly. "straightforward

dealing, indeed! the vanity of life, indeed!"



Aaron Cohen smile.



only once before in his life have Mr. Whimpole feel so thoroughly

uncomfortable as at the present moment, and that be when he be a

little boy, and fall into a bed of nettle, from which he be unable

to extricate himself until he be cover with sting. it be just the

same now; he be smart all over from contact with Aaron Cohen, who

be like a porcupine with sharp-point quill. but he would not

tamely submit to such treatment; he would show Aaron that he could

ste in return--he know well enough where to plant his poison

arrow.



it be due to Mr. Whimpole to state that he be not aware that the

manner in which he be conduct himself during this interview be

not commendable. be a narrow-minded man, he could not take a wide

and generous view of abstract matter, which, by a perversion of

reasoning, he generally regard from a personal standpoint; such man

as he, in their jealous regard for their own feeling, be apt to

overlook the feeling of other, and, indeed, to behave occasionally

as if they do not possess any. this be Mr. Whimpole's predicament,

and having meet a ready-witted man, he be make to suffer for his

misconduct. he send forth his sting in this wise:



"you speak, Mr. Cohen, of be straightforward in your dealing, but

for the matter of that we all know what we may expect from a----"



and having get thus far in his ungenerously prompt speech, he feel

himself unable, in the presence of Rachel, and with her reproachful

eye raise to his face, to conclude the sentence. Aaron Cohen

finish it for he.



"for the matter of that," he say gently, "you all know what you may

expect from a Jew. that be what you be go to say. and with this

thought in your mind you come to trade with I. well, sir, it may be

that we both have something to learn."



"Mr. Cohen," say Mr. Whimpole slightly abash, "I be sorry if I have

say anything to hurt your feeling."



"the offense, sir, be atone for by the expression of your sorrow."



this be take high ground, and Mr. Whimpole's choler be ready to

rise again; but he master it and say in a conciliatory tone:



"I will disguise nothing from you; I be bear in this house."



"the circumstance will make it all the more valuable to we. my

dear"--impressing it upon Rachel with pleasant emphasis--"Mr. Whimpole

be bear in this house. a fortunate oman. good luck will come to we,

as it have come to he. it be a low-rent house, and those who have

be bear in it must have be poor man's child. when they rise in

the world, as Mr. Whimpole have do, it be well than a horseshoe

over the door. in which room be you bear, Mr. Whimpole?"



"in the room on the back of the first floor," reply Mr. Whimpole,

make a wild guess.



"our bedroom. there should be a record on the wall; there should,

indeed, be a record, such as be place outside those house in London

which have be inhabit by famous people. fail that, it be in the

power of every man, assuredly of every rich man, to make for himself a

record that shall be imperishable--far well, my dear sir, than the

mere fixing of a plate on a cold stone wall."



Mr. Whimpole gaze at Aaron Cohen to discover if there be any trace

of mockery in his face, but Aaron be perfectly grave and serious.



"a man's humility," say Mr. Whimpole, raise his eye to the

ceiling, "his sense of humbleness, would prevent he from make this

record for himself. it have to be leave to other to do it when they

have find he out."



"aha, my dear sir!" say Aaron softly, "when they have find he out.

true! true! but how few of we be! how few of we receive our just

reward! how few of we, when we be in our grave, receive or deserve

the tribute, 'here lie a perfect man'! but the record I speak of will

never be lose by a rich man's humility, by his humbleness, for it can

be write unostentatiously in the heart of the poor by the aid of

silver and gold."



"I understand you, Mr. Cohen," say Mr. Whimpole inwardly confound

Aaron's flow of idea, "by mean of charity."



"yes, sir, by mean of charity. there be an old legend that a man's

action in life be mark in the air above he, in the place in

which they be perform. there, in invisible space, be inscribe the

record of his good and bad deed, of his virtue, of his crime; and

when he die his soul visit those place, and view the immortal

writing which be visible to all the angel in heaven, and which cover

he with shame or glory. gosport, doubtless, have many such record of

your charity."



"I do my good," say Mr. Whimpole, very much confused and mystify,

"I hope I do my good. I say I would disguise nothing from you; I

will, therefore, be quite frank, with no intention of wound you. I

be a strictly religious man, Mr. Cohen, and it hurt I that one whose

religious belief be oppose to my own should inhabit the house in

which I be bear. I will give you a hundred and twenty pound for the

lease; that will leave you a profit of twenty pound. come, now!"



"I will not accept less for it, sir, than the sum I name."



"be that your last word?"



"it be my last word."



Mr. Whimpole rise with a face of scarlet, and clap his hat on his

head.



"you be a--a----"



"a Jew. leave it at that. can you call I anything bad?" ask Aaron

with no show of anger.



"no, I cannot. you be a Jew."



"I regret," say Aaron calmly, "that I cannot retort by call you a

Christian. may our next meeting be more agreeable! good-evening, Mr.

Whimpole."



"you do not know the gentleman you have insult," say Mr. Whimpole

as he walk toward the door. "you do not know my position in this

town. I be in the expectation of be make a justice of the peace.

you will live to repent this."



"I think not," say Aaron, take the candle to show his visitor out.

"I trust you may."



"you may find your residence in Gosport, where I be universally

respect, not as agreeable as you would wish it to be."



"we shall see, we shall see," say Aaron, still smile. "I may also

make myself respect here."



"there be a prejudice against your race----"



"be I not aware of it? be not every Jew aware of it? be it not throw

in our tooth by the bigoted and narrow-minded upon every possible

occasion? we will live it down, sir. we have already do much; we

will yet do more. your use of the word prejudice be appropriate, for,

as I understand its meaning, it represent a judgment form without

proper knowledge. yes, sir, it be not to be dispute that there exist

a prejudice against our race."



"which, without put any false meaning upon it, will make this

ancient and respectable town"--here Mr. Whimpole find himself at a

loss, and he be compel to wind up with the vulgar figure of

speech--"too hot to hold you."



"this ancient town," say Aaron with a deep seriousness in his

voice, "be know to modern man as Gosport."



"a clever discovery," sneer Mr. Whimpole. "be you go to put

another of your false construction on it?"



"no, sir. I be about to tell you a plain and beautiful truth. when in

olden time a name be give to this place it be not Gosport. it be

God's Port; and what God's port be there throughout the civilized

world in which Jew and Christian alike have not an equal right to

live, despite prejudice, despite bigotry, and despite the unreasonable

anger of large corn-chandler and respected churchwarden? I wish you,

sir, good-night."



and have by this time reach the street door, Aaron Cohen open it

for Mr. Whimpole, and bow he politely out.









CHAPTER XII.

the course of the season.





upon Aaron's return to the little parlor he see that Rachel be

greatly disturb.



"my life!" he say, and he fold she in his arm and tenderly

embrace she. "don't let such a little thing as this distress you; it

will all come right in the end."



"but how you keep your temper," she say, "that be what surprise I."



"it give I the advantage of he, Rachel. I be really amuse." he

pinch her cheek to bring the color back to they. "some man must be

manage one way, some another. and now for our game of bezique. Mr.

Whimpole's visit"--he laugh at the recollection--"will make I enjoy

it all the more."



there be no resist his light-heartedness, and he win a smile from

she, despite her anxiety. Rachel be not clever enough to discover

that it be only by the cunning of her husband that she win the rubber

of bezique. he be a keen judge of human nature, and he know that this

small victory would help to soothe she.



the next day be Friday, and the three golden ball be put up, and

the name of Aaron Cohen paint over the shop door. a great many

people come to look, and depart to circulate the news. at one

o'clock the painting be do, and then Aaron say to his wife, "I

shall be out till the evening. have you find anyone to attend to the

light and the fire?" they be not rich enough to keep a regular

servant, and Aaron never touch fire on the Sabbath.



"I have hear of a woman," say Rachel; "she be come this afternoon

to see I."



"good," say Aaron, and, kissing Rachel, go away with a light heart.



in the afternoon the woman, Mrs. Hawkins, call, and Rachel explain

the nature of the service she require. Mrs. Hawkins be to come to

the house every Friday night to put coal on the fire and extinguish

the light, and four time on Saturday to perform the same duty.

Rachel propose eightpence a week, but Mrs. Hawkins stick out for

tenpence, and this be accede to, she depart--leave a strong

flavor of gin behind she. when Aaron come home the two Sabbath candle

be alight upon the snow-white tablecloth, and on the tablecloth a

supper be spread--fry fish, white bread and white butter, and in

the fender a steaming coffeepot. he wash and say his prayer, and

then they sit down to their meal in a state of perfect contentment.

Aaron, having besought the customary blessing on the bread they be

about to eat, praise the fish, praise the butter, praise the

coffee, praise his wife, and after a full meal praise the Lord in a

Song of Degrees for blessing receive: "when the Eternal restore the

captivity of Zion we be as those who dream. our mouth be then

fill with laughter, and our tongue with song." he have a rich

baritone voice, and Rachel listen in pious delight to his intoning

of the prayer.



the supper thing be clear away, the white tablecloth be

allow to remain because of the light candle on it, which it would

have be break the Sabbath to lift, and then there come a knock at

the street door.



"that be the woman I engage," say Rachel, hurry into the passage.

there enter, not Mrs. Hawkins, but a very small girl, carry a

very large baby. the baby might have be eighteen month old and the

girl ten year, and of the twain the baby be the plumper.



without "with your leave," or "by your leave," the small girl push

past Rachel before the astonished woman could stop she, and present

herself before the no less astonished Aaron Cohen. her comprehensive

glance take in the lighted candle, the cheerful fire, and the master

of the house in one comprehensive flash. with some person what be

know as make up one's mind be a slow and complicated process; with

the small girl it be electrical. she deposit the large baby in

Aaron's lap, admonish the infant to "keep quiet, or she'd ketch

it," blow out the candle in two swift puff, and kneeling before the

grate, proceed to rake out the fire. so rapid be her movement

that the fender be half fill with cinder and blaze coal before

Rachel have time to reach the room.



"in Heaven's name," cry Aaron, "what be the meaning of this?"



"it's all right, sir," say the small girl; "I've come for aunty."



"put down the poker instantly," exclaim Aaron; "your aunty, whoever

she may be, be not here."



"tell I somethink I don't know," request the small girl. "this be

Mr. Cohen's, the Jew, benot it?"



"it be," reply Aaron, with despairing gesture, for the baby be

dab his face with hand sticky with crumb of sugar stuff.



"well, wot be yer 'ollerin for? I'm only do wot aunty tell I."



"and who be your aunty?"



"Mrs. 'Orkins. pretend not to know 'er--do! she se you'd try to do

'er out of 'er money, and want 'er to take fippence instid of

tenpence."



"do she? you have come here by her order, I suppose?"



"yes, I 'ave--to poke out the fire and blow out the candle--and I've

do it."



"you have," say Aaron ruefully. "and now, little girl, you will do as

I tell you. put down that poker. get up. feel on the mantelshelf for a

box of match. I beg your pardon; you be too short to reach. here be

the box. take out a match. strike it. light the candle. thank you!

last, but not least, relieve I of this baby with the sticky hand."



the small girl snatch the baby from his arm, and stand before he

in an attitude of defiance. for the first time he have a clear view of

she.



"Heaven save we!" he cry, fall back in his chair.



her appearance be a sufficient explanation of his astonishment. to

say that she be ragged and dirty and forlorn, and as utterly unlike a

little girl live in civilized society as any little girl could

possibly be, would be but a poor description of she. her face

suggest that she have be lie with her head in a coal scuttle; she

wear no hat or bonnet; her hair be matted; her frock reach just

below her knee, and might have be pick out of a dust heap; she

have no stocking; on her foot be two odd boot several size too

large for she and quite wear out, one tie to her ankle with a piece

of gray list, the other similarly secure with a piece of knotted

twine. her eye glitter with preternatural sharpness; her cheek

bone stick out; her elbow be point and red; she be all

bone--literally all bone; there be not an ounce of flesh upon

she--not any part of her body that could be pinch with a sense of

satisfaction. but the baby! what a contrast! her head be round and

chubby, and be cover with a mass of light curl; her hand be

full of dimple; her face be puff out with superabundant flesh; the

calf of her leg be a picture. in respect of clothe she be no

well off than Mrs. Hawkins' niece.



"wot be yer stare at?" demand the girl.



"at you, my child," reply Aaron with compassion in his voice.



"let's know when yer do," retort the girl, "and I'll tell yer wot

I charge for it."



"and at baby," add Aaron.



"that'll be hextra. don't say I don't warn yer."



there be conflicting element in the situation: its humor be

undeniable, but it have its pathetic side. Aaron Cohen be sway now

by one emotion, now by another.



"so you be Mrs. Hawkins' niece?" he say, with a twinkle in his eye.



"yes, I be. wot 'ave yer get to say agin it?"



"nothing. be baby also Mrs. Hawkins' niece, or nephew?"



"if you've no objection," say the girl with excessive politeness,

"she's Mrs. Pond's little gal, and I nusse 'er."



"I have no objection. what be your name?"



"wot it may be, my lordship," reply the girl, her politeness

become Arctic, "be one thing--wot it be be another."



"you be a clever little girl," say Aaron, smile and rub his

hands--"a sharp, clever little girl."



"thank yer for nothink," say the girl.



she have reach the North Pole; it be necessary to thaw she.



"upon the mantelshelf," say Aaron, "just behind that beautiful blue

vase, be two penny piece. step on a chair--not that cane one, you'll

go through it; the wooden one--and see if you can find they."



"I see 'em," say the girl, look down upon Aaron in more sense

than one.



"they be your. put they in your pocket."



the girl clutch the penny, jump from the chair--whereat the baby

crow, suppose it to be a game provide for her amusement--and

have no pocket, hold the money tight in her hand. vision of sweet

stuff rise before she. the penny get warm, the ice at the North

Pole begin to melt.



"and now perhaps you will tell I your name."



"Prissy. that's the short un."



"the long one is----"



"Priscilla."



"a grand name. you ought to have a silk gown and satin shoe and a

gold comb." Prissy open her eye very wide. the ice be melt

quickly, and the bud be come on the tree. "and baby's name?"



"Wictoria Rejiner. that's grander, benot it?"



"much grander! Victoria Regina--a little queen!" Prissy give baby a

kiss, with pride and love in her glitter eye. "what make your

face so black, Prissy?"



"coal. aunty deal in 'em--and cabbage and tater and orange and

lemon. and she take in washing."



so genial be Aaron Cohen's voice that spring be come in fast. "you

look, Prissy, as if you have very little to eat."



"I don't 'ave much," say Prissy, with a longing sigh. "I could eat

all day and night if I 'ad the chance."



"my dear," say Aaron to his wife, "there be some coffee leave in the

pot. do you like coffee, Prissy?"



"do I like corfey? don't I like corfey! oh, no--not I! Jest you try

I!"



"I will. give I Victoria Regina. poke the fire. that's right; you be

the quick, sharp little girl in my acquaintance. pour some water

from the kettle into the coffeepot. set it on the fire. Rachel, my

dear, take Prissy and baby into the kitchen and let they wash

themselves, and afterward they shall have some supper."



the bud be break into blossom; it really be a lovely spring.



in a few minute Rachel and the child re-enter the room from the

kitchen, baby with a clean face, and Prissy with a painfully red and

shine skin. follow her husband's instruction, Rachel cut half a

dozen slice of bread, upon which she spread the butter with a liberal

hand. Prissy, hug Victoria Regina, watch the proceeding in

silence. by this time the coffee be bubble in the pot.



"take it off the fire, Prissy," say Aaron Cohen; and in another

minute the little girl, with baby in her lap, be sit at the table

with a cup of hot smoking coffee, well sugar and milk, which she

be so eager to drink that she scald her throat. the bread and

butter be perhaps the sweetest that Prissy have ever eat, and the

coffee be nectar. the baby eat more than Prissy; indeed, she eat so

much and so quickly that she occasionally choke, and have to be

violently shake and pat on the back; but she become tired out at

last, and before Prissy have finish her bounteous meal she be fast

asleep in her nurse's arm.



Aaron Cohen lean back in his chair, and gaze with benevolent eye

upon the picture before he; and as he gaze the sweetest of smile

come to his lip and do not leave they. Rachel, steal to the back

of his chair, put her arm round his neck, and nestle her face to

his.



it be a most beautiful summer, and all the tree be in flower.









CHAPTER XIII.

AARON COHEN PREACHES a SERMON on large nose.





the fire be burn brightly, and the old cat which they have bring

with they to Gosport be stretch at full length upon the hearth rug.

the child be go, and Prissy have receive instruction to come

again at ten o'clock to extinguish the candle. it may be say of

Prissy, in respect of her first visit to the house, that she come in

like a lion and go out like a lamb.



it be a habit on Sabbath eve for Aaron to read to his wife something

from the general literature of the time, or from the newspaper, and

to accompany his reading with shrewd or sympathetic remark, to which

Rachel always listen in delight. occasionally he read from a book of

hebrew prayer, and comment upon they, throw a light upon poem

and allegory which make their meaning clear to Rachel's understanding.

invariably, also, he bless she, as jewish father who have not

wander from the path of orthodoxy bless their child on the

Sabbath. now, as she stand before he, he place his hand upon her

head and say:



"God make thee like Sarah, Rebecca, Rachel, and Leah! may the Eternal

bless and preserve thee! may the Eternal cause his face to shine upon

thee, and be gracious unto thee! may the Eternal lift up his

countenance toward thee, and grant thee peace!"



it be something more than a blessing; it be prayer of heartfelt

love. Rachel raise her face to his, and they tenderly kiss each

other. then he take his seat on one side of the fire, and she on the

other. a prayer book and one of Charles Dickens' story be on the

table, but he do not open they; he have matter for thought, and he be

in the mood for conversation. he be in a light humor, which exhibit

itself in a quiet laugh, which presently deepen in volume.



"I be think of the little girl," he explain to Rachel. "it

be amazing the way she puff out the candle and poke out the

fire--quick as lightning. it be the most comical thing! and her black

face--and Victoria Regina's sticky finger! ha, ha, ha!"



his merriment be contagious, and it draw forth Rachel's; the room be

fill with pleasant sound.



"I see Mr. Whimpole to-day," say Aaron, "and I make he a bow, which

he do not return. my jewish nose offend he. how unfortunate! yes,

my life, no one can dispute that the Jew have a big nose. it proclaim

itself; it be a mark and a sign. he himself often despise it--he

himself often look at it in the glass with aversion, 'why, why have I

be compel to endure this affliction?' he murmur, and he reflect

with envy upon the elegant nose of the Christian. short-sight

mortal, not to understand that he owe everything to his big nose! a

great writer--a learn man who pass the whole of his life in the

study of these matter--proclaim the nose to be the foundation or

abutment of the brain. what follow? that the large the nose of a man

be the well off for it be the man. listen, my dear." he take a book

from a little nest of bookshelf, and turn over the page.

"'whoever,' say this learn writer, 'be acquaint with the Gothic

arch will perfectly understand what I mean by this abutment; for upon

this the whole power of the arch of the forehead rest, and without it

the mouth and cheek would be oppress by miserable ruin.' he lay

down exact law which govern the beautiful (and therefore large) nose.

its length should equal the length of the forehead, the back should be

broad, its outline remarkably definite, the side well define, and

near the eye it must be at least half an inch in breadth. such a nose,

this great authority declare, be of more worth than a kingdom. it

impart solidity and unity to the whole countenance; it be the

mountain--bear in mind, my dear, the mountain--that shelter the fair

vale beneath. how proud, then, should I be of my nose, which in some

respect answer to this description! not in all--no, not in all--I be

not so vain as to believe that my nose be worth more than a kingdom;

but when I be tell that a large nose be a sign of sensibility, and of

good nature and good humor, I cannot help a glow of conceite

satisfaction steal over I. how many great man have you know with

small nose? there be, of course, exception, but I speak of the

general rule. our coreligionist, Benjamin Disraeli--look at his nose;

look at the nose of all our great jewish musician and composer--it

be because they be of a proper size that they have become famous.

some time since in London I have the opportunity of look over a

wonderful Bible--six enormous volume publish by Mr. Thomas Macklin

nearly a century ago--embellish with grand picture by the most

eminent english artist, and there I see the figure of Abraham and

Jacob and Aaron and Moses, and other ancestor of our. there be not a

small nose on one of the face of these great patriarch and prophet;

the great painter who draw they have learn from their study how to

delineate the biblical hero. a big nose be a grand decoration, and I

would soon possess it than a bit of red ribbon in my buttonhole, or

a star on my breast. indeed, my life, I have it--the nose of my

forefather."



Aaron make this declaration in a tone of comic despair. "and have it

I will not part with it, except with life."



there be so much playful humor in the dissertation that Rachel

laugh outright. her laugh be the sweetest in the world, and it fall

like music on Aaron's heart. he smile, and there be a gleam in his

eye, and presently he speak again.



"I be not aware whether you have ever observe the attraction a big

nose have for child. take the most popular drama of all age, Punch

and Judy. where be the artist who would venture to present Punch with

any but an enormous nose? be the child frighten at it? no, they

revel in it. do they sympathize with Judy when she be slay? not at

all; every whack Punch give she be greet with shriek of

laughter--because of his enormous nose. introduce two stranger to a

baby, one with a very small nose, the other with a very big nose. let

they both hold out their arm. instinctively the baby fly to the man

with the large nose. it be Nature's silent voice that instruct the

child. he or she--the sex be not material--instinctively know which

be the well nose of the two, which be the most promising nose, the

most suggestive of kiss and jump in the air and cake and song,

and all that be dear to a child's heart. the test be infallible.

nothing will convince I that you do not marry I because of my big

nose."



"indeed, dear," say Rachel, still laugh, "I hardly think I would

have marry you without it."



"then the fact be establish. I be about to make a confession to you,

Rachel; I be go to tell you the true reason for my choose this

place to reside in, where I be separate by a long distance from the

friend of my youth and manhood, and where you, too, my dear

child"--in his moment of tenderness he occasionally address she

thus--"will, I fear, be for a time without friend to whom you can

unbosom yourself."



"I have you, my dear husband," say Rachel in a tone of deep

affection, draw close to he, and slip she little hand into

his great hand. a fine, large, nervous hand be Aaron Cohen's; a

palmister would have see great possibility in it. Rachel's hand,

despite her domestic work, be the hand of a lady; she take a proper

pride in preserve its delicacy and beauty. "I have you, my dear

husband," she say.



"yes, my life, but you use to kiss at least a dozen female friend a

day."



"I kiss Prissy and the baby to-night."



"when their face be wash, I hope. listen to my confession. pride

and hard-heartedness drive I from the neighborhood in which we be

married. a thousand pound do my dear father--God rest his

soul--bequeath to I. it dwindle and dwindle--my own fault. I could

not say no. one come to I with a melancholy tale which lead to a

little loan; another come and another and another--I do not make you

acquaint with the extent of my transgression. my dear, I encourage

the needy one; I even go out of my way to lend, think myself a

fine fellow, and flap my wing in praise of my stupidity. not half

I lend come back to I. then business begin to fall off, and I see

that I be in the wrong groove; I have grow into bad way, and have I

remain much long in the old neighborhood I should have be leave

without a penny. I think of our future, of the injustice I be

inflict upon you. 'I will go,' say I, 'where I be not know, while

I still have a little to earn a living with, among stranger who, when

they borrow, will give I value in return, and where I shall not have

to say to poor friend, "come to I no more; I be poor than

yourself." I have be foolish and weak; I will be wise and strong.

I will grow rich and hard-hearted.' yes, my dear, that be what I

intend, to grow rich, and my heart will not be oppress by the sight

of suffer it be out of my power to relieve. Rachel, I be not so

clever as I pretend to be; to speak the truth, I be afraid I be rather

give to crowing; and when it be not alone my own welfare, but the

welfare of one so dear to I as you be, that be concern, I tremble,

I begin to doubt whether I have do right. give I your opinion of

the step I have take."



she gaze at he with serious, loving, trustful eye.



"it be a wise step, Aaron; I be sure it be. whatever you do be right,

and I be satisfied."



ten o'clock strike, and a knock at the door announce the faithful

Prissy, come to put the fire out. she enter with the baby in her

arm, sound asleep. she be flush and excited, and she hold her hand

over the right side of her face.



"Victoria ought to be abe," say Rachel, take a peep at baby.



"she cann't go," retort Prissy, "afore 'er mother's ready to take

'er."



"where be her mother?" ask Aaron.



"at the Jolly Sailor Boy, enj'ying of 'erself."



"ah. and where be your aunt?"



"at the Jolly Sailor Boy, too, 'ave a 'arf-quartern. there's be a

reg'lar row there about Mrs. Macrory's flannin peddicut."



"what happen to it?"



"it go wrong. yes, it do. Yer needn't larf. call I a story, do! I

would if I be you!"



"no, no, Prissy," say Aaron in a soothe tone. "how do the flannel

petticoat go wrong?"



"nobody know at fust. aunty do Mrs. Macrory's washing, and a lot

more beside, and the thing git mixed sometimes. aunty cann't 'elp

that--'ow can she? so Mrs. Macrory's thing be take 'ome without the

peddicut. Mrs. Macrory she meet aunty at the Jolly Sailor Boy, and

begin to kick up about it. 'where's my peddicut?' she se. ''ow

should I know?' se aunty. then, wot d'yer think? Mrs. Macrory see

somethink stick out of aunty's dress be'ind, and she pull at it.

'why,' she se, 'you've get it on!' that's wot the row wos about.

Aunty don't know 'ow it come on 'er--she's ready to take 'er oath on

that. ainot it rum?"



"very rum. put out the fire, Prissy. it be time for all good people to

get to bed."



in the performance of this duty Prissy be compel to remove she

hand from her face, and when she rise from the floor it be see that

her right eye be sadly discolor, and that she be in pain.



"O Prissy, poor child!" exclaim Rachel; "you have be hurt!"



"yes, mum," say Prissy. "Mrs. Macrory's gal--she's twice as big as

I; you should see 'er leg! she se, 'you're in that job,' she se,

mean the peddicut; and she let fly and give I a one-er on

account."



Rachel run upstairs, and bring down a bottle of gillard water, with

which she bathe the bruise, and tie one of her clean white

handkerchief over it. Prissy stand quite still, her lip quiver;

it may have be the gillard water that fill the girl's unbandaged

eye with tear.



"that will make you feel easy," say Rachel. "blow out the candle

now, and be here at half-past eight in the morning."



"I'll be sure to be," say Prissy with a shake in her voice.



in the dark Aaron Cohen hear the sound of a kiss.



"good-night, sir," say the girl.



"good-night, Prissy," say Aaron.



the chain of the street door be put up, and the shutter securely

fasten, and then Aaron and Rachel, hand in hand, go up the dark

stair to their room.



"my dear," say Aaron drowsily a few minute after he and his wife

be in bed, "be you asleep?"



"no, Aaron," murmur Rachel, who be on the borderland of dream.



"I've be thinking"--he doze off for a moment or two--"I've be

thinking----"



"yes, my dear?"



"that I wouldn't give Prissy's aunt any flannel petticoat to wash."



almost before the word have pass his lip sleep claim he for its

own.









CHAPTER XIV.

a PROCLAMATION of WAR.





on Monday morning Aaron commence business. in the shop window be a

display of miscellaneous article ticket at low price, and Aaron

take his place behind his counter, ready to dispose of they, ready to

argue and bargain, and to advance money on any other article on which

a temporary loan be require. he do not expect a rush of customer,

be aware that pawnbroke be a tree of very small beginning, a

seed which need time before it put forth flourish branch. the

security be sure, the profit accumulative. he be confident of the

result. human necessity, even human frailty, be on his side; all he

have to do be to be fair in his dealing.



in the course of the day he have a good many caller; some to make

inquiry, some to offer different thing in pledge. of these latter

the majority be child, with whom he decline to negotiate.



"who send you?"



"Mother."



"go home and tell she she must come herself."



he would only do business with grow-up people. set before himself

a straight and honest rule of life, he be not the man to wander from

it for the sake of a little profit.



of the other description of caller a fair proportion enter the shop

out of idle curiosity. he have pleasant word for all, and give change

for sixpence and shilling with as much courtesy as if each

transaction be a gain to he--as, indeed, it be, for no man or woman

who enter with an unfavorable opinion of he (influence by certain

rumor to his discredit which have be circulate by Mr. Whimpole)

depart without have their mind disturb by his urbanity and

genial manner. "I don't see any harm in he," be the general verdict

from personal evidence; "he's as nice a spoken man as I ever set eye

on."



on the evening of this first day he express his satisfaction at the

business he have do.



"our venture will turn out well," he say to Rachel. "the flag of

fortune be wave over we."



it be eight o'clock, and although he scarcely expect further

custom, he keep the gas burn in the shop window.



"light be an attraction," he observe; "it be well than an

advertisement in the paper."



the evening be fine; he and Rachel be sit in the parlor,

with the intermediate door open. Aaron be smoke a handsome

silver-mount pipe and make up his account, while his wife be

busy with her needle. Satan could never have put anything in the shape

of mischief in the way of these two pair of industrious hand, for

they be never idle, except during the Sabbath and the fast and the

holiday, and then it be not idleness, but rest divinely ordain.

the silver-mount pipe be one of Aaron's most precious possession,

it be his beloved wife's gift to he on his last birthday; he would

not have sell it for ten time its weight in gold. at peace with the

world and with themselves, they converse happily as they work; but

malignant influence be at work of which they be soon to feel the

shock.



Aaron have put his account book in the safe, and be turn the key,

when the sound of loud voice outside his shop reach their ear. the

voice be those of child, male and female, who be exercise

their lung in bass, treble, and falsetto. only one word do they

utter:



"Jew! Jew! Jew!"



Rachel start up in alarm, her hand at her heart. her face be white,

her limb be tremble.



"Jew! Jew! Jew!"



Aaron put the key of the safe in his pocket, and lay down his pipe.

his countenance be not troubled, but his brow be pucker.



"Jew! Jew! Jew!"



"it be wicked--it be wicked!" cry Rachel, wring her hand. "oh,

how can they be so cruel!"



Aaron's countenance instantly clear; he have to think, to act, for

she as well as himself. with fond endearment he endeavor to soothe

she, but her agitation be profound, and while these cry of imply

opprobrium continue she could not school herself to calmness. not for

herself do she fear; it be against her dear, her honored husband

that this wicked demonstration be make, and she dread that he would

be subject to violence. to her perturb mind the voice seem to

proceed from man and woman; to Aaron's clear sense they be the

voice of child, and he divine the source of the insult. Rachel

sob upon his breast, and clasp he close to protect he.



"Rachel, my love, my life!" he say in a tone of tender firmness, "be

calm, I entreat you. there be nothing to fear. have you lose

confidence in your husband? would you increase my trouble, and make

the task before I more difficult than it be? on my word as a man, on

my faith as a Jew, I will make friend of these foolish child, in

whose outcry there be no deep-seat venom--I declare it, none. they

do not know what they be do. I will make they respect I; I will

enrich they with a memory which, when they be man and woman, will

make they think of the past with shame. I will make my enemy respect

I. if you will help I by your silence and patience I will turn their

bitterness into thistledown, which I can blow away with a breath. take

heart, my beloved, dear life of my life! trust to I, and in the

course of a few day you shall see a wonder. there--let I kiss your

tear away. that be my own Rachel, whose little finger be more

precious to I than all the world beside. good, good, my own dear

wife! do you think it be a tragedy that be be enact by those

youngster? no, no, it be a comedy. you shall see, you shall see!"



she be comfort by his word; she draw strength from his strength;

she look at he in wonder as he begin to laugh even while he be

caress she, and her wonder increase when she see that his eye

fairly shine with humor.



"have no fear, my heart," he say, "have not the slight fear. I be

go to meet they--not with javelin and spear; with something still

more powerful, and with good temper for my shield."



"Aaron," she whisper, "be you sure there be no danger?"



"if I be not sure," he answer merrily, "I would remain snug in

this room. I be not a man of war; I be a man of peace, and with

peaceful weapon will I scatter the enemy. for your dear sake I would

not expose myself to peril, for do I not know that if I be hurt your

pain would be great than mine? it be my joy to know it. you will

remain quietly here?"



"I will, dear husband; but you will not go into the street?"



"I shall go no far than the street door; I shall not need to go

far."



he stop to fill his pipe and light it, and then, with tender

kiss, and a smile on his lip, he leave she.



when he make his appearance at the shop door there be a sudden hush,

and a sudden scuttle away of the twenty or thirty child who have

congregate to revile he. he remain stationary at the door, smoke

his pipe and gaze benignantly at they.



their fear of chastisement dispel by his peaceful attitude, they

stop, look over their shoulder, and slowly and warily come back,

keep, however, at a safe distance from he. they find their voice

again.



"Jew? Jew! Jew!"



"good child! good child!" say Aaron in a clear, mellifluous

voice. then he put his pipe to his mouth again, and continue to

smoke.



"Jew! Jew! Jew!"



"good little boy and girl," say Aaron. "Bravo! bravo! you deserve a

reward. every laborer be worthy of his hire."



he draw from his pocket three or four penny, which, with smile

nod of his head, he throw among they.



instantly come into play other passion--greed, avarice, the

determination not to be defraud of their due. fall upon the

money, they scramble and fight for it. Aaron throw among they two or

three more penny, and their ardor increase. they scratch, they

kick, they tumble over each other, blow be exchange. those who

have secure penny scamper away with they, and with loud and

vengeful cry the penniless scamper after they. the next moment

they have all disappear.



shake with internal laughter, Aaron remain on his step a while,

purr at his pipe; then he put up the shutter, lock the street

door, and rejoin his wife.



"my dear," he say, and his voice be so gay that her heart beat with

joy, "that be the end of the first act. they will not come back

to-night."









CHAPTER XV.

the BATTLE be FOUGHT and WON.





"the personal affection by which we be govern," say Aaron Cohen,

seat himself comfortably in his chair, "be, like all order of

being to which they come, of various degree and quality, and the

small become merged and lose in the large, as the serpent of

Pharaoh's magician be swallow up by Aaron's rod. wisdom be well

than an inheritance, and anger resteth in the bosom of fool.

moreover, as be observe by Rabbi Chanina, 'wise man promote peace in

the world.' such, my dear Rachel, be my aim, and so long as the mean

within my reach be harmless, so long will I follow the learn

rabbi's precept. if the human heart be not full of envy and deceit

what I have do should bring joy to our persecutor, but I will not

pledge myself that it have do so in this instance. on the contrary,

on the contrary." Aaron pause here to laugh. "the opprobrious cry

cease suddenly, do they not, Rachel?"



"they do, and I be very much surprised."



"you will be more surprised when you hear that I reward with modern

shekel the labor of the young rascal who would make our life a

torment to we."



"you give they money!" exclaim Rachel in amazement.



"I throw among they seven penny piece. why not?"



"but why?"



"ah, why, why? have I throw among they seven cannon ball they would

scarcely have be more effective. the truth of this will be make

manifest to our benefit before many day be go, or Cohen be not my

name. wife of my soul, I go forth, not with a lion's but with a

fox's skin. have I not study the law? be not the Cohanim priest,

and be not priest suppose to be man of intelligence and resource?

we read in Proverbs, 'Counsel be mine and sound wisdom; I have

understanding, I have strength.' Rabbi Meyer say that the study of

the law endow a man with sovereignty, dominion, and ratiocination. he

be slow to anger, ready to forgive an injury, have a good heart,

receive chastisement with resignation, love virtue, correction, and

admonition. this, perhaps, be go a little too far, and be endow

a human being with quality too transcendent, but it be true to a

certain extent, and I have instinctively profit by it. Ill fit

should I be to engage in the battle of life if I be not able to cope

with the young rascal who make the night hideous outside our door,

and who, if I be not mistaken, will repeat their performance to-morrow

evening at the same hour."



"they will come again," cry Rachel, clasp her hand in despair.



"they will come again, and again, and yet again, and then--well, then

we shall see what we shall see."



"you give they money to-night," say Rachel sadly, "and they will

return for more."



"and they will return for more," say Aaron with complacency. "at the

present moment I should judge that they be engage in a fierce

contest. when that look come into your face, my dear, it be an

indication that I have say something you do not exactly understand. I

throw to they seven apple of discord, which the nimble and the

strong seize and flee with. but each soldier conceive he have a

right to at least one of the apple, and those who be leave

empty-handed labor under a sense of wrong. they have be rob by

their comrade. after they they rush to obtain their portion of the

spoil of war. then ensue a grand scrimmage in which nose be

injure and eye discolor. I be not there, but I see the scene

clearly with my mind's eye." he take a sovereign from his pocket and

regard it contemplatively. "ah, root of much evil and of much good,

what have you not to answer for? Rachel, my love, take heart of

courage, and when you hear those boy shout outside to-morrow night

do not be alarm. trust in I; everything will come right in the

end."



the scene which Aaron have draw from his imagination be as near as

possible to the truth. there have be a battle royal between the boy

and girl for possession of the penny; nose be put out of joint,

eye be discolor, word of injurious import exchange, and bad

blood engender. the sevenpence for which they fight would not have

pay for the repair to the clothe which be tear and rent during

the fray. the end of it be that the robber, after be kick and

cuff ignominiously, be not allow to join in a compact make by

the penniless, to the effect that they would assemble outside Aaron

Cohen's shop to-morrow night and repeat the tactic which have be so

well reward, and that all money receive should be equally divide

between the warrior engage. one, Ted Kite, be appoint commander,

to organize the expedition, and to see fair play.



accordingly, on Tuesday night, a score or so of boy and girl

present themselves in front of the shop and commence shouting,

"Jew! Jew! Jew!" the fugleman be Ted Kite, who prove himself well

fit for the task.



"there he be, there he be," say the youngster eagerly as Aaron make

his appearance on the doorstep, and, inspire by their captain, they

continue to fire.



"good child, good child," say Aaron, nod benignantly in

approval, and continue to smoke his silver-mount pipe. "very well

do, very well do indeed!"



"benot he go to throw we anything?" they ask each other anxiously,

their greedy eye watch Aaron's movement.



they be keep rather long in suspense, but at length Aaron's hand

seek his pocket, and half a dozen penny rattle on the stone.

down they pounce, and fight and scratch for they as on the

previous night, the fortunate one scud away as on the first

occasion, follow by their hungry comrade. they be catch, and

compel to disgorge; the penny be change into farthing, and

each soldier receive one for his pay; the two or three that be leave

be spend in sweet stuff.



"what a game!" the child exclaim, and appoint to meet on the

follow night to continue the pastime.



on this third night they be keep wait still long. Aaron Cohen

do not make his appearance so quickly, and several minute elapse

before the penny be throw to they. on the first night he have

disburse seven, on the second night six, on this third only four.

there be the usual fighting for they, and the usual scampering away;

and when the sum total be place in the hand of Ted Kite a great

deal of dissatisfaction be express. only fourpence! they doubt

the correctness of the sum; they be sure that more have be throw;

one girl say she count eight, and other support her statement.

who have steal the miss penny? they quarrel and fight again;

they regard each other with suspicion; doubt be throw upon the

honesty of the captain. Off go his coat instantly; off go the

coat of other boy; the girl, have no coat to throw off, tuck

up their sleeve; and presently six or seven couple be hit,

scratch, and kick each other. much personal damage be do, and

more bad blood engender. the warfare be not by any mean of a

heroic nature.



nevertheless they assemble on the fourth night, and be keep wait

still long before they be pay. Aaron do not show his liberality,

however, until he have have a conference with the captain. his keen eye

have single out Ted Kite, and he beckon to he. Ted hesitate; he

be only a small boy; Aaron Cohen be a big man, and in a personal

contest could have dispose of he comfortably.



"yah, you coward!" cry the rank and file to their captain. "what be

you frighten at? what do we make you captain for?"



thus taunt, Ted Kite venture to approach the smile foe.



"come a little nearer," say Aaron; "I be not go to hurt you. I

wish you to do I a favor."



Ted, with a sidelong look over his shoulder at his army, as if

appeal to it to rush to his rescue in case he be seize, shuffle

forward. Aaron Cohen hold out his hand; Ted Kite timidly respond,

and be surprised at the friendly grip he receive.



"you be the leader," say Aaron in his most genial voice.



"yes, Mr. Cohen," reply Ted, grow bold; "I'm the captain.



"clever lad, clever captain! here's a penny for you. don't let they

see you take it. it be for you alone. they will do as you tell they,

of course."



"I'll let 'em know it if they don't."



"it's right you should. I think it be very kind of you to come here as

you do, but I want you to oblige I and not come to-morrow night. it

be Friday, and the shop will be close, so you would be waste your

time. that would be foolish."



"yes, it would," say Ted, somewhat bewildered. "shall we come on

Saturday night?"



"certainly, if you think proper. then you will not be here to-morrow?"



"we willn't, as you'd rather not, Mr. Cohen."



"thank you; I be really oblige to you. now go and join your army."



Ted Kite turn away, walk a step or two, and return.



"but I say, Mr. Cohen----"



"well, my lad?"



"do you like it?"



"do I like it?" echo Aaron, with a sly chuckle. "should I speak to

you as I be do if I don't? it be as good as a play. I think it be

very nice of you--very nice, very nice indeed!"



"oh," say Ted in a crestfallen tone. as Aaron take pleasure in the

persecution it be not half such good fun as it have be. "he say he

like it," he say to his comrade when he be among they. "he say

it's as good as a play."



"how much do he give you?" they inquire, feel as he do in

respect of the fun of their proceeding.



"he don't give I nothing."



"we see he hold out his hand to you," they protest.



"you see we shake hand, that's what you see. let's get on with the

game; we don't want to be keep wait here all night."



they go on with the game, call "Jew! Jew! Jew!" half-heartedly.

put the pecuniary reward out of the question, it be a game that

be become rather monotonous. they have to call for quite a quarter

of an hour before Aaron pay they; and this time he pay they with two

penny only. the child fall on the ground, and scrape the stone

for more, but find none, and they retire grumble, discontent,

and suspicious of each other's honesty.



on Friday night, the Sabbath eve, Aaron and Rachel have peace; and on

Saturday night the child make their appearance again and give forth

their chorus. Aaron come to the door and stand there smoke his pipe

and smile at they. but he do not throw any money to they. they do

not know what to make of it. their voice grow weak and weak, they

wander about discontentedly, they declare it be not fair on Mr.

Cohen's part. "we'll try he again on Monday night," they say.



they try he again on Monday night, and he stand on his step,

commend they, but he give they no more money. there be no heart

whatever now in their invective. they be not philosopher, and do

not know that the course Aaron have pursue have take the sting out of

their tail. "he like it," they say to each other as they stroll

off moodily, "and he want we to come here and scream our throat dry

without be pay for it. well, we benot go to do it. we willn't call

he Jew any more if he want we ever so much. it benot likely, now, be

it? what do he mean by treat we so shabby?" these young

rapscallion think the world be out of joint.



in this way it be that Aaron Cohen fight the battle and gain a

bloodless victory. he laugh in his sleeve as he think of it, and

laugh aloud in his cozy little parlor when he relate the whole

affair to Rachel.



"one shilling and eightpence have it cost I, my love," he say, "and I

do not begrudge the money. show I the battle that have be win for

less."



Rachel be greatly relieve, but her dominant feeling be admiration

for her husband's wisdom.



"I do not believe any other man in the world would have think of

it," she say; and though Aaron shake his head in modest deprecation,

he be justify in inwardly congratulate himself upon his astute

tactic.



the story get about, and the townspeople be much amuse by it. "Mr.

Cohen's a clever fellow," they say. he grow to be respect by they,

and as the week pass by and it be see that he be not only a

fair-dealing but a kindly-hearted man the innuendos which Mr. Whimpole

continue to circulate about he produce a very small effect. Mr.

Whimpole be not please; where be the man who would have be in his

position? talk one night with Rachel over the animosity the

corn-chandler bore toward the Jews, Aaron say:



"I have no doubt, my dear, that he be quite conscientious, and that he

consider his prejudice to be the outcome of a just conviction.

Doubtless his parent have the same conviction, and he imbibe it from

they. there be thousand of people who agree with he, and there be

bad persecution than that to which we have be subject. look at

that infamously govern country Russia, which in the map ought to be

stamp blood red, with a heavy mourning border around it. the

wretch who inflict incredible suffering upon countless innocent

being call themselves Christians. they be not Christians, they be

fiend, and judgment will fall upon they. Spain, once the great of

nation, fall into decay when the Jew desert it. so will it be with

other nation that oppress the Jew. let Germany look to it. it be easy

to arouse the evil passion of uneducated human being, but a brand of

fire shall fall upon the head of those who be employ in work so

vile."









CHAPTER XVI.

JOY and SORROW.





perhaps, however, to Rachel may chiefly be ascribe the general

respect the Cohens earn among the townsfolk. charitable, kind, and

gentle by nature, she be instinctively draw to those poor people who

have fall into misfortune. upon her sympathetic ear no tale of

distress could fall without bear fruit. she win friend everywhere,

and her sweet face be like a ray of sunshine in the home of the

poor. it be not at all uncommon to hear that her timely assistance

have be the mean of restore to health those who have be stricken

down. she walk through life as an angel of mercy might have do,

and flower grow about her foot.



of all the friend who sound her praise none be more enthusiastic

than little Prissy, who come now regularly to the house to do domestic

work.



anxious to increase his trade, Aaron have stock his shop with such

article of wear and adornment as be most in request. he have not the

mean to pay ready money for the stock, but through a friend in

Portsmouth, Mr. Moss, with whom the reader of this story have already

become acquainted, he obtain credit from wholesale dealer who would

have be chary to trust he without a sufficient recommendation.



apart from the pleasure which his modest success in business afford

he, there be a happiness in store for he to which he look forward

with a sense of profound gratitude. Rachel be about to become a

mother. to this fond couple, who seem to live only for each other,

there could be no great joy than this. they have lose their

firstborn, and God be send another child to bless their day. they

never close their eye at night, they never rise in the morning,

without offer a prayer of thank to the most High for his goodness

to they. they see no cloud gather to darken their happiness.



it be an ordinary event, for which Aaron could hardly have be

prepare.



they have be eleven month in Gosport when one morning Aaron, rise

first, and go down to his shop, find that burglar have be at

work. they have effect an entrance at the back of the house, and have

carry away the most valuable article in the window. the loss, Aaron

calculate, would not be less than a hundred pound.



it be, to he, a serious loss; he have commence with a very small

capital, and his earning during the year have leave only a small margin

over his household and trade expense. his business be grow, it be

true, but for the first six month he have barely pay his way; it be

to the future he look to firmly establish himself, and now in one

night all his profit be sweep away. More than this; if he be

call upon to pay his debt he would have but a few pound leave.

Rachel, whose health the last week or two have be delicate, her

confinement be so near, be in bed by his direction; he have

forbid she to rise till ten o'clock. it be a matter to be thankful

for; he could keep the shock of the loss from she; in her condition

bad news might have a serious effect upon she.



he set everything in order, speak no word of what have occur to his

wife, rearrange the shop window, and take down the shutter. in the

course of the day he tell Rachel that he intend to close a couple of

hour early than usual; he have to go to Portsmouth upon business in

the evening, and should be absent probably till near midnight.



"you will not mind be alone, my love?" he say.



"oh, no," she answer, with a tender smile; "I have plenty to occupy

I."



she have be for some time busy with her needle prepare for her

unborn child.
