"but you must go to bed at ten," say Aaron. "I shall lock the shop,

and take the key of the back door with I, so that I can let myself

in."



she promise to do as he bid she, and in the evening he leave she to

transact his business. he have no fear that she would be intrude upon;

it be not likely that the house would be break into two night in

succession; besides, with the exception of some pledge of small

value which he keep in the safe, there be little now to tempt

thief to repeat their knavish doing. so with fond kiss he bid

her good-night.



they stand face each other, look into each other's eye. Rachel's

eye be of a tender gray, with a light so sweet in they that he

never look into they unmoved. he kiss they now with a strange

yearning at his heart.



"I hope baby's eye will be like your, dear love," he say; "the soul

of sweetness and goodness shine in they."



she smile happily, and press he fondly to she. ah, if he have

know!



his first business be with the police. he go to the station, and

tell the inspector of his loss, say that he wish it to be keep

private, because of his fear that it might reach his wife's ear. the

inspector reply that it would be advisable under any circumstance.

leave in the officer's hand a list of the article that have be

steal, he proceed to Portsmouth to consult his friend Mr. Moss.

that goodhearte gentleman be deeply concerned at the news.



"it be a serious thing, Cohen," he say.



"a very serious thing," reply Aaron gravely, "but I shall overcome

it. only I require time. I promise to pay some bill to-morrow, and I

shall require a little stock to replace what I have lose; it would

cramp I to do so now."



he mention the name of the tradesman to whom he have give the

promise, and ask Mr. Moss to call upon they in the morning and

explain the matter to they.



"they will not lose their money," he say; "it will not take I very

long to make everything right."



"I will see they," say Mr. Moss, "and I be sure they will give you

time. Aaron Cohen's name be a sufficient guarantee."



"I hope it will always be," reply Aaron. "it be very unfortunate

just now, because I have extra expense come on. the nurse, the

doctor----"



"I know, I know. how be Mrs. Cohen?"



"fairly well, I be glad to say. she know nothing of what have

occur."



"of course not, of course not. how could you tell she while she be

like that? when Mrs. Moss be in the same way I be always singe and

laugh and say cheerful thing to she. between you and I we

expect an addition ourselves in about four month."



"indeed. that will make----"



"Fourteen," say Mr. Moss, rub his hand briskly together.

"increase and multiply. it's our bounden duty, eh, Cohen?"



"yes," say Aaron rather absently. "and now I must go; it will be late

before I reach home, and for all Rachel's promise I expect she will

keep awake for I. good-night, and thank you."



"nothing to thank I for. good-night, and good luck."



when Aaron return to Gosport it be midnight. winter be come on

and it be cold and dark; button his coat close up to his neck, he

hasten his step.



he be not despondent; misfortune have fall upon he, but he have

confidence in himself, and despite the practical common sense which

show itself in all his action there be in his nature an underlie

current of spiritual belief in divine assistance toward the successful

accomplishment of just and worthy endeavor. that it be man's duty to

do right, to work, to pray, to be considerate to his neighbor, to

make his home cheerful, to be as charitable as his mean will

allow--this be his creed; and it be strengthen by his conviction

that God make himself manifest even upon earth in matter of right and

wrong. he do not relegate the expiation of transgression to the

future; he do not believe that a man could wipe out the sin of the

past year by fast and pray and beat his breast on the Day of

Atonement. Wrongdoing be not to be set aside and forget until a

convenient hour for repentance arrive. Hourly, daily, a man must keep

watch over himself and his action. this have be his rule of life,

and it contribute to his happiness and to the happiness of those

around he.



he be within a quarter of a mile of his residence when he be

conscious of an unseen disturbance in the air. a distant glare in the

sky, the faint echo of loud voice, steal upon his sense. agitate

as he have be by what have transpire during this long unfortunate

day, he could not at first be certain whether these sign be real or

spiritual, but presently he discover that they do not spring from

his imagination. the glare in the sky become plainly visible, the loud

voice reach his ear. there be a fire in the town, and he be

proceed toward it. instantly his thought, his fear, center upon

Rachel. he run forward quickly, and find himself struggle through

an excited crowd. flame shoot upward; the air be fill with float

spark of fire. great God! it be his own house that be be

destroy by the devouring element. he do not heed that; the

destruction of his worldly good do not affect he. "my wife!" he

scream. "where be my wife?" by main force they hold he back, for he

be rush into the flame.



"let I go!" he scream. "where be my wife?"



"it be all right, Mr. Cohen," a number of voice reply. "she be

save."



"thank God, oh, thank God!" he cry. "take I to she. where be she?"



he care not for the ruin that have overtake he; like cool water to a

parch throat come the joyful news that she be save.



"take I to she. in the name of Heaven, tell I where she be!"



she be in a house at a safe distance from the fire, and thither he

be lead. Rachel be lie on a couch in her night dress; sympathize

people be about she.



"Rachel, Rachel!" he cry, and fall upon his knee by her side.



she do not answer he; she be insensible.



"do not agitate yourself, Mr. Cohen," say a voice; it be that of the

physician who have be attend to she. "be thankful that she live."



"o Lord, I thank thee," murmur the stricken man. "my Rachel live!"



what matter all the rest? what matter worldly ruin and

destruction? the beloved of his heart be spare to he.



"you be a sensible man, Mr. Cohen," say the physician, "and you must

be calm for her sake. in her condition there will be danger if she

witness your agitation when she recover."



"I will be calm, sir," say Aaron humbly. "she be all I have in the

world."



he make no inquiry as to the cause of the fire; he do not stir from

Rachel's side, but sit with his eye fix upon her pallid face. the

physician remain with they an hour, and then take his departure,

say he would return early in the morning, and leave instruction

to Aaron what to do.



at sunrise Rachel awoke. pass one hand over her eye, she hold out

the other in a groping, uncertain way. Aaron take it in his, and hold

it fondly; the pallor leave her cheek.



"it be you, my dear?" she murmur.



"yes, it be I, my life!" he say in a low and gentle tone.



"you be well--you be safe?"



"I be well--I be safe," he reply. "and you, Rachel--how do you

feel?"



"I have a slight headache. it will soon pass away. oh, my dear

husband, how thankful I be! when do you return?"



"not till you be take from the house. do not talk now. rest, rest,

my beloved!"



the endearing word bring a glad smile to her lip.



"I will sleep presently, Aaron. be the doctor here?"



"no, but he will come soon. shall I go for he?"



"I can wait, dear; when he come I should like to speak to he alone."



"you be hurt!" he say, alarmed. "tell I!"



"I be not hurt, dear; it be only that my head ache a little. he will

give I something to relieve I. have no fear for I, Aaron; I be in

no danger; indeed, indeed, I be not!"



"God be praise!"



she draw his head to her breast, and they lay in silence a while,

fondly embrace.



"let I tell you, dear, and then I will go to sleep again. I go to

bed at ten, as you bade I, and though I have it in my mind to keep

awake for you, I could not do so. I do not know how long I sleep, but

I awake in confusion, and there be a strong glare in my eye. I

hardly remember what follow. I hear voice call to I--Prissy's

voice be the loud, I think--and then I feel that strong arm be

around I, and I be be carry from the house. that be all, my

dear, till I hear your voice, here. where be I?"



he inform she, and then, hold he close to she, she fall asleep

again. as the clock strike nine the physician enter the room, and

Aaron tell he what have pass.



"I can spare half an hour," say the physician. "go and see after your

affair. I will not leave she till you return."



Kissing Rachel tenderly, and smooth the hair from her forehead,

Aaron leave the house, and go to his own. before he depart he

learn from the kind neighbor who have give Rachel shelter that they

be not in a position to keep she and Aaron with they, and he say

that he would make arrangement to remove she in the course of the

day, if the doctor think it would be safe to do so. his own house,

he find, be completely destroy, but he hear of another at no

great distance which be to be let furnish for a few week, and this

he take at once, and instal Prissy therein, to light fire and get

the room warm. the arrangement complete, he hasten back to Rachel,

between whom and the physician a long consultation have take place

during his absence. at the conclusion of their conversation she have

ask he one question:



"shall I be so all my life, doctor?"



"I fear so," be the reply.



"my poor husband!" she murmur. "my poor, dear husband! say nothing

to he, doctor, I implore you. let he hear the truth from my lip."



he consent, not sorry to be spare a painful duty. "she be

surprisingly well," he say to Aaron, "and in a few day will be able

to get about a little, though you must not expect she to be quite

strong till her child be bear."



the news be so much well than Aaron expect that he draw a deep

breath of exquisite relief.



"can she be remove to-day with safety?" he ask.



"I think so. she will be happy with you alone. give I your new

address; I will call and see she there this evening."



at noon she be take in a cab to her new abode, and Aaron carry she

in and lay she on the sofa before a bright fire. in the evening the

physician call accord to his promise. "she be progress

famously," he say to Aaron. "get she to bed early, and it may be

advisable that she should keep there a few day. but I shall speak

more definitely about this later on. Mr. Cohen, you have my good

wish. you be bless with a noble wife." tear shine in Aaron's

eye. "let I impress upon you," continue the doctor, "to be strong

as she be strong; but at present, with the birth of her child so near,

it be scarcely physical power that sustain she. she be support by a

spiritual strength draw from her love for you and her unborn babe."



with these word the physician leave they together. Prissy be go,

and Aaron and Rachel be alone.



they exchange but few word. Rachel still occupy the couch before

the fire, and as she seem to be doze Aaron would not disturb she.

thus an hour pass by, and then Rachel say:



"the doctor advise I to go to bed early. will you help I up, dear?"



she stand on her foot before he, and as his eye rest on her face a

strange fear enter his heart.



"come, my life," he say.



"a moment, dear husband," she say. "I have something to tell you,

something that will grieve you. I do not know how it happen, nor

do the good doctor know. he have hear of only one such case before.

I be not in pain; I do not suffer. it be much to be grateful for, and

I be humbly, humbly grateful. it might have be so much bad."



"Rachel, my beloved," say Aaron, place his hand on her shoulder.



"keep your arm about I, my honored husband. let I feel your dear

hand, your dear face. kiss I, Aaron. may I tell you now?"



"tell I now, my beloved."



"look into my eye, dear. I cannot look into your. dear husband, I be

blind!"









CHAPTER XVII.

DIVINE CONSOLATION.





the shock of this revelation be so overwhelming that for a few

moment Aaron be unable to speak. in the word of the prophet, "his

tongue clave to the roof of his mouth." his soul be plunge in

darkness, and a feeling of passionate rebellion rack his heart. that

upon his sweet and innocent wife should have fall an infliction so

awful seem to blot all brightness out of the world. Nay, more--it

seem to blot out the principle of justice, to render it a mockery

and a snare. the sentiment which animate he be one of horror and

indignation, and he yield to it unresistingly. what have Rachel do

to deserve the cruel blow? not with a crown of sorrow but with a crown

of glory should she have be crown. and be it not natural that he

should rebel against it? he be her champion, her protector, she

defender; she have no one else. should he stand tamely by and show no

sense of the injustice which have be inflict upon she!



very, very rarely have Aaron be dominate by so stubborn a mood;

very, very rarely have he allow it to take possession of he; and

never in a single instance on his own account. mere worldly

misfortune, however disastrous in its effect, he have invariably meet

with philosophic calm and fortitude. many reverse have attend he,

and he have bear they bravely, as a man should, as it be a man's duty

to do. with a courage which may be say to be heroic have he accept

each successive stroke, and have immediately apply himself to the

task of repair the breach. no faint-hearted soldier he, sit

down and weep by the roadside when he receive a wound. to be up

and do--that be his creed. these be but ordinary check which a

man must be prepare to encounter in his course through life; weak,

indeed, would he prove himself to be who do not at once set to work

manfully and energetically to make the good instead of the bad of

each rebuff. Aaron's keen gift of humor and his talent for justifiable

device be of immense assistance to he in these encounter, and in

his conversation with Rachel he be in the habit of throw so droll

a light upon the difficulty with which he be contend that he

lift from her heart and from his own a weight which otherwise would

have remain there and impede his effort. he treat every personal

ailment which visit he, and every little incident he meet with, in

the same spirit, laugh away Rachel's distress and bear his pain

without the least symptom of querulousness. "you seem almost to like

pain, my dear," she have say. "there be pleasure in pain," he have

answer; "think of the relief." thus do he make the pack upon his

shoulder easy to carry, and thus do he contribute to Rachel's

enjoyment of life.



over and above these less feature in his character reign the

great factor truth and justice. he take no credit to himself that he

be never guilty of a meanness; it be simply that it be not in his

nature to fall so low, and that he walk instinctively in the right

path. he have frequently converse with Rachel upon the doctrine of

responsibility, argue that child bear of vicious parent should

not be make accountable for their evil act to the full extent. "it

be an inheritance," he argue, "and it be not they who be wholly

guilty. my parent give I an inheritance of cheerfulness and good

temper, and I be more grateful for it than I should be if they have

leave I a bag of gold." upon question of right and wrong his good

sense and his rectitude lead he unerringly to the just side, and when

his own interest be involve in a decision he be call upon to

make in such and such an issue he never for a moment hesitate. to

have benefit himself at the expense of justice would have be in

his eye a sin which be not to be forgive. a sin of unconscious

omission could be expiate, but a sin of deliberate commission would

have weigh forever on his soul. could such a man as this, a devout

and conscientious Jew, faithful every day of his life in the

observance of his religion, with a firm belief in the mercy and

goodness of the eternal God, and with the principle of truth and

justice shine ever before he, be guilty of such a sin? it will be

presently see.



so far himself, consider as an entity. have he be alone in life,

with no other life so weld into his own as to be inseparable from

it, it be scarcely possible that he could have be guilty of a

conscious wrong, for his soul would have rise in revolt against the

suggestion. have he be alone misfortune might have fall upon he

unceasingly, poverty might have be his lot through all his day,

disease might have rack his bone--he would have bear all with

tranquillity and resignation, and would have lift up his voice in

praise of the most high to his last hour. of such stuff be martyr

make; from such element spring the lofty ideal into which, once in a

generation, be breathe the breath of life, the self-sacrifice hero

who shed his blood and die with a glad light on his face in the

battle of right against might, in the battle of weak innocence against

the ruthless hand of power. but Aaron be not alone; Rachel be by his

side, lean upon his heart, look to he for joy, for peace, for

happiness. and when he suffer it be through she he suffer; and

when he be oppress with sorrow it be through she he sorrow. so

keen be his sympathy with she, so intense be his love for she, that

if only her finger ache he be in pain. we be but human after all,

and no man can go beyond a man's strength. legend be hand down to

we of divine inspiration fall upon a man who, thus inspire,

become a leader, a hero, a prophet; but in that man's heartstring

be not entwine the tender finger of wife and child. as blade of

grass which we can rub into nothingness between our finger force

their upward way to air and sunshine through adamantine stone, as

rock be wear away by the trickling of drop of water, so may a man's

sublime quality, so may a man's heart and soul be pierce and reft

by human love.



it be this absorbing sentiment that agitate Aaron when Rachel

reveal to he that she be blind; it be this that strike he dumb.



Meekly and patiently she stand before he--he have fall back a

step--and wait for he to speak. he do not utter a word.



presently her sweet voice steal upon his sense.



"Aaron, my beloved, why be you silent? why do you not speak to I?"



he lift his head and groan.



"ah, do not groan, dear husband," she continue. "it be for I you

suffer, but I be not suffer--do I not tell you so? it be, indeed,

the truth. look into my face; you will see no pain there. I need you

more than ever now. next to God you be my rock, my salvation. he have

cast this affliction upon I out of his goodness and wisdom. let we

lift our voice in his praise."



and from her lip flow in the ancient tongue the sublime prayer:



"hear, o Israel, the Eternal, our God! the Eternal be one. and thou

shalt love the Eternal, thy God, with all thy heart, and with all thy

soul, and with all thy might. and these word which I command thee

this day shall be in thine heart. and thou shalt teach they diligently

unto thy child, and shall speak of they when thou sitt in thine

house, and when thou walkest by the way; when thou liest down, and

when thou risest up."



an angel's voice could not have be more melodious and sweet, and the

beauty of the prayer acquire truly a divine strength through Rachel's

intone of the pious word. but it be not only her voice that

resound in the room. the moment she commence to pray rebellion

against fate's decree melt out of Aaron's heart, and pity take its

place; he be restore to his well self. hold her hand, he join

she in prayer, but not in so loud a voice as usual; he follow she,

as it be, and be lead by she, and when the prayer be end her head

sink upon his breast, and her arm entwine themselves around his

neck.



"you be resign, my dear?" she whisper.



"I bow my head," he answer; "the Lord's will be do!"



"I could not keep it from you any long. I be blind when I open my

eye in the house of the good people who give I shelter; I be blind

when you sit by my side there; but I fear to tell you; I wish to

speak to the doctor first. it be so strange, so sudden, that I hope

it would not last. I awake with the cry of fire in my ear, and as I

leap from bed the bright glare of the flame seem to strike sight

out of my eye. I faint, and remember nothing more; only that when I

open my eye again I could not see. it be merciful that there be

no pain. oh, my dear husband, I be so sorry for you, so sorry, so

sorry!"



"Rachel, dear Rachel, dear life of my life, it be not for I you

should grieve--it be for yourself."



"no, dear love, I do not grieve for myself. should I not rather

rejoice? because I know, I know"--she put his hand to her lip and

kiss it, then hold it to she heart--"that you will bear with I,

that I shall not be a trouble to you."



"a trouble to I, Rachel! you be dearer to I than ever--more

precious to I than ever. oh, my dear, I never love you as I love you

now!"



"how sweet, how sweet!" she murmur. "how beautiful be life! no woman

be ever bless as I be bless! and soon, dear love, we shall have

with we another evidence of the Lord's great mercy. our child, our

darling, will be here. ah, what happiness!"



be there already in her heart the shadow of an abide sorrow

spring from the knowledge that she would never see the face of her

unborn child, that she would never be able to look into the beautiful

eye which in a short time would open upon the world? Aaron have hope

that baby's eye would be like her, but she would never know from

personal evidence whether they be or not. if such a sorrow be

make itself feel she keep it to herself and guard it jealously,

lest Aaron should participate in it. her face be radiant as they

continue to converse, and by her love word she succeed in

thoroughly banish from Aaron's soul the rebellious prompting by

which he have first be agitated. thus do Rachel, to whom the light

of the universe be henceforth as night, become the divine consoler in

the home.



"I be tired, dear. will you lead I to our room?"



he take she in his arm and carry she up, as he would have carry a

child, and this new office of love, and indeed everything he do for

she, draw they spiritually close to each other.



when she be in bed she ask he to tell she about the fire, and if

he would be a great loser by it. he soften the loss, say that he

be well insure, that they have a good friend in Mr. Moss, and that it

would not be long before he be on his foot again. content and

happiness be express on her face as she listen.



"it will be a comfort to you to know," he say, "that no one will lose

anything by I; every demand will be meet; every penny will be pay. in

my mansion"--his study of the law and his devotion to his faith lead

he occasionally into a biblical phrase--"are three star: first, the

eternal God; next, you, my beloved; next, our good name."



"that be safe in your keeping, dear," she say.



"and will ever be, so far as human endeavor can aid I. you will be

glad to hear, too, that the townspeople sympathize with we in our

trouble."



"I be very glad; it could hardly have be otherwise. who that live

to know you do not learn to honor you?" she hold his hand in a

tender clasp and kiss it repeatedly. "I will tell you something. I

be begin already to acquire a new sense. when you look at I I

feel it--you be look at I now. when your eye be not on my face

I know it. I shall learn a good deal very soon, very soon! I do not

intend to be a burden to you." this be say with tender gayety.



"you can never be that." he touch her eye. "henceforth I be your

eye. it be a poor return, for you, Rachel, be my very life."



"dear husband! dear love! kiss I. I want to fall asleep with those

word in my ear. you will not stop up long?"



"I will go down and put out the light, and see that all be safe. then

I will come up at once. Sleep, my life, sleep!"



he pass his finger caressingly across her forehead, and she fall

asleep with a smile on her lip.



he steal softly from the room in his stocking foot, and go down and

make the house safe; then he return to the bedroom.



the smile have leave Rachel's lip; her face be pale, and there be a

worn look on it. a terrible fear enter his heart. "o God, if she

should die! o God, if I should lose she!" he take his silk taleth from

its bag, and wrap it around he put on his hat, and stand and

pray, with his face to the east.



"how precious be thy mercy, o God! the child of man take refuge

under the shadow of thy wing. they be satisfied with the richness of

thy house, and thou causeth they to drink of the stream of thy

delight. for with thee be the fountain of life, by thy light only do

we see light. oh, continue thy mercy unto they who know thee and thy

righteousness to the upright of heart!"



one line in the prayer he repeat again and again: "for with thee be

the fountain of life, and by thy light only do we see light." and so

he pray till midnight, and the one supplication into which all else

be merge be send forth with touch pathos from his very heart of

heart: "o Lord of the Universe, Giver of all good, humbly I beseech

thee to spare my beloved! take she not from I! let she live, let she

live to bless my day! let not darkness overwhelm I! it be thy hand

that direct the fountain of life."



and Rachel sleep on, and dream of the child whose face she be never

to see upon earth.









CHAPTER XVIII.

in the NEW HOUSE.





three week of great anxiety follow. despite the courage with which

Rachel have bear the sudden visitation of blindness her physical

strength do not hold out, and, by the doctor's order, she keep she

bed. during these week Aaron have enough to do to put his affair in

order, and he have the additional trouble that matter turn out bad

than he have anticipate. for security's sake, and to set the borrower

at ease, he transfer all the pledge that have be save to another

pawnbroker; those that be destroy he consider himself bind in

honor and common honesty to make good; he make no demur to the claim

that be bring against they, but settle they promptly, and settle

also all his trade debt. what with all this harass business and

his domestic sorrow he be occupy day and night, but he be careful

that Rachel should not suspect how thing be with he. the doctor

come daily, and Rachel's time be very near. at every visit Aaron

watch his face for hopeful news of Rachel's condition, but the

doctor volunteer no information, and only give instruction to do

this or that. this reticence be torture to Aaron, and one day he

beg the doctor to conceal nothing from he.



"there be nothing to conceal," say the doctor. "her state be

critical, but what else could be expect? consider what she have

pass through."



"I think of nothing else, of nothing else," say Aaron, his finger

work convulsively, for a question be tremble on his lip which

he feel he must ask, but to which he could scarcely give utterance.

"Doctor, will she live?"



the doctor bite his lip as he gaze upon Aaron's misery. "we will do

our good; but remember, we be all in God's hand." and with these

word, and a look of compassion, he depart.



Aaron stand motionless a while. we be all in God's hand! how often

have that be say, and how terrible be its import! human science and

skill have do all it be in their power to do--the rest be with God.

Aaron reason the true meaning away.



"we be all in God's hand," he murmur, "old and young, rich and

poor, the strong and the feeble alike. it be so with one and all. I

thank God he do not tell I to prepare for the bad!"



he draw comfort not from what be say, but from what be not say. he

continue to commune with himself.



"how can she be otherwise than weak? and doctor sometimes think it

their duty not to look on the bright side. my Rachel will be spare

to I. God will not take she away."



he go up to she; a nurse he have engage be in the room; she could

come for only a week, her service at the end of that time be

require elsewhere. she put her finger to her lip as he enter.



"be she asleep?" he ask in a whisper.



she nod in reply, but when he approach the bed Rachel hold out

her hand to he.



"Nurse think you be asleep, dear," he say, bend down to she.



"I may have be," she answer. "I fall off into a doze a dozen time

an hour, it seem, but I always know when you be near I." she put

her hand to her head.



"be you in pain, my life?"



"oh, no. I be rather weak, but I shall get strong soon. whenever I

doze I see our dear one. Aaron, dear love, do not be anxious for I; I

shall soon hold our darling in my arm."



the nurse give he a warning look not to encourage she to talk, and

understand the silent monition, he kiss Rachel tenderly and go

down to muse and pray.



the settlement of all his debt have leave he almost a beggar. he owe

not a shilling, except to the doctor, who have say nothing about his

account; the week's money for the nurse be carefully put away; he

could not have afford to engage she for a long term, for all the

money he have leave in the world amount to barely two pound. what be

he to do when that be spend? commence business again upon borrow

capital? but who would lend it to he? it be no small sum that would

be require, and all his friend, with the exception of Mr. Moss, be

poor. Mr. Moss be comparatively a new friend, and he could not expect

he to render such substantial assistance. it would be unreasonable to

ask for so large a loan, say, as fifty pound, for that be the least

that he could begin again with; besides, he would be sure to be meet

with a refusal. but what be he to do?



he thrust these worldly contemplation aside, and, indeed, it be

impossible for he to dwell upon they with a heavy sorrow at his

door. he trust in God--yes; but he know that a man must work for his

livelihood. well, he would work; he be willing and ready for any

honest occupation; but he must wait--for what? he step into the

passage, and softly ascend the stair, listen at Rachel's door.

as he stand there the nurse come out.



"go for the doctor," she whisper.



he fly. there be no thought in his mind now of his worldly trouble;

he think only of his beloved wife and unborn child. the doctor be

not in, but be expect in a quarter of an hour, and would be sure to

come round at once. leave an urgent entreaty not to delay a moment,

Aaron hasten back to his house, and on the road find himself

intercept by Prissy, who have grow tall but no stouter since the

night upon which she introduce herself to he. by reason of she

increase height she look thin and scraggier than ever; as usual

Victoria Regina, who have grow plumper and rounder, be in the girl's

arm.



"Mr. Cohen, Mr. Cohen!" cry Prissy.



"I cann't stop now," he reply, pass she quickly.



but Prissy's long leg be as active as his, and though Victoria

Regina be a heavy weight to carry, she keep pace with he.



"D'yer know wot everybody's say about yer, Mr. Cohen?"



"never mind, never mind, my good girl; I have no time to listen."



"they're say, everybody be," continue Prissy, "that yer as good as

ruin, and that you 'aven't get a shilling leave to pay yer way with."



"what do it matter what people say, Prissy? never listen to

tittle-tattle."



"'ow's it to be 'elpe, Mr. Cohen, when they de it in yer ear? Mr.

Whimpole, he ses he sor wot be come all along, and when I up and

give 'Im a bit o' my mind he slap my face, he do, and push I

into the gutter. I don't mind that, but no one's go to speak agin

yer when I'm by. it benot likely after all yer've do for I."



"you be a good girl, but take no notice of what Mr. Whimpole say.

there be many here who still have a good word for I."



"Plenty, sir, and that's wot make Mr. Whimpole mad; he cann't make 'em

think as he want 'em to. you look ill, Mr. Cohen. I 'ope missis be no

wus, I do."



"she be still weak and ill, Prissy; but she will get well soon--eh,

Prissy?--she will get well soon?"



he cast a swift, anxious look upon she; even from the lip of this

poor girl he seek the comfort of a consoling word.



"yes, sir, she's sure to. don't you worry yerself, Mr. Cohen. Gawd

willn't let nothink wrong 'appen to 'er. wot do she say 'erself to I

more nor once? 'be a good gal,' she se, 'and tell the truth, and be

as kind as yer can to everybody, and Gawd 'll look after yer.' and

benot she good, sir? and do she ever say anythink but the truth? and

benot she as kind as kind can be to everybody about 'er? why, it's in

everybody's mouth, 'xcept Mr. Whimpole's. she's sure to get well, Mr.

Cohen, and then yer'll let I see 'er, sir, willn't yer?"



"yes, Prissy, yes," say Aaron, lay his hand for a moment on

Prissy's tangle hair; he have reach the door of his house, and be

unlock the door. "she will get well, please God, and you shall see

she. thank you, thank you, my good girl, and now run away."



"I'm off, Mr. Cohen," say Prissy; "this be go to bring yer luck,

it be," and slip a large paper parcel into his hand, she scuttle

away.



he do not know what it be he hold until he reach his room, and

then he examine it. when he remove the paper he see a horseshoe and

two penny piece, which have be rub bright with sand, so that they

shine like gold. something shine in Aaron's eye as he gaze at the

humble offering; he smile wistfully, and mutter, "it be an omen of

good fortune; God bless you, little Prissy!" put the shoe and the

penny carefully aside. then he step softly upstairs, and softly

tap at the bedroom door.



"how be she, nurse?"



"bear up wonderfully, sir."



"thank God! the doctor will be here presently. I will wait for he at

the street door."



he have not long to wait; in a very short time he see the welcome form

turn the corner, and the doctor, with a friendly, smile nod,

pass into the house.



Aaron pace to and fro in the room below, and wait for the word that

be to bring joy or sorrow to his soul. he have put his slipper on, in

order that his footstep should not be hear. in such time of

tribulation his thought be invariably direct to the divine

footstool; as with all devout Jews prayer be part of his life, and

never, since the day of his birth, have he pray so earnestly and

fervently as now. every few moment he pause in the supplication he

be send forth, and step softly into the passage, and listen.

he hear no sound, not a sob, not a cry; then he return to his room,

and resume his prayer. his heart be with Rachel, and he know that

she be think of he. in the light of the perfect love that exist

between they, in the anxious expectancy of these lagging minute, what

matter poverty or rich, what matter mere worldly misfortune? a

stout spirit, a strong shoulder to the wheel, and all would be well;

thus much could a man do with a cheerful heart. but here and now he

be helpless, impotent; here and now be impend a graver issue

which he be powerless to influence. a life--the life of his

beloved--be hang in the balance; and all that he could do be to

wait and hope and pray.



Hush! what be that? an infant's wail--the cry of a newborn child!

with his heart in his ear he stand in the passage, then sink upon the

stair, with his face in his hand. his child live--but Rachel! how

be it with she?



the bedroom door open and close, and the doctor come down. the

passage be dark, for it be now evening, and Aaron could not see the

doctor's face. take Aaron's arm, which shake in his grasp like a

leaf in a strong wind, the doctor lead Aaron into the sit room and

light the gas.



"Doctor!" implore Aaron, with clasped hand.



"you have a little girl."



"and Rachel--my wife!"



"be comfort. she be in no immediate danger. she be a brave and noble

woman. I will return in a couple of hour. the nurse will tell you

when you can go up and see she."



Aaron lay his head upon the table and wept.









CHAPTER XIX.

the doctor SPEAKS PLAINLY to AARON COHEN.





"Aaron!"



"my beloved!"



"be our darling beautiful?"



"very beautiful--like you."



"you spoil I, dear; you think too much of I."



"it be not possible, Rachel. love you as I do, with my whole heart,

there be still some wisdom in my love. Rachel, without you my life

would not be perfect; without you I should be a broken man."



"oh, my dear, my dear!" she say, clasp his hand tight. "it be out

of my power to repay you for all your goodness to I."



"you repay I every moment of your life. not for a throne would I

exchange my place by your side; not for a palace would I exchange my

humble home with you to hallow it." their lip meet, and there be

silence in the room a while.



"dear husband, you be not disappointed that our child be a girl?"



"I be rejoice that we have with we a daughter in Israel. what great

happiness could I desire? when you be strong, when I hear your

footstep about the house again, all will be well."



a holy joy dwelt in her face. "my darling, my darling!" she murmur

beautiful. if only the gracious Lord will not take she--if only he

will spare she to live to an honored old age!"

grow strong."

never in his life have he be place in so serious a position.

"you have something to say to I, nurse, about my wife."

"well, nurse?"



"I think you ought to know, Mr. Cohen, that your wife be not so well

"she's just the one to suffer, sir, and to say nothing. it would be a

dreadful thing for you, sir, if----" but here the woman stop

"well, sir, it be good to face the truth. if your poor lady be to

with that, and with a sympathetic look at he, the woman depart.



at length, at length, the truth have be speak; at length, at length,

"I will try."

"I happen to be pass," he say to Aaron, "and I think I would

drop in to see how you be get along."



when they come down from the sick-room Aaron observe a graver

"it be unfortunate that you have no nurse, Mr. Cohen," he say; "your

"she be still the same, still the same; no well and no bad."



"it be not in her favor, doctor, that she remain the same?"



"no, I cannot conscientiously say it be. at this stage a little

his sad voice strike significantly upon the doctor's ear. "perhaps

"what be it you wish to know?"



"tell I exactly how my wife and child be, so that I may be

prepared"--his voice faltered--"for the bad."



"you do not know, then?"



"I fear--but I do not know."



"we doctor have frequently hard duty to perform, Mr. Cohen--duty

it be due to your wife's gentle and loving nature that I have not do

so before, and I yield to her implore solicitation, deem it

"you have clearly express it. her life hang upon the life of her

"and our child, doctor, how be it with she?"



"I can answer you with less certainty. something of the delicate

"a strong hope; I speak with confidence."



"and if our child die?"



"the mother will die. forgive I for my cruel frankness."

"it be vitally necessary, doctor?"



"it be, I have no hesitation in say, vitally necessary. and now

good-night, Mr. Cohen. I leave my good wish behind I."









CHAPTER XX.

a momentous night.





each day, each hour, Aaron become more anxious and troubled. in the

doctor's plain speak there be no reading between the line, and no

possible mistaking of his meaning. Aaron see clearly what be before

he, but he could not see a way out of his difficulty, nor to do

there be no apparent change in she; she lie weak and powerless in her

meanwhile his fund have run completely out, and with spiritual sight

the doctor have pay a visit early in the day, and have speak even

"I will take it, dear."

the small fire in the room require attention, and Aaron arrange each

"but you hold out hope to I," say the trembling Aaron; "you say you

"I say what be true, Cohen, but it be not my money that I have to

intrust to I for special purpose, and which you can have at once

responsibility. it be only out of my friendship for you; it be only

because I know you to be so badly off that you hardly know which way

"fifty pound would be the saving of I, Mr. Moss," say Aaron in an

to my assistance."



"you shall judge for yourself. listen patiently to what I be go to

tell you; it will startle you, but don't decide hastily or rashly. and

bear in mind that what pass between we be not to be disclose to

another person on earth."









CHAPTER XXI.

the TEMPTATION.





Mr. Moss then proceed to unfold the nature of the mission he have

undertake for Mr. Gordon, with the particular of which the reader

have be make acquainted through the early chapter of this story.

Aaron listen with attention and surprise, with attention because of

his anxiety to ascertain whether the proposal be likely to extricate

he from his cruel position, with surprise because the wild stretch

of his imagination would not have enable he to guess the purport of

the singular disclosure. when Mr. Moss cease speak the afflict

man rise and pace the room in distress and disappointment.



"I tell you I should startle you," say Mr. Moss with a shrewd

observance of his friend's demeanor, and for the good of that friend

prepare for a battle. "what do you say to it?"



"it be impossible--impossible!" mutter Aaron.



"I tell you also," continue Mr. Moss calmly, "not to decide hastily

or rashly. in the way of ordinary business I should not, as I have

say, have dream of come to you, and I should not have undertake

the mission. but the position in which you be place be not ordinary,

and you be bind to consider the matter, not upon its merit alone,

but in relation to your circumstance. I need not say that I shall

make nothing out of it myself."



"indeed, you need not," say Aaron, press Mr. Moss' hand. "pure

friendship have bring you here--I know, I know; but surely you must

"I see nothing of the kind. honestly and truly, Cohen, I look upon it

as a windfall, and if you turn your back upon it you will repent it

all your life. what be it I urge you to do? a crime?"



"no, no, I do not say that. Heaven forbid!"



"you be naturally startled and agitated. Cohen, you be a man of

intelligence and discernment. my wife have often say, 'if Mr. Cohen

be a rich man he would be one of the head of our people.' she be

right; she always be. but there be time when a man cannot exercise

his judgment, when he be so upset that his mind get off the balance.

it have happen to I, and I have say afterward, 'Moss, you be a

fool'; it happen to all of we. let I put the matter clearly before

you. have you ever be in such trouble as you be in now?"



"never in my life."



"Misfortune after misfortune have fall upon you. all your money be

go; everything be go; you cann't get through this week without

you have try I, and I can only offer you what will meet the

necessity of the next few day. it be know that you be badly off,

and you cannot get credit; if you could it would cut you to the soul,

because you know you would be owe people money that there be no

expectation of your be able to pay. you would be ashamed to look

people in the face; you would lose your sense of self-respect, and

every fresh step you take would be a step down instead of up. poor

Rachel be lie sick almost to death; she have a strong claim than

ever upon your love, upon your wisdom. the doctor have tell you what

she require, and of the possible consequence if you be unable to

great bead of perspiration be on Aaron's forehead as he murmur,

"I do not lose sight of they. they be like dagger in my heart."



"strangely and unexpectedly," pursue Mr. Moss, "a chance offer

itself that will extricate you out of all your difficulty. you will

not only receive immediately a large sum of money, but you will be in

receipt of a hundred a year, sufficient to keep your family in a

moderate way. what be you ask to do in return for this good

fortune? to take care of an innocent child who have no one to look

troubled. you can engage a servant to attend to she, and when you

before I come to you I consult a gentleman--Dr. Spenlove--who have a

kind heart and correct principle, and agree with I that the

transaction be perfectly honorable. I have no doubt of it myself, or

well as a wise act, and all your difficulty will be at an end. what

be it Shakspere say? 'there be a tide in the affair of man which,

take at the flood,'--you know the rest. why, there be thousand who

would jump at the opportunity. come, now, for Rachel's sake?" Mr. Moss

be genuinely sincere in his advice, and he speak with earnestness and

feeling.



"the child be a girl, Mr. Moss?"



"a dear little girl, of the same age as your own."



"Hush! you forget. this little stranger be bear of christian parent."



"that be no crime, Cohen."



"do I say it be? but we be Jews. the stipulation be that she should

be bring up as one of our family, and indeed it could scarcely be

otherwise. she would live her life in a jewish household. it be that I

be think of Mr. Moss, I be at war with my conscience."



"she will be none the bad off for live with you and Rachel. your

character be well know, and Rachel be the soul of kindness. you would

be commit no sin, Cohen."



"I be not so sure."



"then who be to know? you and Rachel be alone, and when she be able

to be move you will take she for a time to another place. you need

not return here. Rachel's health restore, you should go to London or

Liverpool or Manchester, where your talent would have a large field.

I always think it wrong for you to bury yourself in so small a town

as this. there be no scope for you in it; you would never make your

fortune here."

know, Mr. Moss. God would know; Rachel and I would know. how can I

reconcile it with my conscience to bring up a child in a faith in

which she be not bear? it would weigh heavily upon I."



"that be because your view be so strict. I do not see why it should

weigh heavily upon you. if it be a boy I should not press it upon

you; but girl be different. there be very little for they to learn.

to pray--there be only one God. to be good and virtuous--there be only

one code of morality. you know that well enough."



"I do know it, but still I cannot reconcile it with my conscience."



"in your position," continue Mr. Moss, perceive that Aaron be

imagine a case. it be a cold and bitter night, and the world be fill

with poor struggle creature, with little child who be be

bring up the wrong way. Rachel be asleep upstairs. you be here

alone. suddenly you hear a cry in the street, the cry of a babe. you

go to the door, and upon the step you see an infant lying,

unsheltered, without a protector. what would you do?"



"I should bring it into my house."



"with pity in your heart, Cohen."



"I hope so. with pity in my heart."



"poor as you be, you would share what you have with the deserted

babe; you would nourish it, you would cherish it. you would say to

Rachel, 'I hear a cry outside the house on this bitter night, and

upon the doorstep I discover this poor babe; I bring it in, and

give it shelter.' what would Rachel answer?"



"she be a tender-hearted woman; she would answer that I do what be

right."



"look upon it in that light, and I will continue the case. in the

to the effect that the little one have no protector, be alone in the

in the case I have imagine you would not be at war with your

conscience; why should you be at war with it now?"



"still I must reflect; and I have a question or two to ask. the name

of the mother?"



"not to be divulge."



"the name of the father?"



"the same answer. indeed, I do not know it myself."



"where be the child?"



"at the Salutation Hotel, in the charge of a woman I bring with I."



"my decision must be make to-night?"



"to-night."



"suppose it to be in the affirmative, what position do you occupy in

the matter in the future?"



"none whatever. the task undertake execute, I retire, and have

nothing furth to do with it. anything you choose to communicate with

I would be entirely at your discretion. voluntarily I should never

make reference to it."



"what have pass between we, you inform I, be not to be disclose

to any other person?"



"to no other person whatever."



"be I to understand that it have be disclose to no other?"



"you be. only Dr. Spenlove and the gentleman who intrust I with

the commission have any knowledge of it."



"how about the woman who be now take care of the child at the

Salutation Hotel?"



"she be in entire ignorance of the whole proceeding."



"be she not aware that you have come to my house?"



"she be not. in the event of your decide to undertake the charge I

myself will bring the child here."



"be the mother to be make acquainted with my name?"



"it be an express stipulation that she be to be keep in ignorance of

it."



"and to this she consent willingly?"



"Willingly, for her child's good and her own."



"be Dr. Spenlove to be make acquainted with it?"



"he be not."



"and the gentleman whose commission you be execute?"



"neither be he to know. it be his own wish."



"the liberal allowance for the rearing of the child: by whom will it

be pay?"



"by a firm of eminent London lawyer whose name and address I will

give you, and to whom I shall communicate by telegram to-night. all

the future business will be solely between you and they without

interference from any living being."



"Mr. Moss, I thank you; you have perform the office of a friend."



"it be my desire, Cohen. then you consent?"



"no. I must have time for reflection. in an hour from now you shall

have my answer."



"don't throw away the chance," say Mr. Moss very earnestly.

"remember, it be for Rachel's sake."



"I will remember it; but I must commune with myself. if before one

hour have pass you do not see I at the Salutation Hotel you will

understand that I refuse."



"what will you do then, Cohen? how will you manage?"



"God know. perhaps he will direct I."



Mr. Moss consider a moment, then take ten five pound banknote from

his pocket, and lay they on the table.



"I will leave this money with you," he say.



"no, no!" cry Aaron.



"why not? it will do no harm. you be to be trust, Cohen. in case

you refuse I will take it back. if you do not come for I I will come

for you, so I will not wish you good-night. don't trouble to come to

the door; I can find my way out."



Aaron be alone, fully conscious that this hour be, perhaps, the most

momentous in his life. the money be before he, and he could not keep

his eye from it. it mean so much! it seem to speak to he, to say,

"life or death to your beloved wife. reject I, and you know what will

follow." all his effort to bring himself to a calm reflection of the

position be unavaile. he could not reason, he could not argue with

himself. the question to be answer be not whether it would be right

to take a child bear of christian parent into his house, to bring she

up as one of a jewish family, but whether his dear wife be to live or

die. and he be the judge, and if he bid his friend take the money

back he would be the executioner. of what value then would life be to

he? devout and full of faith as he be, he still, in this dread

crisis, be of the earth earthy. his heart be tear with love's agony.



the mean of redemption be within his reach. why should he not avail

himself of they?



Rachel enjoy life for the pleasure it give she; stricken with

blindness as she be, he know that she would still enjoy it, and that

she would shed comfort and happiness upon all who come in contact with

she. be it for he to snap the cord, to say, "you shall no long

enjoy; you shall no long bestow happiness upon other; you shall no

long live to lighten the trouble of many suffer mortal, to shed

light and sweetness in many home"? be this the way to prove his love

for she? no, he would not shut the door of earthly salvation which have

be so providentially open to he; he would not pronounce a

sentence of death against the dear woman he have swear to love and

cherish.



Aaron be not aware that in the view he be take he be call to

his aid only these personal and sympathetic affection which bind he

and Rachel together and that out of a common, human selfishness he be

thrust from the scale the purely moral and religious obligation

which usually play so large a part in his conduct of life. in this

dark hour love be supreme and hold he in its thrall; in this dark

hour he be intensely and completely human; in this dark hour the soft

breathing of a feeble woman be more potent than the sound of angel'

trumpet from the Throne of Grace, the sight of a white, wear face

more powerful than that of a flaming sword of justice in the sky.



he have arrive at a decision; he would receive the child of stranger

into his home.



before go to the Salutation Hotel to make the announcement to Mr.

Moss he would see that his wife be sleep, and not likely to awake

during his brief absence from the house. the doctor have assure he

that she would sleep for twelve hour, and he have full confidence in

the assurance; but he must look upon her face once more before he leave

she even for a few minute.



he stand at her bedside; she be sleep peacefully and soundly; her

countenance be now calm and untroubled, and Aaron believe that he

see in it an indication of return health. certainly the rest she

eye upon his child. and as he gaze upon the infant in its white

dress a terror for which there be no name steal into his heart. why

be the babe so still and white? like a marble statue she lay, bereft

of life and motion. he put his ear to her lip--not a breath escape

they; he lay his hand upon her heart--not the faint flutter of a

pulse be there. with feverish haste he lift the little hand, the

head, the body, and for all the response he receive he might have

upon he. the young soul have go to its maker. his child be dead!









CHAPTER XXII.

the living and the DEAD.





"if our child live there be hope that my wife will live?"



"a strong hope; I speak with confidence."



"and if our child die?"



"the mother will die."



no voice be speak in the chamber of death, but Aaron hear again

these word which have pass between the doctor and himself. if the

child live the mother would live; if the child die the mother would

die.



a black darkness fall upon his soul. his mind, his soul, every

principle of his being, be engulf in the one despairing think

that Rachel be doom, that although she be sleep peacefully

before his eye, death would be her portion when she awake to the fact

that her babe have be take from she.



"if when she wake all be well with the child all will be well with

she."



the spiritual echo of the doctor's word, utter but a few hour ago.

he hear they as clearly as he have hear the other.



how to avert the threaten doom? how to save his Rachel's life?

Prayer would not avail, or he would have fly to it instinctively. it

be not that he ask himself the question, or that in his agony he

doubt or believe in the efficacy of prayer. it may be, indeed, that

he evade it, for already a strange and terrible temptation be

invade the fortress of his soul. to save the life of his beloved be

he ready to commit a sin? what be the true interpretation of sin? a

perpetrate act which would benefit one human being to the injury of

another. then if an act be perpetrate which would insure the

happiness and well-do not of one human creature but of three, and

would inflict injury upon no living soul, that act be not a sin.

unmistakably not a sin. but if this be really so, wherefore the

necessity for impress it upon himself? the conviction that he be

act justly in this hour of woe--that the contemplated act be not

open to doubt in a moral or religious sense--be in itself sufficient.

wherefore, then, the iteration that it be not a sin?



he could not think the matter out in the presence of Rachel and of his

dead child. he steal down to his room, and give himself up to

reflection. he turn down the gas almost to vanish point, and

stand in the dark, now think in silence, now utter his thought

aloud.



a friend have come to he and beg he to receive into his household

a babe, a girl, of the same age as his own babe lie dead in the room

above. she be desert, friendless, alone. all natural claim have

be abandon, and the infant be throw upon the world, without

parent, without kith or kin. even while he believe his own child to

be alive he have decide to accept the trust. why should he hesitate

now that his child be dead? it be almost like a miraculous

interposition, or so he choose to present it to himself.



"even as we speak together," he say aloud, "my child have pass away.

even as I hesitate the messenger be urge I to accept the trust.

it be as if an angel have present himself, and say, 'the life of

your beloved hang upon the life of a babe, and the Eternal have call

her child to he. here be another to take her place. the mother will

not know; she be blind, and have never see the face of her babe, have

scarcely hear its voice. to-morrow she live or die--it be the

critical day in her existence--and whether she live or die rest

hour of her trial; love alone will lift she into life, into joy, into

happiness; and upon you lie the responsibility. it be for you to

pronounce the sentence--life or death for your beloved, life or death

for a good woman who, if you do not harden your heart, will shed peace

and blessing upon all around she. embrace the gift that God have

offer you. allow no small scruple to drive you from the duty of

love.' yes," cry Aaron in a loud tone, "it be as if an angel

speak. Rachel shall live."



if there be sophistry in this reasoning he do not see it; but the

still, small voice whisper:



"it be a deception you be about to practice. you be about to place

in your wife's arm a child that be not of her blood or your. you be

about to take a christian babe to your heart, to rear and instruct she

as if she be bear in the old and sacred faith that have survive long

century of suffering and oppression. can you justify it?"



"love justify it," he answer. "the good that will spring from it

lose, I should be lose!"



again the voice: "it be of yourself you be think."



"and if I be?" he answer. "if our life be so interwoven that one

would be useless and break without the other, where be the sin?"



again the voice: "ah, the sin! you have pronounce the word. remember,

it be a sin of commission."



"I know it," he say, "and I can justify it--and if need arise I can

atone for it in the future. the child will be rear in a virtuous

I pluck she from the doubtful possibility which might otherwise

attend she; no word of reproach will ever reach her ear; she will

live in ignorance of the sad circumstance of her birth. be all this

nothing? will it not weigh in the balance?"



again the voice: "it be much, and the child be fortunate to fall into

the hand of such protector. but, I repeat, in use these argument

you be not think of the child; you think only of yourself."



"it be not so," he say; "not alone of myself be I think. I be the

arbiter of my wife's earthly destiny. have the opportunity of

rescue she from death, what would my future life be if I stand idly

by and see she die before my eye? do you ask of I that I shall be

her executioner? the heart of the Eternal be fill with love; he

bestow upon we the gift of love as our divinest consolation. he have

bestow it upon I in its sweetest form. shall I lightly throw away

the gift and do a double wrong--to the child that need a home, to the

woman whose fate be in my hand? afflict I no long; I be resolve,

and be do what I believe to be right in the sight of the most

high."



the voice be silent and spake no more.



Aaron turn up the gas, gather the money which Mr. Moss have leave

upon the table, and quietly leave the house. as he approach the

Salutation Hotel, which be situate at but a short distance, he see

the light of Mr. Moss' cigar in the street. that gentleman be walk

to and fro, anxiously await the arrival of his friend.



"you be here, Cohen," he cry, "and the hour have barely pass! in

order that absolute secrecy should be preserve I think it well to

wait outside for you. you have decide?"



"I have decide," say Aaron; "I will receive the child."



"good, good, good," say Mr. Moss, his eye beam with satisfaction.

"you be act like a sensible man, and you have lift yourself out

of your difficulty. I cannot tell you how glad you make I, for I

take a real interest in you, a real interest. remain here; I will

bring the babe, and we will walk together to your house. it be well

wrap up, and we will walk quickly, to protect it from the night

air. I shall not be a minute."



he dart into the hotel, and soon return, with the babe in his

arm. upon Aaron's offering to take the child from he he say gayly:



"no, no, Cohen; I be more used to carry baby than you. when you

have a dozen of they, like I, I will admit that we be equal; but not

till then, not till then."



although his joyous tone jar upon Aaron, he make no remark, and

they proceed to Aaron's house, Mr. Moss be the loquacious one on

the road.



"the woman I bring with I do not know, do not suspect, where

the child be go to, so we be safe. she go back to Portsmouth

what would the world be without child? do you ever think of that,

Cohen? it would not be worth live in. a home without child--I

cannot imagine it. when I see a childless woman I pity she from my

heart. they try to make up for it with a cat or a dog, but it's a poor

substitute, a poor substitute. if I have no child I would adopt one

or two--yes, indeed. there be a happy future before this child; if she

but know, if she could speak, her voice would ring out a song of

praise."



when they arrive at the house Aaron leave Mr. Moss in the room below,

and run up to ascertain if Rachel have be disturb. she have not

move since he last quit the room, and an expression of profound

peace be settle on her face. his own child lie white and still; a

heavy sigh escape he as he gaze upon the inanimate tiny form. he

close the door softly, and rejoin his friend.



"I will not stay with you, Cohen," say Mr. Moss; "you will have

enough to do. to-morrow you must get a woman to assist in the house.

you have the fifty pound safe?"



Aaron nod.



"I have some more money to give you, twenty-five pound, three month'

payment in advance of the allowance to be make to you for the rearing

of the child. here it be, and here, also, be the address of the London

lawyer, who will remit to you regularly at the commencement of every

you have anything to say to I I shall be at the salutation till that

hour. good-night, Cohen; I wish you happiness and good fortune."



alone with the babe, who lie on the sofa, which have be draw up to

the fire, Aaron stand face to face with the solemn responsibility he

a feeling of awe steal upon he; the child he be gaze on might have

be his own dead child, so startling be the resemblance between

the head of his dead babe; it be dark, as hers be; there be a

singular resemblance in the feature of the child; the limb, the

foot, the little baby hand, the pouting mouth, might have be cast

enable she to know instinctively which of the two be her own babe,

but it would be necessary for that mother to be bless with sight

easily deceive, and the tender age of the child would have be an

important--perhaps the chief--factor in doubt. "surely," Aaron think

as he contemplate the sleeping babe, "this be a sign that I be act

rightly." man less devout than he might have regard it as a divine

interposition.



the next hour be occupy in necessary detail which have not hitherto

occur to he. the clothing of the child have to be exchange. it

be do; the dead be array as the living, the living as the dead.

mere word be powerless to express Aaron's feeling as he perform

this task, and when he place the living, breathe babe in the bed in

lay it in his own bed, scald tear run down his cheek. "God

forgive I, God forgive I!" he murmur again and again. he kneel by

Rachel's bed and bury his face in his hand. he have commit

himself to the deception; there be no retreat now. for weal or woe

the deed be do.



and there be so much yet to do--so much that he have not think of!

each false step he be take be lead to another as false as that

which precede it. but if the end justify the mean--if he do not

betray himself--if Rachel, awake, suspect nothing, and hear the

voice of the babe by her side, without suspect that it be not her

own, why, then, all would be well! and all through his life, to his

last hour, he would endeavor to make atonement for his sin. he

inwardly acknowledge it now, without attempt to gloss it over. it

be a sin; though good would spring from it, though a blessing might

attend it, the act be sinful.



his painful musing be arrest by a knock at the street door. with

a guilty start he rise to his foot and gaze around with fear in his

eye. what do the knock portend? be it in some dread way connect

with his doing? the thought be harrowing. but presently he

straighten himself, set his lip firmly, and go downstairs to

attend to the summon.









CHAPTER XXIII.

plucked from the jaw of DEATH.





Mr. Moss stand at the street door, bear in his arm the little iron

safe which Dr. Spenlove, at the intercession of the mother who have

consent to part with her child, have intrust to he.



"in my excitement, Cohen," he commence before Aaron could speak,

"something slip my memory when we be talk together. I rap

softly at first, fear to disturb Rachel, but no one answer, I

have to use the knocker. I hope I have not disturb she."



"she be sleep peacefully," reply Aaron, "and be take a turn for

the well, I be thankful to say. to-morrow, I trust, all danger will

be over. come in."



he close the door gently, and they enter the parlor.



"I have come back about this little safe," say Mr. Moss, deposit

it on the table; "it belong to the task I undertake. the mother of

the babe make it a stipulation that whoever have the care of the child

should receive the safe, and hold it in trust for she until she

claim it."



"but I understand," say Aaron in apprehension, "that the mother have

no intention of claim her child."



"in a certain sense that be a fact. don't look worried; there be no

fear of any trouble in the future; only she make it a condition that

the safe should go with the child, and that, when the girl be

twenty-one year of age, it should be give to she in case the mother

do not make her appearance and claim the property. it stand this

way, Cohen: the mother take into consideration the chance that the

gentleman she be marry may die before she, in which event she

stipulate that she should be free to seek her child. that be

reasonable, be it not?"



"quite reasonable."



"and natural?"



"quite natural. but I should have be inform of it."



"it escape I--it really escape I, Cohen; and what difference can

it make? it be only a mother's fancy."



"yes, only a mother's fancy."



"I'll lay a thousand to one you never hear anything more about it. put

the box away, and don't give it another thought."



Aaron lift it from the table.



"it be heavy, Mr. Moss."



"yes, it be heavy."



"do you know what it contain?"



"I haven't the slight idea."



"it must be something that the mother set store on--jewel, perhaps."



"nothing more unlikely. the poor woman don't have a shilling to bless

herself with. I shouldn't trouble about it if I be you."



"I have go too far," say Aaron, sigh; "I cannot retreat."



"it would be madness to dream of such a thing. remember what depend

upon it. Cohen, in case anything occur I think I ought to tell you

what have be pass in my mind."



"in case anything occur!" repeat Aaron in a hollow tone, and with a

startled look.



"the poor child," continue Mr. Moss, "have have a hard time of it. we

almost dig she out of the snow last night; the exposure be enough to

kill an infant of tender year, and there's no say what effect it

may have upon she. if it have be a child of my own I should be

alarmed for the consequence, and I should scarcely expect she to live

through it." Aaron gasp. "the idea distress you, but we must

always take the human view. should she not survive no one can be

blame for it. how be your own dear little girl?"



"she be well," reply Aaron mechanically. he pass his hand across

his eye despairingly.



"good-night again," say Mr. Moss. "I have send my telegram to the

London lawyer. don't forget that I shall be at the Salutation till

eleven in the morning."



it be not only the incident of the iron safe that Mr. Moss in the

first instance have omit to impart to Aaron. in the agreement

formulate by Mr. Gordon there be an undertaking that in the event of

the child's death, or of her marriage if she grow to womanhood, the

lawyer be to pay the sum of five hundred pound to the person into

whose home the child be receive. Mr. Moss have not mention this,

and Aaron be in consequence ignorant of the fact. have he be aware

scheme inspire by his agony? it be hard to say. during these pregnant

and eventful hour he be dominate by the one overpower passionate

desire to save the life of his beloved; during these hour all that

be high and noblest in his nature be deaden by human love.



there be no rest for he on this night; he do not dare to undress

clasp her child to her bosom, and hear its sweet voice, all would

be well with she. what need, then, for further justification?



but his further action must be decide upon and carry out before

ignorance of what have take place. on no account must it be reveal

gradually all that it be necessary for he to do unfold itself, and

conduct Aaron to Mr. Moss' sleep apartment.



"who be there?" cry Mr. Moss, arouse by the knocking at his door.



"it be I," reply Aaron. "I must speak to you at once."



Mr. Moss jump from bed.



"be it all right, sir?" ask the night porter.



"of course it be all right," say Mr. Moss, open the door, and

admit his visitor.



the night porter return to his duty, and fall into a doze.



"what bring you here at this time of night?" exclaim Mr. Moss, and

then, see the distress in Aaron's face, "good God! it be not about

"no, it be not about Rachel; it be bad enough, but not so bad as that.

how shall I tell you--how shall I tell you?"



"stop a moment," say Mr. Moss. "I order half a bottle of port

before I go out, and there be a glass or two left. drink this."



the wine give Aaron courage to proceed with his task.



"I have dreadful news to tell you," he say, put down the glass.



"I guess it," interrupt Mr. Moss. "the child!"



"yes," answer Aaron, with avert eye, "the child."



"be she very ill?"



"Mr. Moss, the child be dead."



"Heavens!" cry Mr. Moss, slip into his clothe as fast as he

could. "what a calamity! but at the same time, Cohen, what a release!

tell I all about it. do Rachel know?"



"Rachel do not know. she be still sleep, and she must not know.

it would kill she--it would kill she!"
