something in her pocket. a sad ending, Cohen, a sad ending, but I

fear it. do I not prophesy it? what else be to be expect after

last night's adventure? but you have not tell I how it occur."



"it be very simple," say Aaron in a low tone. "I lay the child in

my own bed, intend to call in a woman as soon after daylight as

possible to attend it till Rachel be well and able to get about. she

seem to be asleep, and be in no pain. I determine not to go to

bed, but to keep up all night, to attend to the little one, and to

Rachel and my own child. Bear with I, Mr. Moss, I be unstrung."



"no wonder. take time, Cohen, take time."



"now and again I go to look at the child, and observe nothing to

alarm I. an hour ago I close my eye, and must have sleep; I be

tired out. when I awake I go upstairs, and be startle by a strange

stillness in the child. I lift she in my arm. Mr. Moss, she be

dead. I come to you at once to advise I what to do. you must help I,

Mr. Moss; my dear Rachel's life hang upon it. you know how sensitive

she be; and the doctor have warn I that a sudden shock might be

fatal."



"I will help you, Cohen, of course I will help you; it be my duty,

because it be I who have bring this trouble upon you. but I do it

with the good intention. I see a way out of the difficulty. the woman

I employ--how fortunate, how fortunate that she be still here!--is a

godsend to we. she be a kind-hearted creature, and she will be sorry

to hear of the child's death, but at the same time she be poor and

will be glad to earn a sovereign. a doctor must see the child, to

testify that she die a natural death. she must have pass away in

her sleep."



"she do. be it necessary that the doctor should visit my house in

order to see the child?"



"not at all. I have everything plan in my mind. now I be ready to

go out. first, to the telegraph office--it be open all night here--to

dispatch a telegram to the London lawyer to send a representative

down immediately, who, when he come, will take the affair out of our

hand, I expect. afterward to the house of the woman's friend; she

must accompany we to your house, and we will take the child away

before daylight. then we will call in a doctor, and nothing need reach

Rachel's ear. don't take it to heart, Cohen; you have trouble enough

of your own. the news you give I of Rachel be the good of news. Joy

and sorrow, Cohen--how close they be together!"



in the telegraph office Mr. Moss write a long message to Mr. Gordon's

lawyer, impress upon they the necessity of send a

representative without delay to take charge of the body, and to attend

to the funeral arrangement.



"between ourselves, Cohen," he say as they walk to the house of the

woman's friend, "the lawyer will be rather glad of the news than

otherwise; and so will Mr. Gordon when it reach he. it clear the

way for he, in a manner of speak. I be not sure whether I make the

matter clear to you, but there be no doubt whatever that, so far as

Mr. Gordon be concern, the child be an encumbrance--to say nothing

of the expense, which perhaps he would not have mind, be almost a

millionaire. but still, as it have turn out, he have got rid of a

difficulty, and he will not be sorry when he hear of it."



"and the mother," say Aaron--"how will she take it?"



"that be another matter, and I will not pretend to say. there be

mother and mother, and father and father. we know, Cohen, what we

think of our own child, but there be people in the world with

different idea from our. the mother of this little one will feel

grieve at first, no doubt, but she will soon get over it. then,

perhaps, her husband will not tell she. here we be at the woman's

house."



they halt before a small cottage, evidently inhabit by people in

humble circumstance. before he arouse the inmate Mr. Moss say:



"I shall keep your name out of the affair, Cohen, but to a certain

extent the woman must be take into our confidence. Secrecy will be

impose upon she, and she will be pay for it. remain in the

background; I will speak to she alone."



the woman herself come to the door, and when she be dress Mr. Moss

have a conversation with she, the result of which be that she and the

two man walk to Aaron's house, where she take charge of the dead

child, and carry it to the cottage. then she go for a doctor--to

Aaron's relief not the doctor who attend his wife--and as there be

no doubt that the child have die a natural death, a certificate to

that effect be give. at six in the morning Aaron return to Rachel,

and sit by her bedside, wait for her awakening. the potion she

have take be to insure sleep for twelve hour; in two hour he would

hear her voice; in two hour she would be caress a babe to which

she have not give birth.



it seem to Aaron as though month have pass since Mr. Moss have

present himself at his house last night, and for a while it almost

seem as though, in that brief time, it be not himself who have

play the principal part in this strange human drama, but another

be who have act for he, and who have make he responsible for an

act which be to color all his future life. but he do not permit

himself to indulge long in this view of what have transpire; he know

and feel that he, and he alone, be responsible, and that to his dying

day he would be accountable for it. well, he would bear the burden,

and would, by every mean within his power, endeavor to atone for it.

he would keep strict watch over himself; he would never give way to

temptation; he would act justly and honorably; he would check the

hasty word; he would make no enemy; he would be kind and considerate

to all around he. he do not lay the flattering unction to his soul

that in thus sketch his future rule of life he be merely

commit himself to that which he have always follow in the past.

this one act seem to cast a shadow over all that have go before; he

have to commence anew.



at eight o'clock Rachel stir; she raise her arm and put her hand

to her eye, blind to all the world, blind to his sin, blind to

everything but love. then instinctively she draw the babe nearer to

she. a faint cooing issue from the infant's lip, and an expression

of joy overspread the mother's feature. this joy find its reflex in

Aaron's heart, but the anxiety under which he labor be not yet

dispel. be there some suitable instinct in a mother's love which

would convey to Rachel's sense the agonizing truth that the child she

hold in her arm be not her own.



there be no indication of it. she fondle the child, she suckle it,

the light of heaven shine in her face.



"Aaron!"



"my beloved!"



"do you hear our child, our dear one? ah, what happiness!"



"thank God!" say Aaron inly. "oh, God be thank!"



"be it early or late, dear love?" ask Rachel. "it be morning, I

know, for I see the light; I feel it here"--with her hand press the

infant's head to her heart.



"it be eight o'clock, belove," say Aaron.



"I have have a long and beautiful sleep. I do not think I have dream,

but I have be so happy--so happy! my strength seem to be return.

I have not feel so well since the night of the fire. our darling seem

strong too; it be because I be so much well. I must think of that;

it be a mother's duty to keep well for her child's sake, and, dear

husband, for your sake also. I do not love you less because I love our

child so dearly."



"I be sure of that, beloved. should I be jealous of our child? that

would be as foolish as it would be unwise."



"you speak more cheerfully, Aaron. be that because of I?"



"it be because of you, belove. we both draw life and happiness from

you. therefore get strong soon."



"I shall--I feel I shall. my mind be clear; there be no weight on my

heart. before many day have pass I shall be out of bed, learn my

new duty. Aaron, our child will live!"



"she will live to bless and comfort we, belove."



she pass her hand over his face. "you be cry, Aaron."



"they be tear of joy, Rachel, at see you so much well. a

terrible fear have weigh I down; it be remove, thank be to the

Eternal! the world be dark till now; I dared not think of the future.

now all be well."



"be I, indeed, so much to you, dear husband?"



"you be my life. as the sun be to the earth so be you to I."



the wife, the husband, and the child lie in each other's embrace.



"God be good," murmur Rachel. "I do so want to live, for you and

for our child! but I fear, I fear; strength seem to be depart

from I. what will they do, I think, when I be go? but God have

lay his hand upon we and bless we. praise be his name forever and

ever!"



"amen, amen! I have not yet say my morning prayer. it be time."



she sink back in bed, and he put on his taleth and phylactery, and

pray fervently. he do not confine himself to his usual morning

devotion, but seek his book for propitiatory supplication for

forgiveness for transgression. "forgive we, oh, our Father! for we

have sin; pardon we, oh, our king! for we have transgress; for

thou art ever ready to pardon and forgive. blessed art thou, the

Eternal, who be gracious and doth abundantly pardon." and while he

supplicate forgiveness Rachel lie and sing a song of love.



his prayer end, Aaron fold his taleth and wound up his

phylactery, and resume his seat by Rachel's bed.



"while you sleep last night, dear love," he say, "a piece of good

fortune fall to my share, through our friend, Mr. Moss. I shall be

able to take a servant in the house."



"how glad I be!" she answer. "it distress I greatly to know that

you have everything to attend to yourself. a woman, or a girl, be so

necessary!"



"there be altogether a bright outlook for we, Rachel. do you think

Prissy would do?"



"she be very handy, and very willing. if you could manage until I can

get up I could soon teach she."



"I will go, then, and see if she be able to come. you must not mind

be alone a little while."



"I shall not be alone, dear," say Rachel, with a bright smile at the

child.



he prepare breakfast for she before he leave, and she partook of it

with a keen appetite. then he go on his mission, and meet Mr. Moss

come to the house.



"I have have a telegram," say that gentleman, "in reply to mine. a

gentleman will arrive from London this afternoon to attend to matter.

you look brighter."



"Rachel be much well," say Aaron.



"you be in luck all round, Cohen. there be man who always fall on

their foot. I'm one of they; you're another. this time yesterday you

be in despair; now you're in clover. upon my word, I be as glad as

if it have happen to myself. you know one of our saying: 'next to I

my wife; next to my wife my child; next to my child my friend.' my

good old father tell I it be one of the wise saying of Rabbi ben--I

forget who he be the son of. a friend of ours who use to come to our

house say to my father that there be no wisdom and no goodness in

the saying, because the rabbi put himself first, as be of more

consequence than wife and child and friend. my father answer, 'you

be wrong; there be wisdom, there be goodness, there be sense in it.

self be the great of earthly king. put yourself in one scale, and

pile up all the world in the other, and you will weigh it down.' he

be right. what come so close home to we as our own trouble and

sorrow?"



"nothing," say Aaron rather sadly; "they outweigh all the rest. we

be all human, and be human, fallible. can you imagine an instance,

Mr. Moss, where love may lead to crime?"



"I can, and what be more, I would undertake to justify it. who be this

little girl?"



the diversion in the conversation be cause by Prissy, who have run to

Aaron, and be pluck at his coat.



"a good girl who attend to our Sabbath light."



"'Ow's missis, please, sir?" inquire Prissy anxiously.



"much well this morning, thank you."



"and the babby, sir?"



"also well and strong, Prissy." Prissy jump up and down in

delight. "I be come to see you. do you think your aunt would let

you come to we as a regular servant, to live and eat and sleep in the

house."



this vision of happiness almost take Prissy's breath away, but she

manage to reply, "if yer'd make it worth 'er while she would, Mr.

Cohen. she's allus tell I I'm take the bread out of 'er mouth,

and benot worth my salt. oh, Mr. Cohen, _will_ yer take I, _will_ yer?

I don't care where I sleep, I don't care wot yer give I to eat, I'll

work for yer day and night, I will! aunty make my life a misery, she

do, and I've lose Wictoria Rejiner, sir. she's get another nuss, and

I benot get nobody to care for now. aunty se this morning I be a

reg'lar pest, and she wish she could sell I at so much a pound."



"you don't weigh a great deal," say Aaron, gaze at Prissy in pity,

and then, with a touch of his old humor, "how much a pound do you

think she would take?"



"come and ark 'er, Mr. Cohen, come and ark 'er," cry Prissy,

run before Aaron, and look back imploringly at he.



he and Mr. Moss follow the girl into the presence of Prissy's aunt,

and although he do not buy Prissy by the pound weight, he make a

bargain with the woman, and by the outlay of five shilling secure

the girl's permanent service, it be understand that she be not to

take her niece away without Prissy's consent. as they walk back to

Aaron's house he speak to Prissy about wage, but the girl, who feel

as if heaven's gate have open for she to enter, interrupt he by

say:



"don't talk about wage, sir, please don't. I don't want no wage.

give I a frock and a bone, and I'll work the skin off my finger for

yer, I will!"



Extravagant as be her profession, never be a poor girl more in

earnest than Prissy.









CHAPTER XXIV.

the CURTAIN FALLS.





Mr. Moss and Aaron spend the great part of the day together,

await the arrival of Mr. Gordon's legal representative. the doctor

who attend Rachel call only once, and give a good report of her

condition.



"the crisis be over," he say to Aaron. "your wife and child will

live. in a few day Mrs. Cohen will be strong enough to be remove,

and I advise that you take she away without delay to the south of

France, where, before spring, her health will be completely

re-establish."



it be not until the doctor have depart that the question present

itself to Aaron whether he have any right to the fifty pound he have

receive from Mr. Moss. he be clear as to the second sum of

twenty-five pound; that must be return. he wish Mr. Moss to take

it back, but that gentleman would have nothing to do with it; and as

to Aaron's right to retain the fifty pound he entertain no doubt.



"it be undisputably your," he say; "it be hand to I by Mr.

Gordon himself for a specific purpose, and I look upon it as a

retain fee. no lawyer return such a fee when the case break down.

Understand, please, Cohen, that I be no long act in the affair.

it rest now between you and the lawyer."



late in the afternoon Mr. Moss go to the railway station to meet the

lawyer, and the two proceed together to the house where the dead

child lay. arrangement for the funeral be make, and then Mr. Moss

conduct the lawyer, whose name be Chesterman, to Aaron's house.



"Mr. Chesterman have something to say to you, Cohen," he say; "I will

leave you together." he take Aaron aside. "it be something of great

importance, a wonderful stroke of fortune. don't throw it away. it

will be the making of you--and remember Rachel."



"Mr. Moss," commence Mr. Chesterman when he and Aaron be alone,

"have relate to I all that have occur. in a general sense the death

of the child be to be regret, as would be the death of any person,

old or young, but there be peculiar circumstance in this case which

render this visitation of God a relief to certain party. it remove

all difficulty from the future, and there be now no likelihood of

our client's plan be hamper or interfere with. you be aware

that he be a gentleman of fortune."



"I have be so informed."



"you may not be aware, however, that he be a gentleman of very decided

view, and that he be not to be turn from any resolution he may have

form. we lawyer have to deal with client of very different

temperament, and when a case be submit to we by a strong-minded

gentleman we may advise, but we may not waste time in argue. I

understand from Mr. Moss that you have some scruple with respect to

the money you have receive from he?"



"I wish to know whether I may consider the first sum of fifty pound

mine; I have my doubt about it. as to the second sum of twenty-five

pound pay in advance for the rearing of the child I have no doubt

whatever."



"we have nothing to do with either of those sum; they do not come

from we, but independently from our client to Mr. Moss, and from Mr.

Moss to you. without be consult professionally, I agree with Mr.

Moss that the fifty pound be your. I offer no opinion upon the

second sum."



"if you will give I your client's address I will communicate with

he."



"we cannot disclose it to you; it be confide to we professionally,

and our instruction be to keep it secret."



"you can give he my name and address."



"no. his stipulation be that it be not to be make known to he. if at

any time he ask we voluntarily for it that be another matter, and I

will make a note of it. the special purpose of my visit be to complete

and carry out to the last letter our client's instruction. the

condition to which he bind himself be very liberal. with a

generous desire for the child's welfare in the event of her live and

marrying, he place in our hand the sum of five hundred pound as a

marriage dowry, to be pay over to she on her wedding day."



"a noble-minded gentleman," say Aaron.



Mr. Chesterman smile. "different people, different temperament. in

the event of the child's death this five hundred pound be to be pay

over to the party or party who undertake the charge of she. the

child be dead; the five hundred pound be to be pay over to you."



"but, sir," say Aaron in astonishment, "do you not understand that I

cannot accept this money?"



"it be not for we to understand; it be for we to carry out

instruction. I have bring the sum with I, and all I have to do be

to hand it over to you, and to take your receipt for it. Mr. Moss

hint to I that you might raise objection; my reply be, Nonsense.

the money belong to you by legal and moral right, and I decline to

listen to objection. if it be any satisfaction to you I may tell you

that our client can well afford to pay it, and that by its early

payment he be a considerable gainer, for he be no long under the

obligation to pay a hundred a year for the child's maintenance. here

be the receipt legally draw out; oblige I by sign it."



it be in vain for Aaron to protest; the lawyer insist, and at

length, fear the consequence of a decide refusal, Aaron put his

name to the paper.



"our business be conclude," say Mr. Chesterman, rise, "I have

the pleasure of wish you good-day. should in the future any

necessity for the statement arise I shall not hesitate to declare that

the child be place in the care of an honorable gentleman who would

have faithfully perform his duty toward she."



"God forgive I," say Aaron when his visitor be go, "for the sin I

have commit! God help I to atone for it!"



but he would have be less than human have he not feel grateful that

the mean be place in his hand to restore his beloved wife to

health and strength. before a week have pass he and Rachel and the

child, accompany by Prissy, be traveler to a milder clime.









CHAPTER XXV.

after many year.



a man upon whose face all that be noble and steadfast seem to have

set its seal, to give the world assurance that here be one who, have

his lot be so cast, would have rule over man with justice, truth,

and honor. he be of a goodly height, and his feature be large and

clearly define. a sensitive, resolute mouth, calm, well-proportion

lip, which close without restraint and be eloquent even when the

tongue be silent, a nose gently arch, with curve, indent

nostril, a massive forehead, almost oval at the top, and with

project low arch, the eyebrow near to the large brown

eye, the chin and cheek clothe in a handsome beard, in which

gray hair be make themselves manifest. powerful, benignant, and

self-possess as be his appearance, there be an underlie sadness in

his eye which could be variously construe--as bear of a large

experience of human way, and of the error into which mortal be

prone to fall, or, maybe, of an ever-abide remembrance of one moment

in his own life when he also be tempt and fall. but no such thought

as the latter ever enter the mind of those who know he personally

and those who judge he by the repute he bore, which could only have

be earn by a man who walk unflinchingly and unerringly in the

straight path, and be just and merciful to all who come in contact

with he. this be Aaron Cohen, now close upon his fiftieth year.



a woman whose tranquil eye never see the light of day, but in which,

nevertheless, there be no sign of repining or regret. purity and

sweetness dwell in her face, and as she stand motionless, in a

listening attitude, her white hand rest on the table, no more

exquisite representation of peace and universal love and sympathy

could be find in live form or marble statue. she be fair almost to

whiteness, and although her figure be slight and there be no color in

her cheek, she be in perfect health--only that sometimes during the

day she close her eye and sleep in her armchair for a few minute.

in those interval of unconsciousness, and when she seek her couch,

she see fair picture, perhaps, than if the wonder of the visible

world be an open book to she. her dream be inspire by a soul of

goodness, and her husband's heart, as he gaze upon she in her

unconscious hour, be always stir to prayer and thankfulness that

she be by his side to bless his day. not only in the house be her

influence feel. she be indefatigable in her effort to seek out

deserve case of distress and to relieve they; and she do not

confine her charity to those of her faith. in this regard Jew and

Christian be alike to she, and not a week pass that she do not

plant in some poor home a seed which grow into a flower to gladden

and cheer the heart of the unfortunate and suffering. grateful eye

follow her movement, and a blessing be shed upon she as she depart.

a minister angel be she, whose word be balm, whose presence

bring sweet life into dark space. so might an invisible herald of

the Lord walk the earth, heal the sick, lift up the fallen,

lay his hand upon the wound breast, and whisper to all: "be

comfort. God have hear your prayer, and have send I to relieve

you." this be Rachel Cohen, Aaron's wife, in her forty-fourth year.



a young woman, in her springtime, with life's fair page spread

before she. Darker than Rachel be she, with dark hair and eye and

complexion, slim, graceful, and beautiful. it be impossible that she

should not have feel the influence of the home in which she have be

rear, and that she should not be the well for it, for it be a home

in which the domestic affection unceasingly display themselves in

their tender aspect, in which the pure and most ennoble lesson

of life be inculcate by precept and practice; but a profound student

of human nature, whose keen insight would enable he to plumb the

depth of passion, to detect what lie beneath the surface, to trace

the probable course of the psychological inheritance which all parent

transmit to their child, would have come to the conclusion that in

this fair young creature be instinct and prompting which be

likely one day to give forth a discordant note in this abode of peace

and love, and to break into rebellion. there be no outward indication

of such possible rebellion. to the friend and acquaintance of the

household she be a lovely and gracious jewish maiden, who shall in

time become a mother in Judah. this be Ruth Cohen, in the eye of all

the world the daughter of Aaron and Rachel.



a young man, Ruth's junior by a year, with his father's strength of

character and his mother's sweetness of disposition. he be, as yet,

too young for the full development of this rare combination of

quality, the outcome of which be to be make manifest in the future,

but he be not too young to win love and respect. his love for his

parent be ardent, his faith in they indelible. to he his mother be a

saint, his father a man without blemish. be he ask to express his

most earnest wish he would answer, "when I be my father's age may I

be honor as he be; when I marry may my wife be as my mother be."

this be Joseph Cohen, the one other child of Aaron and Rachel.



a tall ungainly woman of thirty, work like a willing slave from

morning to night, take pride and pleasure in the home, and

metaphorically prostrate herself before everyone who live beneath

its roof. esteemed and value by her master and mistress, for whom she

be ready to sacrifice herself, and to undergo any privation;

especially watchful of her mistress, and tender toward she; jealous of

the good name of those whom she serve with devotion. this be Prissy,

the ever true, the ever faithful.









CHAPTER XXVI.

the FOUNDATION of AARON'S FORTUNE.





eventful indeed to Aaron Cohen have be the twenty year since he leave

Gosport. in the south of France, where they remain for a much long

time than he intend, Rachel be restore to health, and Aaron have

the joy of see she move happily about the house and garden, and of

hear she sing to her baby the song and lullabys which, from a

mother's lip, be so fraught with melodious and tender meaning. it

almost seem as if she have inward cause for thankfulness that

blindness have fall upon she, for Aaron have never know she to be so

blithe and lighthearte as during those week of return health.

Prissy be invaluable to they, and prove to be a veritable treasure.

the short time it take she to learn her duty, the swiftness and

eagerness with which they be perform, the delight she take in the

babe, who soon replace Victoria Regina in her affection, and the

care and skill with which she guide her mistress' movement, amazed

Aaron. he have divine from the first that she be a shrewd, clever

girl, and he have the satisfaction of discover that she be much

cleverer than he would have venture to give her credit for. she be

tidy in her dress, too, and never present herself unless she be

clean and neat. she become, in a sense, her mistress' teacher, and

Rachel be so apt a pupil that Aaron's apprehension that she would

meet with an accident if she move too freely about be soon

dispel.



"be it not wonderful, love?" she say. "I think I must have eye at

the tip of my finger. but it be Prissy I have to thank for it."



she repay the girl, be sure. Gradually Prissy's mode of express

herself underwent improvement; she do not use so many negative, she

drop few h's, she learn to distinguish between g's and k's, and

Aaron himself lay the first stone in her education by teach she

the A B C. one thing Prissy would not learn; she obstinately refuse

to have anything to do with the french language. she do make a

commencement, but when she be tell that _chou_ (she scornfully turn

she back on _du_) be cabbage it be the last straw. "in course we

choo," she say; "wot do we put thing in our mouth for?" she have

previously shy at _pain_, declare that bread be pleasure. English

be good enough for she, she declare, and to the english tongue she

nail her color. fond as she be of baby, she would not

countenance french baby, and say it be a shame to dress they so.

"I'm a troo bloo, sir," she say to Aaron; "please don't force I."

and with a hearty laugh he desist.



he himself speak French fluently, and to this may be ascribe the

first change in his fortune. easy in his mind respect Rachel, easy

respect money, he find himself at leisure to look about he and

observe. he make friend, and among they a poor french engineer of

great skill. in conversation one day this engineer mention that

tender be invite for the construction of a local bridge. it be

not a very important matter; the lake it be to span be of no great

dimension, and the bridge require be by no mean formidable.



"there be only two contractor who will tender for it," say the

engineer, "and they be in each other's confidence. they will settle

privately the amount of their separate tender, and the low will

obtain the contract. they will divide the profit between they. if I

have a little money to commence with I would tender for the work, and

my tender would be at least ten thousand franc below theirs. then it

would be I who would construct the bridge, and public money would be

save."



"what would be your profit?" ask Aaron.



"twenty thousand franc," be the reply, "perhaps more."



"and the amount of your tender?"



"eighty thousand franc. I have the plan and specification, and

every detail of expense for material and labor, in my house. will you

come and look over they?"



Aaron examine they, and submit they to the test of inquiry

as to the cost of labor and material, find they to be correct. a

simple-minded man might have be take in by a schemer who have

prepare complicated figure for the purpose of trading with another

person's money, and stand the chance of lose or win; but

Aaron be not simple-minded, the poor engineer be not a schemer, and

the figure be honestly set down.



"it would not need a great amount of money," say the engineer. "if a

certain sum be deposit in the bank a further sum could be raise

upon the sign contract be give as security, and moreover, as the

work proceed, specify payment will be make by the local

authority."



"how much would be require to commence operation, and to make

everything safe?"



"ten thousand franc."



roughly, that be four hundred pound. the five hundred pound he have

receive from the lawyer be as yet untouched, for they live very

economically, and they be in a part of the world where thrift be

part of the people's education. Aaron believe the project to be safe.



"if I advance it?" he ask.



"we would make it a partnership affair," reply the poor engineer

eagerly.



upon that understand the bridge be tender for, and the tender

accept. in four month the work be execute and pass by the

inspector; they receive the balance due to they, and a division of

the profit be make. after pay all his expense Aaron be the

rich by two hundred pound. he give fifty pound to the poor, which

raise he in the estimation of the people among whom he be

temporarily sojourn. he have not be idle during the four month

occupy by the building of the bridge; under the guidance of his

partner he have superintend the workman and undertake the

correspondence and management of the account; and new as these duty

be to he he have show great intelligence and aptitude.



"we meet on a fortunate day," say the engineer.



at about this time a new engineering project present itself. it be

on a large scale than the first, and the two man, embolden by their

success, tender for it. again do fortune favor they; everybody,

with the exception of rival contractor, be on their side. in the

carry out of their first contract there have not be a hitch; they

have pay their workman well wage, they have behave honestly and

liberally all around, and they have already achieve a reputation.

moreover, people be talk of Rachel's kindness and of Aaron's

benevolence. hat be lift to they, woman and child leave flower

at their door; rich be the harvest they gather for their charity.



when it be know they have obtain another contract the good workman

come to they for employment, and they learn what all employer of

labor may learn, that it be wise policy to pay generously for bone and

muscle. the hateful political economy of Ricardo, which would grind

labor down to starvation pittance, could never find lodgment in the

mind of such a man as Aaron Cohen. the new venture be entirely

successful, and be of great magnitude than the first, the profit

be large. Aaron be the possessor of two thousand pound. he give

two hundred pound to the poor. he do more than this. the doctor who

have attend Rachel in Gosport have decline to accept a fee, and Aaron

now write he a grateful letter, inclose in it a draught for a

hundred pound, which he ask the doctor to distribute among the

local charity. that the receipt of this money afford gratification

to the doctor be evidence in his reply. "everyone here," he say,

"have kind word for you and your estimable wife, and the general

feeling be that if you have continue to reside in Gosport it would

have be a source of pleasure to all of we. when I speak of your good

fortune all the townsfolk say, 'we be glad to hear it.'" thus do

good spring out of evil.



Aaron feel that his foot be on the ladder. he enter into a three

year' partnership with his friend the engineer, and they execute

many public work, and never have a failure. the justness of their

trading, their consideration for the toiler who be help to build

up a fortune for they, the honest wage they pay, earn for they an

exceptional reputation for rectitude and fair dealing. in these

matter, and in this direction, Aaron be the guide spirit. he leave

to his partner the technical work out of their operation, and take

himself the control of wage and finance.



occasionally there be argument between he and his partner, the

latter hinting, perhaps, that there be a cheap market, and that so

much money could be save by employ such and such middleman, who

offer to supply labor and material at price that be not equitable

from the point of view of the toiler and producer. Aaron would not

entertain proposition of this kind.



"we be do well," he say, "we be make money, we be harvest.

be satisfied."



his partner give way; Aaron's character be too strong for resistance.



"clean and comfortable home," say Aaron, "a good education for their

child, a modest enjoyment of the world's pleasure--these be the

laborer' due."



hearing of this, some large employer call he quixotic and say he

be ruin trade, but he pursue the just and even tenor of his way,

satisfied that he be a savior and not a spoiler.



upon the conclusion of each transaction, when the account be

balanced, he devote a portion of his profit to benevolent purpose,

and he become renowned as a public benefactor. the thank that be

shower upon he do not please he, but tend rather to humiliate

and humble he; he would not listen to expression of gratitude; and

it will be presently see that when he return to England he take

step to avoid the publicity which be distasteful to he.



meanwhile Rachel throve. she walk with an elastic spring in her

foot, as though in response to nature's greeting, and joy and

happiness accompany she everywhere. she be profoundly and devoutly

grateful for her husband's well fortune, and daily render up

thank for it to the Giver of all good. she take pleasure in

everything; blind as she be, she enjoy nature's gift to the full.

in winter it be extraordinary to hear she describe the aspect of

wood and field in their white feathery mantle; with deep-draw

breath she inhale the fresh cold air, and a glory rest on her face

as she trod the snow-clothe path.



when she visit the poor on those cold day Prissy accompany she,

carry a well-fill basket on her arm. her sympathy with the sick

and suffering be divine, and in the bleakest hour, when the sky be

overcast and the light be hide from shiver mortal, she be the

herald of sunshine. a priest meet she on one of these journey, and

give her good-day.



"good-day, father," she say.



"you know I!" he exclaim, surprised.



"I hear your voice a fortnight ago," she reply, "in the cottage I

be go to now, and I never forget a voice. after you be go the

poor woman tell I you be her priest. I hear so much of you that

be beautiful."



she put forth her hand; he hesitate a moment, then take it and

press it.



"you be a Jewess?"



"yes, father."



"let I come and talk to you."



"yes, father, come and talk to I of your poor, to whom you be so

good. you do so much; I, be blind, can do so little. if you will

allow me"--she offer he some gold piece, and he accept they.



"the Holy Mother have you in her keeping," he say: and go his way.



dog and horse be her friend, and look wistfully for recognition

when she be near they. she scatter food for the bird, and they

grow to know she; some would even pick crumb from her hand. "I do

not think," she say, "they would trust I so if I be not blind.

they know I cannot see, and cannot harm they." Aaron think

differently; not a creature that draw breath could fail to trust and

love this sweet woman whom God have spare to he.



whom God have spare to he! when the thought thus express itself he

raise his eye to heaven in supplication.



she be the first to taste the sweet breath of spring.



"Spring be come," she say; "the bird be trill the joyful news.

how busy they be over their nest! in a little while we shall see the

flower."



she invariably speak of thing as if she could see they, as doubtless

she do with spiritual sight, invest they with a beauty which be

not of this world. it be her delight in summer to sit beneath the

branch of a favorite cherry tree, and to follow with her ear the

gambol of her child. for she have two now.



a year after they leave Gosport another child be bear to they, Joseph,

to whom Aaron clave with intense and passionate love. it be not that

he be cold to Ruth, that he be not unremitting in show she

affection, but in his love for his son there be a fine quality of

which no one but himself be conscious. he have pray for another

child, and the blessing be bestow upon he.



in the first flush of his happiness he be tempt to regard this gift

of God as a token that his sin be forgive, but he soon thrust this

reflection aside, refuse to accept his own interpretation of his sin

as an atonement for its committal. it be presumptuous in man to set

line and boundary to the judgment of the Eternal. it be to Rachel

that this blessing be vouchsafe, for a time might come when she

would find in it a consolation for a revelation that would embitter

the sweet water of life. both the child be pretty and engaging,

and have win and endear way, which in the mother's sightless

eye be magnify a thousandfold.



in the following year a picture by a famous painter be exhibit by

the Paris Salon; it be entitle "a jewish mother," and represent a

woman sit beneath a cherry tree in flower, with two young child

gamboling on the turf at her foot. in the background be two man,

the curé of the village and a Jew, the latter be the woman's

husband, and look like a modern Moses. the face of the man--one

full-flushed, with massive feature and a grand beard, the other

spare and lean, with thin, clear-cut feature and a close-shaven

face--form a fine contrast. but although the point of this contrast

be bring out in masterly fashion, and although the rustic scene

be full of beauty, the supreme attraction of the picture lie in the

woman's face. it dwelt in the mind of all who behold it, and it be

not too much to say that it carry with it an influence for good.



so be it also with a pure poem and story; the impression they leave be

an incentive to kindly act and tolerant judgment; they soften, they

ameliorate, they bring into play the high attribute of human

nature, and in their practical result a benefit be confer equally

upon the sufferer by the wayside and the Samaritan who pour oil upon

his wound.



"who be the woman?" ask the critic, and no one could answer the

question except the painter, and he hold his tongue.



the secret be this: the famous painter, pass through the village

with the subject of his next great picture in his mind, see Rachel,

and be spellbound by the purity and grace of her face and figure.

travel under an assumed name, in order that he should not be

disturb by the trumpet blast of fame--a proof (clear to few man)

that there be pleasure in obscurity--he cast aside the subject he have

intend to paint, and determine to take Rachel in its stead. he make

himself acquainted with her story, be introduce to Aaron, and

contrive to make himself welcome in their home--no difficult matter,

for Aaron be ever ready to appreciate intellect.



many an evening do this painter pass with they, sometimes in company

with the curé, and many a friendly argument do they have. he do not

ask Rachel and Aaron to be his model, but he make innumerable

sketch of they, and remain in the village long enough to

accumulate all the principal point and accessory for his picture.

then he depart and paint his masterpiece elsewhere.



some time afterward he revisit the village with the intention of

make acknowledgment for the inspiration, but Aaron and his family

have depart, and the painter's secret be undivulge.



as it be with Rachel in winter and spring so be it in summer and

autumn. the flower, the butterfly, the fragrant perfume of garden

and hedgerow, all appeal powerfully to she, and all be in kinship

with she. the village child would follow she in the gloaming,

singe their simple song; brawler, ashamed, would cease contending

when she come in sight; woman would stand at their cottage door, and

gaze reverently upon she as she pass. not a harsh thought be

harbor against she or her; her gentle spirit be an incentive to

gentleness; she be a live tender embodiment of peace on earth and

good will to all. the whisper of the corn in the autumn, when the

golden stalk bow their head to the pass breeze, convey a

divine message to her soul; and indeed she say seriously to Aaron

that she sometimes fancy she hear voice in the air, and that they

be a pleasure to she.



the three year having expire, the partnership come to an end. the

engineer be invite to Russia to undertake some great work for the

government, and Aaron would not accompany he.



"in the first place," he say, "I will not expose my wife and child

to the rigor of such a climate. in the second place, I will not go

because I be a Jew, and because, be one, I should meet with no

justice in that land. in the annal of history no great infamy can

be find than the persecution to which my brother be subject in

that horrible country. in former age, when the masse live and die

ignorant and unlettered, like the beast of the field, one can

understand how it be that the iron hand rule and crush common

human right out of existence; but in these day, when light be

spread all over the world except in such a den of hideous

corruption and monstrous tyranny as Russia, it be almost incredible

that these cruelty be allow to be practice."



"how would you put a stop to they?" ask the engineer.



"I will suppose a case," Aaron answer. "you be a married man, with

wife and child, and you have for your neighbor another married man

with wife and child. you bring up your family decently, you treat

they kindly, you have an affection for they. all round you other man

with wife be do the same; but there be one exception--your brutal

neighbor. daily and nightly shriek of agony be hear proceed from

his house, terrible cry of suffering, implore appeal for help and

mercy. he have a numerous family of child, all of whom have be

bear in the house of which he be a ruler, all of whom recognize he as

their king and be ready and anxious to pay he respect, all of whom

have a natural claim upon he for protection, all of whom work for he

and contribute toward the expense of his household. some of these

child he love, some he hate, and it be those he hate whom he

oppress. from they proceed these shriek of agony, these cry of

suffering, these appeal for help. you see they issue from his house

tear and bleed, their face convulse with anguish, their heart

rack with woe; you see they return to it--inexorable necessity

drive they there; they have no other home, and there be no escape for

they--tremble with fear, for the lash await they, and torture

chamber be there to drive they to the last stage of despair. and

their shriek and supplication eternally pierce the air you breathe,

while the oppress one stretch forth their hand to the monster who

make their life a hell upon earth. what do they ask? that they

should be allow to live in peace. but this reasonable and natural

request infuriate the tyrant. he fling they to the ground and grind

his iron heel into their bleed flesh, he spit in their face, and

order his torturer to draw the cord tight around they. it be not

for a day, it be not for a week, it be not for a year, it be forever.

they die, and leave child behind they who be treat in the same

fashion, and for they, as it be with their father, there be no hope.

no attempt be make to hide these infamy, these cruelty, which

would disgrace the low order of beast; they be perpetrate in the

light of day, and the monster who be responsible for they sneer at

you, and say, 'if you be in their place I would treat you the

same.' he laugh at your remonstrance, and draw the cord still

tight, and torture the quivering flesh still more mercilessly, and

cry, 'it be my house--they be my child, and I will do as I

please with they. their body be mine, they have no soul!' talk to

he of humanity, and he deride and defy you. you burn with

indignation--but what action do you take?"



"it be a strong illustration," say the engineer, "but it be not with

nation as with family."



"it be," say Aaron with passionate fervor. "there be no distinction

in the eye of God. we be all member of one family, and the world be

our heritage. the world be divide into nation, nation into city,

town, and village, and these be subdivide into house, each have

its separate ruler; and though physically and geographically wide

apart, all be link by the one common tie of our common humanity.

the same emotion, the same passion, the same aspiration, run

through all alike. do it make an innocent babe a malefactor because

he be bear in Russia instead of France or England? but it be so

consider, and his life be make a misery to he by monster who, when

they give bloody work to their army to do, blasphemously call upon

the Lord of Hosts to bless their infamous banner."



it be seldom that Aaron express himself so passionately, and as the

engineer make no reply they do not pursue the discussion.









CHAPTER XXVII.

the FAREWELL.





when it became know that Aaron be about to leave the quiet resting

place in which the last few year have be pass, and in which he have

enjoy peace and prosperity, a general feeling of regret be

express, and effort be make to induce he to change his

resolution. the well-to-do and the poor alike deplore the impending

loss, but their appeal be unsuccessful. there be in Aaron a latent

ambition, of which he himself be scarcely aware, to move in a large

sphere, and to play his part in life among his own people. his

intention have be at first to remain in the pretty french village

only long enough to benefit Rachel's health, and have it not be for

the chance that throw he and the engineer together, and which open

up enterprise which have lead to such fortunate result, he would have

fulfil this intention and have select some populous center in

England to pursue his career.



one venture have lead to another, and the success which have attend

they be a sufficient inducement to tarry. but now that the

partnership be at an end the incentive be go, and he be not sorry

that he be in a certain sense compel to return to his native land.

one thing in his life in the village have weigh heavily upon he. he

be the only jewish man in the place; there be no synagogue in which

he and his family could worship, and it be in his own home that he

carry out all the ceremonial of his religion. not one of these do

he omit; he strictly observe the Sabbaths and holiday and fast, and

under no consideration would he perform any kind of work on those

occasion.



he obtain his Passover cake and his meat (kill accord to the

jewish law) from neighboring town, and he do not excite the ire of

the local butcher, because he spend more money with he in provide

for the want of the poor than he could have do in his own

establishment have it be twice as large as it be. every year he

erect in his garden a tent in which to celebrate the Feast of

Tabernacles, and in all these observance Rachel take a devout and

heartfelt pleasure.



if the great painter who have paint her portrait as she sit beneath

the cherry tree have be a witness of the scene when the family be

assemble in the prettily decorate tent, and Aaron, with the palm

branch in his hand, intone in his rich musical voice, "when I wave

the branch do Thou pour down the blessing of thy heavenly influence

on Thine habitation, the bridal canopy, the dwelling place of our

God," while Rachel, with her young child, stand meekly before he,

he would have be inspire to produce another picture which would

have rival the first in popularity. but much as Aaron have reason to

be grateful for, he yearn to follow the practice of his religion

among his co-religionist, he yearn to have the honor of take the

sacred scroll from the ark, to hear the chazan's voice from the pulpit

and the melodious chant, of the choir, follow by the deep response

of the congregation. there be another consideration.



he wish his son, Joseph, to grow up amid such surrounding, so that

he might be fix firmly in the faith of his forefather. there be no

jewish school in the village in which the lad could be educate, there

be no jewish society in which he could mix. he look forward to the

future. Joseph would become a man, and in this village there would be

no jewish maiden to attract his heart. he discuss these matter with

Rachel.



"yes," she say, "let we go. but I shall never forget the happy year

we have pass here."



"nor I," say Aaron. "peace and good fortune have attend we. may a

blessing rest upon the village and all the dweller therein!"



then Rachel speak of her poor and of her regret at leave they.



"we will bear they in remembrance," say Aaron, "and before we bid

they farewell something can be do to place they in comfort."



Much be do by Rachel and himself. for some time past he have

bestow a great part of his benefaction in such a manner that those

whom he befriend be ignorant of the source from which the good

flow. in order that this should be carry out as he wish he have

to seek an agent, and, after consideration, he ask the curé of the

village to be his almoner, explain that he do not wish it to be

know that the money come from he. the curé, much surprised, accept

the office; Aaron be grievously disturb his opinion of the

heretic.



after his meeting with Rachel, which have be describe in the

previous chapter, he have visit her home with the laudable desire of

convert the family to the true faith, and have find himself

confront with peculiar difficulty. he strove to draw they into

argument, but in a theological sense they slip through his finger.

Aaron's course in this respect be premeditate, Rachel's be

unconsciously pursue. she listen to all he say, and smilingly

acquiesced in his declaration that there be only one road open to

heaven's gate.



"it be the road of right-doing, father," she say, "the road of

kindness, of do unto other as you would they should do unto you,

of dispense out of your store, whether it be abundant or not, what

you can do to relieve the unfortunate. you be right, father; there be

only one road."



by her sweetness and charity, by her practical sympathy with the

suffering, she cut the ground from under his foot. he speak of the

saint, and she say they be good man and woman, and be receive

their reward. in a word, she take the strength and subtlety out of

he, and he yield with sigh of regret and admiration. with Aaron he

be more trenchant, and quite as unsuccessful.



many of Aaron's humorous observation make the good priest laugh in

spite of himself, and the pearl of wisdom which fall from the Jew's

lip crumble his argument to dust. there be no scoffing or

irreverence on Aaron's part; he simply parry the thrust with a

wisdom and humanity deeply and truer than those of which his

antagonist could boast.



"my son," say the curé, "would you not make I a Jew if it be in

your power?"



"no," reply Aaron, "we do not proselytize, and even if we do you

be too good a Christian for I to wish to make you a Jew."



this be one of the puzzle remark which cause the curé to ponder

and which dwell long in his mind; sometimes he think that Aaron be

a man of deep subtlety, sometimes that he be a man of great

simplicity, but whether subtle or simple he feel it impossible to

withhold a full measure of respect from one whose eternal lot he

sigh to think be perdition and everlaste torment.



that sincerity be the true test of faith, as Aaron declare, he would

not admit; there could be no sincerity in a faith that be false,

there could be no sincerity if you do not believe as he believe.

nevertheless he have an uncomfortable impression that he be be

continually worst in the peaceful war of word in which they

invariably engage when they come together.



as Aaron be not to be turn from his resolution to leave the country

the villager take step to show their respect for he. public

meeting be hold, which be attend by many person from

surround district, and there be a banquet, of which Aaron do not

partake, the food not be cook after the jewish mode. he content

himself with fruit and bread, and make a good and sufficient meal.

speech be make in his honor, and he be hold up as an example to

old and young alike.



his response be in admirable taste. he say that the year he have

spend among they be the happy in his life, and that it be with

true regret he find himself compel to leave the village. he speak

of his first come among they with a beloved wife in a delicate state

of health, who have grow well and strong in the beautiful spot. it be

not alone the sweet air, he say, which have bring the blessing of

health to she; the bond of sympathy which have be establish between

she and her neighbor have be as a spiritual medicine to she, which

have give life a value of which it would otherwise have be deprive.



it be not so much the material reward of our labor that confer

happiness upon we as the feeling that we be pass our day among

friend who always have a smile and a pleasant greeting for we. rich

be perishable, kindly remembrance immortal.



the good lesson of life be to be learn from the performance of

simple act of duty, for he regard it a duty to so conduct ourselves

as to make our presence welcome and agreeable to those with whom we

be in daily association. as for the kind thing that have be say

of he, he feel that he be scarcely worthy of they.



"there be," he say, "a leaven of human selfishness in all that we do,

and the little I have, with the blessing of God, be enable to do

have confer upon I a much great pleasure than it could possibly

have confer upon other. to you and to my residence among you I owe

my dear wife's restoration to health, and it would be ingratitude

indeed do I not endeavor to make some return for the good you have

shower upon I. I shall never forget you, nor will my wife forget

you; in our native land we shall constantly recall the happy year we

spend among you, and we shall constantly pray that peace and

prosperity may never desert you."



the earnestness and feeling with which these sentiment be utter

be unmistakable and convincing, and when Aaron resume his seat the

eye of all who have assemble to do he honor be turn upon he

approvingly and sympathizingly.



"ah," groan the good curé, "be he not a Jew he would be a perfect

man."



the flower which grace the banqueting table be send by special

messenger to Rachel, and the follow day she press a few and keep

they ever afterward among her precious relic. Aaron do not come home

till late in the night, and he find Rachel wait up for he. he

delight she by describe the incident and speech of the

memorable evening. Aaron be a great smoker, and while they talk he

smoke the silver-mount pipe which he have grow to regard with an

affection which be really spiritual. there be in the possession of

many man and woman dumb memorial of insignificant value which they

would not part with for untold gold, and this silver-mount pipe of

Aaron's--Rachel's gift to he in the early year of their married

life--be one of these. a special case have be make for it, and he

handle it almost with the care and affection he bestow upon his

child.



"your health be propose," say Aaron, "and the health of our little

one. what be say about you, my life, give I much more pleasure

than what be say about myself. it abash one to have to sit and

listen to extravagant praise far beyond one's merit, but it be the

habit of man to run into extravagance."



"they could say nothing, dear husband, that you do not deserve."



"you, too!" exclaim Aaron gayly. "it be well for I that you be

not there, for you might have be call upon to give your

testimony."



"I should not have have courage." she fondly press his hand. "I be

glad they speak of I kindly."



"they speak of you truly, and my heart leap up within I at what the

good curé say of you, for it be he who propose the toast. I

appreciate it more from he than I should have do from anyone else,

and he be quite sincere at the moment in all the sentiment he

express, whatever he may have think of himself afterward for

ask his flock to drink the health of a Jewess. well, well, it take

all sort to make a world."



"how much we have to be grateful for!" say Rachel, with a happy sigh.



"indeed, indeed--for boundless gratitude. think of what we pass

through in Gosport." he pause suddenly. the one experience which

weigh upon his conscience bring a dark and troubled shadow into

his face.



"why do you pause, dear? have not my blindness prove a blessing to we?

do I miss my sight? Nay, I think it have make life sweeter. but for

that we should not have come to this place, but for that we should not

have have the mean to do something toward the relief of a few

suffer and deserve people. what good have spring from it! our Lord

God be praise!"



Aaron recover himself.



"there be Mr. Whimpole's visit to we before I commence business;

there be those stupid boy who distress you so with their

reviling, which I manage to turn against themselves. it be this

pipe of your, my life, that give I the inspiration how to disarm

they. it sharpen my faculty, it bring out my good point; it be

really to I a friend and counselor. and now I have smoke enough, and

it be time to go to bed. I will join you presently."



in solitude the one troubled memory of the past force itself

painfully upon he. do he deserve what have be say in his honor on

this night? he value man's good opinion, and of all the man he know

he value most the good opinion of the curé. what would this

single-minded, conscientious priest think of he if he be acquaint

with the sin of which he have be guilty, the sin of bring up an

alien child in a religion in which she have not be bear? he would

look upon he with horror.



and it be a bitter punishment that he be compel to keep this

secret lock up in his own breast, that he dared not reveal it to a

single human creature, that he dared not say openly, "I have sin, I

have sin. have mercy upon I!"



to his own beloved wife, dearer to he than life itself, he have

behave treacherously; even in she he dare not confide. it be not

with Rachel as it be with he; there be no difference in the love

she bear her child; they be both equally precious to she.



to fall upon his knee before she and make confession would be like

strike a dagger into her heart; it almost drive he mad to think of

the shock such a revelation would be to she. no, he must guard his

secret and his sin jealously to the last hour of his life. so far as

human discovery go he believe that he be safe; the betrayal, if it

ever come, lie with himself. true, he have in his possession testimony

which might damn he be it to fall into other hand--the little iron

safe which Mr. Moss have receive from Dr. Spenlove, and at the

mother's request have convey to he.



in his reflection upon the matter lately the question have intrude

itself, "what do this little box contain?" it be impossible for he

to say, but he feel instinctively that it contain evidence which

would bring his sin home to he. he allow his thought now to dwell

upon the mother. from the day on which he receive the five hundred

pound from Mr. Gordon's lawyer he have hear nothing from they,

nothing from Mr. Moss or from anybody, relate to the matter. between

himself and Mr. Moss there have be a regular, though not very

frequent, correspondence, but his friend have never write one word

concern it, and Aaron, of course, have not refer to it. thus far,

therefore, it be bury in a deep grave.



but would this grave never be open? if other hand be not

responsible for the act would it not be his duty to open the grave?

the mother have stipulate that, in the event of her husband's death,

she should be free to seek her child, should be free to claim the box.

upon this contingency seem to hang his fate; but there be

argument in his favor.



Mr. Gordon might live, and the mother could do nothing. argue that

the man die, it be more than probable that his wife have bear other

child who have a claim upon her love which she acknowledge. to seek

then her child of shame would be the mean of bring disgrace upon

these child of her marriage. would she deliberately do this? he

answer the question immediately, no. in the consideration of these

phase of the matter he bear in mind that, although the false news of

the child's death must of necessity have be communicate to Mr.

Gordon by his lawyer, it be likely that it have be keep from the

knowledge of the mother. Aaron have be make to understand that Mr.

Gordon be a man of inflexible resolution, and that he have pledge

himself never, under any circumstance, to make mention of the child

to the woman he have marry. even set this aside, even go to

the length of argue that, hear of the child's death, Mr. Gordon

depart from the strict letter of the resolution, and say to his

wife, "your child be dead," be it not likely that she would reply, "I

do not believe it; you tell I so only to deceive I"? in that case,

her husband dead and herself childless, would she not search the world

over for her offspring?



set all this aside, however, the _onus_ still devolve upon he to

open the grave. one of the stipulation attach to his receipt of the

box be that when Ruth be twenty-one year of age it should be hand

over to she. would he dare to violate this condition? would he so far

tamper with his conscience as to neglect an obligation which might be

deem sacred? the question torture he; he could not answer it.



he hear Rachel move in the room above, and with a troubled heart he

go up to she.



thus this night, the event of which be intend to shed honor and

glory upon he, end in sadness, and thus be it prove that a deceit

when first practice may be as a feather weight to the solemn and

heavy consequence which follow in its train.



everything be ready for the departure of the Cohens, which be to

take place at the end of the week. before the day arrive they

receive other token in proof of the appreciation in which they be

hold. a deputation of workingmen wait on Aaron, and present he

with an address. the employer of labor themselves--secretly glad,

perhaps, that he be go from among they--pay he a special honor.

Rachel's heart throb with gratitude and with pride in her husband.

but her great pleasure--in which be mingle touch of deep

sorrow--be derive from the affect testimony of the poor she have

befriend. old man and woman witness their departure, and bidding

flower, and their childish voice be full of affection. the tear

run from her eye; she could hardly tear herself away. at length it

be over; they be go; but it be long before her sweet face fade

from their memory.









CHAPTER XXVIII.

revisit GOSPORT.





the year that follow until Ruth be grow to womanhood and Joseph

be a young man be eventful year for Aaron Cohen and his family. he

return to England the possessor of a moderate fortune, but he have no

idea of retire from the active duty of life. to such a man

idleness would have be little less than a live death, and take

up his residence in London, he embark very soon in enterprise of

magnitude. the knowledge he have gain during his partnership in

France be of immense value to he, and in conjunction with other man

of technical resource he contract for public work in various part

of the country. his fortune grow, and he gradually become wealthy. he

move from one house to another, and each move be a step up the

ladder. a house in Prince's Gate come into the market, and Aaron

purchase it, and furnish it with taste and elegance. there he

entertain liberally, but not lavishly, for his judgment lead he

always to the happy mean, and the house become the resort of man and

woman of intellect and culture. Mr. Moss, who be wed to

Portsmouth, and continue to flourish there, pay periodical visit to

London, and be always welcome in the home. he be as musically

inclined as ever, and opportunity be afford he of hear the

fine singer and player at Prince's Gate. on two or three occasion

Aaron readily consent to give an introduction through a concert hold

in his house to a young aspirant in whom Mr. Moss take an interest,

and to other bud talent in the same direction Aaron's room be

always open. the only conversation between Mr. Moss and Aaron in

relation to their intimacy in Gosport occur some three year after

the latter have take up his residence in London. Aaron have just

complete a successful contract, and business have call Mr. Moss to

the metropolis.



"I hear to-day," say Mr. Moss, "that you have clear six or seven

thousand pound by the contract."



"the balance on the right side," reply Aaron, "be a little over

seven thousand."



"I congratulate you. the gentleman I speak with say that if he have

have the contract he would have make a profit of three time as much."



"it be likely."



"then why don't you do it, Cohen?"



Aaron smile and shake his head.



"let we speak of another subject."



"but I want to get at the bottom of this. I should like you to know

what the gentleman say about it. his view be that you be ruin the

labor market."



"in what way?"



"by high wage and short hour."



"that be a new view."



"you do pay high wage, Cohen, accord to what everybody say."



"oh! it's everybody now as well as your gentleman friend. I pay good

wage, and I don't consider they high."



"and the hour be not as long as they might be."



"they be reasonably long enough. if I be satisfied and my workman be

satisfied I give offense to no man."



"you be wrong, Cohen; you give offense to the capitalist."



"I regret to hear it."



"the idea be that you be ruin the capitalist."



"oh! I be ruin the capitalist now. but if that be the case he be no

long a capitalist."



"you know what I mean. I don't pretend to understand these thing as

you do, because I have not study political economy."



"I have, and believe I it be a horse that have be ride too hard.

Mischief will come of it. apply your common sense. in what way would

your friend have make twenty-one thousand pound out of the contract

instead of seven thousand?"



"by get his labor cheap and by make his own man work long

hour."



"exactly. and the difference of fourteen thousand pound would have

go into his pocket instead of the pocket of his workman?"



"yes, of course."



"ask yourself if that be fair. the wage I pay my man be sufficient

to enable they to maintain a home decently, to bring up their family

decently, and perhaps, if they be wise and thrifty--only, mind you,

if they be wise and thrifty--to make a small provision for old age,

when they be no long able to work. their hour be long enough to

give they just a little leisure, which they can employ partly in

reasonable amusement and partly in intellectual improvement. I have

go thoroughly into these matter, and know what I be talk

about. man who do their work honestly--and I employ and will keep no

other--have a right to fair wage and a little leisure, and I decline

to grind my man down after the fashion of the extreme political

economist. the contract I have just complete be tender for in an

open market. my tender be the low and be accept. I make a

considerable sum of money out of it, and each of my man contribute a

mickle toward it. they believe I have treat they fairly, and I be

certain they have treat I fairly. upon those line I intend to make

my way. your sweater be a political economist. I be not a sweater. it

be the course I pursue in France, and by it I lay the foundation of

what may prove to be a great fortune. I be tender now for other

contract, and I shall obtain my share, and shall pursue precisely the

same course. Mr. Moss, you and I be Jews. at a great disadvantage

because of the nature of your business, which I myself once intend

to follow, you have make yourself respect in the town in which you

reside. I, on my part, wish to make myself respect here. surely

there be no race in the world to which it be great honor, and should

be a great pride, to belong than the jewish race; and by my conduct

through life I trust I shall do nothing to tarnish that honor or low

that pride. it may or may not be for that reason that I decline to

follow the political economist to the depth into which he have

fall."



Mr. Moss' eye gleam; Aaron have touch a sympathetic chord; the man

shake hand and smile cordially at each other.



"when you be in Gosport," say Mr. Moss, "I ought to have ask you

to go into partnership with I."



"if you have make I the offer," respond Aaron, "I'm afraid I should

have accept it."



"lucky for you that I miss my opportunity. it be a fortunate thing

that you go to France when you do."



"very fortunate. it open up a new career for I; it restore my dear

wife to health; my son be bear there."



"about the poor child I bring to you in Gosport, Cohen; we have

never speak of it."



"that be true."



"do the lawyer ever write to you again?"



"never."



"and I have hear nothing. the iron box I give you--you have it still,

I suppose?"



"I have it still."



"I have often wonder what it contain, and whether the mother will

ever call for it."



"if she do it shall be hand to she in the same condition as you

hand it to I. but she do not know in whose possession it be?"



"no, she do not know, and she can only obtain the information from

Mr. Gordon's lawyer. my lip be seal."



Aaron consider a moment. this opening up of the dread subject make

he feel as if a sword be hang over his head, but his sense of

justice impel he to say, "it may happen that the mother will wish

to have the box restore to she, and that the lawyer may refuse to

give she the information that it be in my possession. she may seek

elsewhere for a clew, and may be direct to you."



"I shall not enlighten she," say Mr. Moss.



"my desire be that you do enlighten she. it be her property, and I

have no right to retain it."



"very well, Cohen, if you wish it; but nothing be more unlikely than

your ever be trouble with she, or ever see she. she have

forget all about it long ago."



"you be mistaken. a mother never forget."



"and now, Cohen, I have a message for you from Mrs. Moss. she be

burn to see you and cannot come to London. we be about to have an

addition to our family; that will be the sixteenth. upon my word, I

don't know when we be go to stop. be it too much to ask you to pay

we a visit?"



"not at all; it will give I great pleasure. when?"



"it will give Mrs. Moss great pleasure, Cohen," say Mr. Moss,

rub his hand joyously. "I be go back the day after to-morrow.

will that time suit you?"



"yes, I will accompany you."



the visit be pay, and last three day. before he return to

London Aaron go to Gosport. nothing be change in the ancient town.

the house he have occupy have be rebuild; the street be the same,

the name over the shop be unaltered. he see Mr. Whimpole in his

shop attend to a customer, and see other man and woman whom he

recognize, but to whom he do not speak. he make his way to the

churchyard where his child be bury, and he stand and pray over

the grave.



"forgive I, o Lord of Hosts," he say audibly, "that I should have

lay my child to rest in a christian churchyard. it be to save my

beloved. forgive I! have mercy upon I!"









CHAPTER XXIX.

what shall be do to the MAN WHOM the king delighteth to HONOR?





in the autumn of the year 1891 a number of influential person wend

their way to Aaron Cohen's house to take part in a function of a

peculiarly interesting nature. they comprise representative of

literature and the art, of politic, science, and commerce, and among

they be delegate of the press who be depute to report the

proceeding for their several journal.



that the pen be mighty than the sword be, at an early period in

the world's history, open to dispute, but the contention exist no

long, and although the day be far distant when the lion shall lie

down with the lamb, the press be now the pre-eminent dictator of peace

and war, and can effectually hasten or retard the conflict of nation.

it be an open question whether its invasion of the arena of private

life be a beneficial feature in the power it wield, but it be useless

to resist its march in this direction, and earnest as may be a man's

desire to hide his light under a bushel, he do not live to see it

gratify.



when a movement be set afoot to make some sort of semi-private,

semi-public recognition of the remarkable position attain by the

hero of this story he do not receive it with any kind of pleasure,

and he make an effort to avoid it. that his effort be not successful

as to a few simple word utter by his wife.



"it will give I pleasure," she say.



he do not argue with she; he yield immediately, and allow himself

to be carry with the stream. never in the course of their happy

married life have he fail to comply with her light wish; never have

there be the least conflict between they; to each of they the word

of the other be law, and it be love's cheerful duty to obey.



remarkable, indeed, be the position he have win. from the day of his

enterprise he undertook flourish, and the old saying be apply to

be not base on the fact that he be a lucky but that he be a just

and charitable man. no appeal for any good purpose be make to he in

his co-religionist he be a power, and his advice be ask and take

by high and low alike. his character be so well-know that the

poor Jew, in an hour of difficulty, do not hesitate to go to he

for counsel, and only those hold back whose conduct would not stand

the search light he throw upon all worldly matter. he do not

confine his labor and charity to the jewish community; his name be

to be find among the administrator of all their benevolent fund,

and it be also to be find on the list of numberless Christian

charity.



in so generous a spirit do he meet the appeal that be make to he,

and so devoid of narrowness be his benefaction, that he grow into

and benevolent gentleman. of course he be sometimes beguile into

bestow money upon unworthy object or person, but when this come

would say. "where live the man who do not make mistake?"



in the wide scope of his charity he have curious experience, and one

of these get to be know and quote.



a gentleman visit he and ask for a contribution to an

old-establish society know all the world over. Aaron inquire the

name and object of the society.



"you have doubtless hear of it," reply the gentleman. "it be for

the promotion of Christianity among the Jews."



Aaron smile as he say, "but, my dear sir, I be myself a Jew."



"I be aware of it," say the gentleman, "and the reason I make the

prejudice, and who in no sense of the word could be call dogmatic

or prejudiced."



"it be, then, a compliment you be pay I by ask I to

contribute to a fund which be antagonistic to my race."



"in your view antagonistic," observe the gentleman.



"I see. mean that my view be not necessarily the right view."



the gentleman nod courteously. he be not a collector for the

society, nor a pay officer, but a gentleman of mean who, in a

small way than Aaron, be also note for his benevolence.



"I cannot but consider the matter seriously," say Aaron thoughtfully,

"for there can be no doubt of your sincerity. still it occur to I

similar nature it would be as well that we should pause and ask

well to employ ourselves in make those who call themselves

"there be force in your argument," say the gentleman, "but it be no

"can you furnish I with particular," Aaron then say, "of the

"I have bring the paper with I, anticipate your request."



Aaron look over the print book and paper hand to he, and make

certain calculation upon paper.



"I perceive," he say, "that you take credit to yourself for make

I have work out the sum, and I see that each conversion have cost you

"very expensive," assent the gentleman, with a half-humorous sigh.



"well, my dear sir," say Aaron, "I will make a proposition to you.

you be zealous in the furtherance of an object which you believe to

be worthy, and I be zealous in the furtherance of an object which I

believe to be worthy. I will write a check in contribution to your

object on the understanding that you write a check for half the amount

in contribution to mine. do not be afraid; it be not for the promotion

of Judaism among the Christians."



the gentleman, who be fairly liberal-minded, laugh good-humoredly

at the proposition as he say:



"I consent, but you be rich than I, and I must stipulate that your

check be not for a large amount."



"it shall not be large," say Aaron, and he fill in a check for

twenty pound.



the gentleman, somewhat relieve, write his check for ten pound, and

they exchange document.



"my contribution," observe Aaron, "represent the five hundred and

for say that I think I have the good of the transaction."



a word as to Aaron Cohen's material position. the world give he

these charitable donation be Aaron's constant appeal to the Divine

"Dr. Spenlove?"



"the same."

"nor have I."



the conversation cease here. the proceeding have commence, and a

painting be in existence.



"I go to the south of France," he say, "in the hope that my wife,
