such be not my belief. there be an all-see Providence who show we

the right path. he speak through our reason and our conscience, and

except for the accident of birth, which lay a heavy burden upon many

unfortunate being, and which should render they not fully responsible

for the evil they do, we ourselves be responsible for the

consequence of our action. we must accept the responsibility and the

consequence."



he pause a few moment before he continue.



"when man of fair intelligence err they err consciously; it be useless

for they to say that they err in ignorance of the consequence. they

must know if they write with black ink that their writing must be

black."



he pause again.



"but it may be that a man commit a conscious error through his

affection, and if that error inflict injury upon no living being--if

it even confer a benefit upon one or more--there may be some

palliation of his error. in state that you set for I a standard too

high I be state my firm belief. no man be stainless, no man be

without reproach; the doctrine of infallibility apply to human

affair be monstrous and wicked; it be an arrogation of divine power.

I be, as all man be, open to error; in my life, as in the life of

all man, there have be mistake, but I may still take the credit to

myself that if I have commit a conscious error it have harm no

living soul, and that it have spring from those affection which

sweeten and bless our life. a reference have be make to my be a

Jew. I glory that I be one. the tradition and history of the race to

which I be proud to belong have be of invaluable service to I, and

to the circumstance of my be a Jew I owe the incident of this day,

which will ever be a proud memory to I and to my family. in the name

of my dear wife and my own I thank you cordially, sincerely, and

gratefully for the honor you have pay to we--an honor not beyond my

wife's merit, but far beyond my own."



other speech follow, and when the proceeding be at an end Dr.

Spenlove ask Mr. Moss to introduce he to Mr. Cohen.



"Cohen," say Mr. Moss, "Dr. Spenlove wish to know you."



Aaron start.



he never forget a name or a face, and he recollect the mention of

Dr. Spenlove's name when Mr. Moss come to he in Gosport with the

child.



"without exactly know it, perhaps," say Dr. Spenlove, "you have

be most kind in movement in which I have take an interest. I be

glad of the opportunity of make your acquaintance."



nothing more; no reference to the private matter.



Aaron breathe more freely.



he respond to Dr. Spenlove's advance, and the gentleman part

friend.



Rose Moss be in the room during the proceeding, and her fair young

in that honor.



when the gratifying but fatigue labor of the day be at an end,

and Aaron, Rachel, and Rose be alone, Rachel say:



"I be sorry, dear Rose, that Joseph be not here to hear what be say

about his father."



"it would not have make he love and honor he more," say Rose.



Rachel press her hand and kiss she; she have grow to love this

sweet and simple girl, who seem to have but one thought in life, she

lover. then the sightless woman ask they to describe the picture to

she, and she listen in an ecstasy of happiness to their word.



"be it not wonderful?" she say to Aaron. "a famous picture, they

say, and I the principal figure. what can the painter have see in

I?"



"what all man see, my life," reply Aaron, "but what no one know as

I know."



"it have be a happy day," sigh Rachel; she sit between they, each

hold a hand. "you do not hear from our dear Ruth this morning?"



"no, dear mother." for thus be Rose already permit to address

Rachel.



"she will be home in two day, and our dear lad as well. I wish he

be back from Australia, even before he have start, and so do you,

my dear. but time soon pass. just now it seem but yesterday that we

be in France."



the day wane. Rachel and Rose be together; Aaron be in his study,

write letter. a servant enter.



"a gentleman to see you, sir."



Aaron look at the card, which bear the name of Mr. Richard

Dillworthy.



"I be busy," say Aaron. "do he wish to see I particularly? ask he

if he can call again."



"he say his business be press, sir."



"show he in."



the servant usher the visitor into the room--a slightly build,

middle-aged man, with iron-gray hair and whisker. Aaron motion he

to a chair, and he place a card on the table bear the name and

address of a firm of lawyer.



"I be Mr. Dillworthy, of Dillworthy, Maryx & Co.," he say.



"yes."



"I have come to speak to you upon a family matter----"



"a family matter!" exclaim Aaron, interrupt he.



"on behalf of a client. I shall take it as a favor if you will regard

this interview as private."



"certainly."



"it refer principally to your daughter, Miss Ruth Cohen."









CHAPTER XXX.

the honorable percy STORNDALE.





for the second time on this eventful day Aaron feel as if his sin be

about to be bring home to he, as if the temple which, by long year

of honorable and upright conduct, he have build for himself be about

to crumble to dust.



in that temple be enshrine not only his good name, but what be of

far great value to he, his wife's happiness and peace of mind. it

child." out of Rachel's innate goodness and sweetness spring the deep

without, but the spirit of love be the offspring of one's own heart,

and it be make endure and ennoble by one's own high quality;

and in a like manner it be one's low passion which debase and

degrade it.



in whatever fashion Rachel would receive her husband's confession he

know full well that it would inflict upon she the most exquisite

suffering; the cherish ideal of her life would be shatter, and she

would sit forever afterward in sackcloth and ashe. he have sow a

harvest of woe, and his constant fervent prayer be that he might not

be compel to reap it with his own hand.



Agitated as he be, he do not betray himself by word or sign, but by

a courteous movement of his hand invite his visitor to proceed.



"it be a family matter," say Mr. Dillworthy, "of a peculiarly

"possibly, possibly, but my client hold strong view, and feel he

"favor I with the name of your client."



"Lord Storndale."

"but you be familiar with his name?"



"not at all. it be the first time I have hear it."



"you surprise I. Lord Storndale be a peer."



"I know very few peer, and have have no occasion to study the

"I repeat, the name be strange to I."

"never; and I be at a loss to understand the association of their

the lawyer pause. in this unexpected turn of affair a deviation

suggest itself to his legal mind which would be likely to assist

he.



"Mr. Cohen, you have the reputation of be an earnest and sincere

Jew."



"I follow the precept and the obligation of my faith," say Aaron,

with a searching glance at his visitor.



"in this backsliding and time-serve age orthodoxy--especially, I

should say, in the jewish religion--have a hard time of it. the custom

and duty of an enlightened civilization must clash severely with the

Aaron signify assent.



"what be your opinion of mixed marriage?"



Aaron do not answer immediately; he have a suspicion that there be

"I do not approve of they," he say.

Aaron by a great effort control himself.



"I will wait till you have quite finish, Mr. Dillworthy. before I

Mr. Dillworthy, having obtain his point, wisely drop the subject.

he briefly express his obligation to Aaron, and rise to take his

before he reach the door, however, he turn, and in a tone of

courteous deference ask if Mr. Cohen could spare he a few moment

Aaron assenting, the lawyer resume his seat, and take a pocketbook

from his pocket, search in it for a letter.









CHAPTER XXXI.

the spirit of the dead PAST.





Aaron observe he anxiously. the disclosure that have already be

be look. "I be afraid I have leave it behind I. excuse I a

moment; I wish to refresh my memory."



he run his eye over the letter, and nod as he go through its

point of importance.



"do it concern the unhappy affair we have discuss"? inquire

"I trust not."

truly this be a day of startling surprise to he. he recollect the

"then you be on term of intimacy with he?"



"no. we meet to-day for the first time."

"I will consider it," say Aaron, and his voice be trouble; "that be

Mr. Dillworthy give he a kind look and say: "it be not an opportune

interested, when another subject, the welfare of a dear daughter, must

naturally engross your attention. pray forgive I, Mr. Cohen."



Aaron bend his head, and as the lawyer close the door behind he sink

into his chair with a heavy sigh.









CHAPTER XXXII.

before all, DUTY.





on this evening many press matter claim his attention, and

before Mr. Dillworthy's visit he have intend to devote himself

entirely to they.



he take an active part in the dispensing of several jewish charity,

and his personal attendance be necessary to a wise distribution of

their fund. some of these charity be modest in the limited extent

of their aim, but they need care and attention, and his presence

be always anxiously look for by both the administrator and

recipient.



meeting of two of the charity be to be hold this evening, and

he have promise to preside at both. he must not disappoint they.

before all, duty. that be the thought that come to he--before all,

duty, and it be only the iteration of it that bring a true sense

of its significance to his mind. before all, duty, in these public

matter--but do it not also apply to private life? and if so, what

part in the strict adherence to the axiom do love occupy?



what be his duty here at home in respect of his wife and the girl he

have bring up as their daughter? he endeavor to thrust the

reflection aside, and draw forth some paper which bear reference to

the charity and to another matter of great public, importance which

have occupy he for week past, and which he be on the point of

bring to a successful conclusion.



he strove now to concentrate his attention upon the paper, for he be

to attend a late night meeting at eleven o'clock at which a decision

be to be arrive at which be to affect thousand of poor family.



there have be a great strike in the building trade, and vast number

of man have voluntarily throw themselves out of employment, and have

choose what be almost next door to starvation in their adherence to a

principle. the strike have be bring about chiefly by Aaron's great

rival contractor, a Mr. Poynter, an employer of labor on a gigantic

scale, and a man as well know as Aaron himself.



to say that these two be rival do not necessarily imply that they

be enemy, for that be a game that two must play at, and it be a

game in which Aaron play no part. he do not approve of Mr.

Poynter's method--he go no far than that.



on the other hand, Mr. Poynter hate Aaron with a very sincere and

conscientious hate. he hate he because he have lose several

profitable contract which Aaron have obtain, and this hatred may be

apply in a general sense because he hate every successful rival,

great or small.



he hate he because Aaron be genuinely respect by large body of

workingmen, and have great influence with they; and this hatred may

also be apply in a general sense, because he hate all employer of

labor who be hold by their workman in high respect than himself.



he hate Aaron because he be a Jew, and this may certainly be apply

in a general sense, because he have a bitter hatred of all Jews, and

would have willingly subscribe liberally and join in a crusade to

hunt they out of the country.



that a Jew could be a good man, that he could be a just

man, that he could do anything without an eye to profit or

self-aggrandizement--these be monstrous proposition, and no man of

sense, certainly no true Christian, could seriously entertain they.

Mr. Poynter be a Christian, a true Christian, regular in his

attendance at church and fairly liberal also in his charity, though

his left hand always know what his right hand do. and here he find

another cause for hate Aaron.



he hear his name quote as a man of large benevolence, and he go so

far as to declare that Aaron's charity be a means to an end.



"he look upon they as an investment," he say; "they bring he a good

return. do you ever know a Jew part with money without an eye to the

main chance?"



when he hear that it be generally report that Aaron give away in

secret much more than he give away in public his comment be, "what be

easy than to set such a rumor afloat? any rich man can do it by an

expenditure of ten pound a year. if money be bestow in secret who

be to know of it but the donor? if it become public who could have

speak of it first but the donor? no one but a fool would be gull by

so transparent a trick."



these detraction be generally utter to man who sympathize with

the speaker, and they be not without effect. by which it will be

see that Aaron have enemy, as all man have.



Mr. Poynter pose as a moral man, and it be the very essence of these

usurper of morality that they must stand alone, and that upon their

pedestal there shall be no room for any other braggart. he be a

married man with son and daughter and a wife, who all look upon

the husband and father as a pattern.



whether his child follow the pattern or not do not concern this

history, which have to do with the head of the family alone. whatever a

man may be in the prime of life the early Adam, if it differ from

the later, will very likely assert itself in the blood of his

descendant, and this may have be the case with Mr. Poynter's

child, despite the respect in which they hold he.



you come into contact with a sober-face man, whose distinguishing

mark be one of intense respectability; you see he at home in the

bosom of his family, whom he entertain with severely respectable

platitude; you hear his opinion on matter of current interest, a

trial, a scandal in high life, tittle-tattle of the stage, the court,

the Church, and society in general.



what an intensely respectable gentleman, what severely respectable

view, what strict morality, what an estimable father of a family!

ah, but draw the curtain of year aside, and we behold another

man--another man, yet still the same: a man about town, philander,

deceive, lying, and play the base part to serve his selfish

pleasure. where be the morality, where the respectability now?--and

which of the two be the true man?



be this the case with Mr. Poynter? the course of event may possibly

supply the answer to this question presently. meanwhile nothing be

more certain to-day than that he be accept as he present himself.

but if in the past life of such a man as Aaron Cohen may be find an

episode of his own creating upon which he look with dismay, why might

it not be so with such a man as Mr. Poynter?



Aaron Cohen and he have be acquaint for many year, and at Aaron's

hand Mr. Poynter have receive mortification again and again. in a

country like England, where operation of magnitude be be

continually undertake, there be room for all who occupy the high

rung of the ladder; it be only the low rung which be overcrowded,

and which need clearing by mean of emigration to land where there be

room for the toiling, suffer million. but Mr. Poynter choose to

believe that there be not room for Aaron and himself, and he have

nurse and foster an ardent wish to drag Aaron down.



perhaps it be the knowledge of his own early life that make he

think, "if I could find something in his past that would bring shame

upon he--if I could only rake up something that would show he in his

true light! it would be the commercial and social ruin of he. he

would never be able to hold up his head again."



he would gladly have pay for some such discovery.



at the present time he have special reason for hate. one reason be

that the strike in the building trade be affect he seriously. he

be engage in large contract in the carrying out of which some

thousand of man be need, and it be chiefly against himself that

the strike be order by the union. he be on the brink of great

loss, and Aaron have be call in as a mediator and arbitrator.



the strike at an end he be safe, but every day that it be prolong

mean so many hundred of pound out of his pocket. his fate seem to

hang upon the final advice to the man which Aaron be to give, and his

profit would be large or small accord to the nature of that

advice.



he lay the credit of the strike at Aaron's door, for in their

enterprise he and Aaron employ different method. Aaron have pursue

in England the course he have pursue in France.



he pay his man liberally, give they bonus, even to a certain extent

acknowledge they as co-operator. in Mr. Poynter's eye this be a

crime, for it strike at the very root of his prosperity. "he be a

socialist," Mr. Poynter say; "man of his stamp be a danger to

society."



another reason be that tender have lately be call for on work of

exceptional magnitude, and he have entertain hope of obtain the

contract. again he be worst by this insidious enemy. within the

last few hour he have hear that Aaron's tender have be accept. he

grind his tooth with rage. he could have undertake the work in

spite of the strike, for he have nearly complete arrangement for the

introduction of foreign workman, whom he be determined to employ if

the english worker hold out.



there would be a row, of course, and the low class would cast

obloquy upon he, for which he would have to thank his rival enemy.

when he hear that he have lose the contract he say to a friend: "I

would give half I be worth to drag he down." and he mean what he

say, although he probably name a large percentage than he would be

willing to pay.



the last meeting of the striker be now be hold. it have be

call for seven o'clock, and it be know that the discussion would

occupy several hour. Aaron be not ask to attend this discussion,

which be to be private, even the representative of the press not

be admit.



Eleven o'clock be the hour at which he be expect, and it be

understand that he would bring with he certain proposition from the

master, which, with the workman's view, be to be discuss, and a

decision arrive at. to-morrow morning's paper would announce whether

the strike be to be continue or be at an end.



he study the paper before he: the argument and statement of

employer of labor, comparison of wage here and in foreign

country, the comparative rate of live here and there, document

of every description, among which be pathetic letter from wife of

the striker, implore he to put an end to the strike.



he have master they all, and be familiar with every detail, but he

wish to divert his attention for this night from his own private

affair. his mind must be free; he would think of they to-morrow. he

have public duty to attend to. before all, duty.



the word haunt he. he could think only of his beloved wife and of

Ruth. very well. he have half an hour to spare before he leave his house

for the jewish meeting; he would devote the time to a consideration

of his private duty.



he gather his paper, arrange they in order, and put they in his

pocket. he dally with they at first, but feel that he be

prolong the simple task in order to shorten the time for serious

thought, he smile pitifully at his weakness, and complete it

expeditiously.



in admit Ruth into his household, in adopt she as a daughter,

he have undertake a sacred responsibility. he be fully conscious of

this twenty year ago in Gosport, and what he have do have be do

deliberately.



it be a question then of the sacrifice of a precious life. the doctor

have set it clearly before he.



the pregnant word they have exchange be in his memory now, and

might have be speak only a few moment since.



"her life," the doctor have say, "hang upon the life of her child."



"if our child live," Aaron have ask, "there be hope that my wife

will live?"



"a strong hope," the doctor have answer.



"and if our child die?" ask Aaron.



the doctor answer: "the mother will die."



he recall the agony of those hour, the suffering through which

Rachel have pass with so much sweetness and patience, his poverty

and helplessness, the dark future before he. then come the ray of

light--Mr. Moss, with the strange commission of the deserted child. he

have not court it, have not invite it, he have have no hand in it. he

have regard it as a message from Heaven.



what follow?



the death of his own babe, the calm and peaceful death, the young soul

take to heaven, his beloved wife in an untroubled sleep by the side

of her dead babe. it be a visitation of God. again, could he be

accuse of having have a hand in it? Heaven forbid!



on the contrary, who could blame he for believe that it be a

divine direction of the course he be to take? and who be wrong?

surely not the mother who have desert her babe. surely not the babe,

who have find a happy home. the wrong--and herein be the sting---be

to Rachel, whose life have be save by the deceit. so far, then, be

he not justify?



but if before the committal of a sin we could see the consequence of

the sin--if he have see the consequence of his--would he not have

pause and say: "it rest with God. let it be as he will. I will be

no party to the deceit"? in that case Rachel's life would have be

sacrifice. there be no human doubt of it. Rachel would have die,

and the blessing she have shed around she, the good she have be

enable to do, the suffering heart she have relieve, the light she

have bring into despairing home, would never have be. against a

little evil so much good. against a slight error so much that be

sweet and beautiful.



but in these reflection he have take into account only Rachel and

himself--only their two life. how about Ruth herself?



he have never disguise from himself that there be much in Ruth's

character which be not in accordance with Rachel's view or his own,

which she do not assimilate with either of their nature. be one

of his family in the eye of the world, he have bring she up as a

Jewess. she be bear a Christian. be this not a crime of which she

have be make the victim? he have experience great difficulty in her

education. he wish to correct the defect which exist in ninety-nine

English Jewesses out of a hundred--he wish she to pray in the Hebrew

tongue, and to understand her prayer.



to this end he himself have endeavor to teach she to read and

translate Hebrew. she would not learn. even now as a woman she

understand but a very few word, and this scanty knowledge be

mechanical. a parrot might have learn as much. she have an aversion

to jewish society.



as a child, when she be necessarily in lead string, she be take

by Rachel to the synagogue on every Sabbath day, but when she begin to

have intelligent idea she rebel; she would not go, and Rachel

walk to the house of God alone.



it be a grief to she that Ruth would not follow in her footstep, and

she and Aaron have frequently converse upon the subject.



"it be so with many jewish woman," Aaron say. "it would be wrong to

force she; she will find out her error by and by."



but Ruth never do, and Rachel suffer in silence.



there be another sorrow. between their son Joseph and Ruth do not

exist that love which brother and sister should bear each other.

Joseph be ready with demonstrative affection, but Ruth do not

respond. Aaron have take note of this, but he be powerless to remedy

it, and the lad, who be as solicitous as his father to spare the dear

mother pain, make no trouble of it.



Ruth respect and admire her reputed father, and in the feeling she

entertain toward he there be an element of fear, because of his

strength of character, but she do not love he as a child should. he,

know what he know, find excuse for she. "it be in her blood," he

say to himself.



all this be hide from Rachel, to whom Ruth be tender and kind. who

could be otherwise to so sweet a woman? but Rachel do not know of

what she be deprive until Rose Moss begin to make long visit to

their home. "Rose be like a daughter to I," she say, and only Aaron

be aware of the depth of mean these simple word convey.



but now he have to consider the matter, not from his or Rachel's point

of view, but from Ruth's. she be a woman in her springtime, and love

have come to she, and she have hold out her arm to it. and the man she

love be a Christian.



it be not within his right to take into consideration that the man

she love be a spendthrift and a scapegrace. the question have often

intrude itself, since she be grow to womanhood, whether he would

not be add sin to sin by encourage she to marry a Jew. she have

answer the question herself. what right have he to gainsay she? he

might, as a true and sincere friend, say to she: "this man will not

make you happy. he have vice and defect which will bring misery upon

your home. you must not marry he." but he have no right to say to she:

"you must not marry this man, because he be a Christian." it would be

a detestable argument for one in his position, and in her, to

advance.



then Mr. Dillworthy might be wrong in his estimate of the young man's

character. the only objection Lord Storndale have to the union be that

Ruth be a Jewess. but she be not a Jewess, and it be in his power

to go to the young man's father and make the disclosure to he. Lord

Storndale's natural reply would be: "let it be clearly understand. you

have do this lady a grievous wrong. you be a wealthy man. repair

the wrong by make a suitable settlement upon she. but it must be

publicly do, and the injustice of which you have be guilty must be

publicly acknowledge." the only answer he could make would be: "it be

just. I will do as you dictate."



what would be the effect as regard himself? among his

co-religionist he be hold up as a pillar of the old jewish faith.

his voice have be raise against apostasy; he have take a decided

stand against the more liberal idea of civilized life which prevail

and be adopt by a large section of his race.



even now he be pledge to deliver a public address against the

backsliding of the modern Jew, who be disposed to adapt his life to

the altered circumstance of the time. he have write his address,

and public attention have be draw to the come event. his argument

be to himself convincing, and by they he hope to stem the tide.



he have always be orthodox, and he hope to prevail against the wave

of heterodoxy which be sweep over modern Judaism. he have step

forward as a champion. in the light of the duty which properly

devolve upon he, how dare he, himself a transgressor, presume to

teach his brother their religious duty? his sound judgment of thing

which interested or affect he be due to his common sense, which,

he have be hear to say, be a rare quality.



"you be always right," Mr. Moss once say to he. "how be it?"



"if I form a correct opinion," he reply, with a smile, "it be

because I exercise my common sense. I do not judge from my own

standpoint."



he do this now. he put himself in the place of other man. he listen

to his own confession. he pass the verdict upon himself.



"this man have be live the life of a hypocrite. he have accept

money for false service. not in word, but by his act, he have lie.

he have violate the canon of his religion. he have deceive his

wife--for money, which he pretend to despise. he have rob a young

girl of her birthright. and he dare to preach to we of duty!"



who would believe if he tell the true story of his hard trial--if he

describe the bitter tribulation of his soul when his beloved wife be

lie at death's door? he have counsel many man in their day of

struggle and temptation to be brave and do their duty. how have he

perform his in _his_ hour of temptation? no one would believe the

only story he could plead in extenuation of his sin. he would be

condemn by all.



and he be in the zenith of his fame. on this very day, when exposure

seem to be approach with swift and certain step, he have be

honor as few man live to be. if he feel pleasure in the position he

have win it be because it be a source of pride and pleasure to

Rachel. be he, with his own hand, to destroy the ideal he have

create? be this the plain duty that lie now before he?



"the carriage be at the door, sir."



it be a servant who interrupt his torture musing. he have give

order to be inform when his carriage be ready. with slow step he

leave his study.









CHAPTER XXXIII.

a CHEERFUL doctor.





there be an apartment in Aaron Cohen's house which be call the

cozy room, where the family be in the habit of sit when they have

no visitor, and it be here that their real domestic happiness

reign. here Aaron use to smoke his old silver-mount pipe, and

chat with his wife, and indulge in his entertaining pleasantry when

he be in the humor, and here the feeling use to steal over he that

life would hold more joy for he and those dear to he if they dwelt

in a small house and his doing be less under the public eye.



"I be convince," he would say, "that those who be in the low

middle class be the good off. they have few care, they have more

time for domestic enjoyment, they can attend without hindrance to

their own affair. there be no happiness in rich. why do I continue

to wish to accumulate more money?"



"because," Rachel would answer affectionately, "it enable you to

contribute to the happiness of other. but I should be as content if

we be poor."



on the occasion of Mr. Dillworthy's visit to Aaron a scene of a

different nature be be enact in the cozy room. Rachel be

overpower with languor, and she fall into a doze. the apartment be

large, but an arrangement of screen and the disposal of the furniture

make it look small; domestically speak, there be no comfort in any

but a small room.



Rose during her present visit have notice with concern that Mrs. Cohen

appear weak, and that her movement, which be always gentle, be

more so than usual, and that her quiet way seem to be the result of

physical prostration. she speak of it to Rachel, who confess that

she have not feel strong lately, but caution Rose to say nothing of

it to Aaron.



"he be so easily alarmed about I," she say, "and he have great

anxiety upon he."



"but you should see the doctor," urge Rose solicitously.



"I will wait a day or two," answer Rachel, and again enjoin Rose

not to alarm her husband.



on the evening of this exciting day she look so pale and fatigue

that she yield to Rose's solicitation, and without Aaron's

knowledge send for the physician who be in the habit of attend

she. while wait for he she fall asleep in her armchair in the cozy

room. at her request Rose play softly some of Rachel's favorite

piece. the piano be behind a screen at one end of the room, and Rose

do not know that she have fall asleep. while thus employ Prissy

quietly enter the room. the faithful woman look at her mistress,

and step noiselessly to the screen.



"Miss Rose," she whisper.



the girl stop play immediately, and come from behind the screen.



"be it the doctor, Prissy?" she ask.



"no, miss."



Prissy point to her mistress, and Rose go to the armchair, and

adjust a light shawl which be fall from the sleep lady's

shoulder. it be a slight action, but it be do with so much

tenderness that Prissy smile approvingly. she like Rose much well

than Ruth, who do not hold in her affection the place the other

member of the family do. humble as be her position in the

household, she have observe thing of which she disapprove.



Ruth be from home more frequently than she consider proper, and have

often say to she: "you need not tell my mother that I have go out

unless she ask you."



Prissy have not disobey she, and the consequence be that Ruth be

sometimes absent from the house for hour without her father or mother

be aware of it. Prissy's idea be that her young mistress would

bring trouble on the house, but she keep silence, because she would

otherwise have get into trouble herself with Ruth, and would also have

distress her dear lady if she have make mention of her suspicion,

for which she could have offer no reasonable explanation. Prissy's

distress of mind be not lessen because Ruth, when she enjoin

secrecy upon she, give her money, as if to purchase her silence. she

would have refuse these bribe, but Ruth force they upon she, and

she feel as if she be in a conspiracy to destroy the peace of the

family.



"I do not know she be asleep," say Rose, come back to Prissy.



"I'm sure you don't, miss. she fall off, you know."



"yes, I know," say Rose with affectionate solicitude. "what do you

want, Prissy?"



"I've get a letter for you, miss."



"I don't hear the postman."



"the postman don't bring it, miss," say Prissy, give she the

letter. "a boy. Said immejiet."



"it must be from--no." she be think of her lover as she look at

the letter, but she see it be not his hand. she recognize the

writing--it be Ruth's. "the envelope be not very clean, Prissy."



"so I tell the boy when he bring it to the back door."



"the back door!" exclaim Rose, rather bewildered.



"it's curious, ben't it, miss, that it ben't send by post?"



"yes, it be. what do the boy say?"



"it's what I say first, miss. 'you've be and drop it in the

gutter,' I say. but he only laugh and say it be give to he this

morning, and that he be to bring it to the servant' entrance and ask

for Prissy."



"but why don't he deliver it this morning?" ask Rose, her

bewilderment growing.



"I don't know, miss. he's be play in the street all day, I

expect. anyway he say I be to give it you when nobody be look.

it's Miss Ruth's writing, miss."



Rose make no remark upon this, but ask: "do he say who give it to

he?"



"a young lady he say, miss."



"that will do, Prissy."



"can I do anything for you, miss?



"nothing, thank you."



Prissy go, Rose look at the envelope, and see write in one

corner, "read this when you be alone." troubled and perplexed, she

stand with the letter in her hand, but when the door be open again

and the doctor be announce she put it hastily into her pocket and

go forward to meet he.



Dr. Roberts have attend Rachel for some year past, and take the

deep interest in she.



"sleep," he say, step to her side. he turn to Rose, and

question she, learn why he have be send for.



"she fall asleep," he say, with his finger on Rachel's pulse. "ah,

you be awake," as Rachel sit upright. "now let we see what be the

matter. you be not in pain? no. that's good."



"it be only because Rose be so anxious," say Rachel. "there be

really nothing the matter with I, doctor."



"but you feel weak and drowsy at time. we will soon set that right."



Dr. Roberts be one of those cheerful physician whose bright way

always brighten his patient. "make the good of a case," be a

favorite saying of his, "not the bad."



he remain with Rachel a quarter of an hour, advise she to get to

bed, give her instruction as to food, order she a tonic, and take

his leave. Rose go with he into the passage.



"there be no danger, doctor?"



"not the slight, my dear," he answer in a fatherly manner. "but I

would advise perfect rest. don't tell she anything exciting. she must

not be worried. get a humorous story, and read it to she. make she

laugh. let everything be bright and cheerful about she. but I need not

say that. it always be, eh? if you have any trouble keep they to

yourself. but what trouble should a young girl like you have?"



he meet Aaron at the street door.



"ah, Mr. Cohen, I have be to see your wife--in a friendly way."



"she be not ill?" ask Aaron in an anxious tone, step back.



"no--a little weak, that be all. don't go up to see she; I have just

leave she, and she will think there be something the matter, when

there's nothing that cannot be set right in a few day. she want

tone, that be all, and rest, and perfect freedom from excitement. that

be essential. such a day as this, flattering and pleasant as it must

have be, be not good for she. keep her mind at rest, let she hear

nothing to disturb she, speak of none but cheerful subject to she,

and she will be herself again in a week. follow my advice, and there

be not the least cause for anxiety."









CHAPTER XXXIV.

RUTH'S SECRET.





Dr. Roberts' hearty and confident tone carry conviction with it.

Aaron's anxiety be dispel; easy in his mind respect Rachel's

health, he feel like a man reprieve. a few day be still leave for

reflection, and he go forth to his public duty with a sense of

great relief.



Rose, meanwhile, be busy for some time attend to Rachel, who

wish the young girl to remain with she till she be asleep. with

Ruth's letter in her pocket, which have be deliver almost

clandestinely at the house, and which she be enjoin to read when

she be alone, she be compel to bridle her impatience. she do not

dare to speak of it to Rachel, and the course the conversation take in

the bedroom do not tend to compose she. Rachel speak only of family

matter--of her husband and her child--in which category she

include Rose. presently the conversation drift entirely to the

subject of Ruth.



"young girl," say Rachel, "confide in each other. there be a true

affection between you, be there not, my dear?"



"yes," reply Rose, wonder what be come, and dread it.



"it happen sometimes," continue Rachel, with a sigh, "that parent

do not entirely win their child's confidence. Joseph have not a

secret from I. he be happy. do you think Ruth be quite happy, my

dear?"



"I think so," say Rose.



"I be not ask you to break a confidence she may have repose in

you."



Rose could not refrain from interrupt she.



"but, dear mother, I know nothing."



as she utter the word a guilty feeling stole over she. what do the

letter in her pocket contain?



Rachel draw the girl's face to her, and caress she.



"now it be you," she say, "who be speak as if you be in trouble.

I be very inconsiderate, but love have its pain as well as its joy.

you have no trouble, Rose?"



"none, dear mother. I be perfectly happy."



"see how mistaken I be; and I hope I be mistake also about Ruth. I

fear that she have a secret which she be conceal from I. blind

people be suspicious, Rose, and breed trouble for themselves and

other."



"not you, dear mother," say Rose, kiss she. "now you must go to

sleep. this be quite against the doctor's order."



Rachel smile and yield; she take pleasure in be lead by those she

love.



in the solitude of her chamber Rose read the letter:





"darling Rose: I be in great trouble, and you must help I. you be

the only friend I have in the world--but no, I must not say that; it

be not true. what I mean be, you be the only friend at home I can

trust.



"Father and mother, and you, too, think I be in Portsmouth with your

family. dear Rose, I be in London--I have be in London all the week.

the happiness of my life be in your hand--remember that.

"Ruth."

CHAPTER XXXV.

the honorable percy STORNDALE makes an APPEAL.

it be a human failing to run into extreme. no man be quite so good or

CHAPTER XXXVI.

a DUTY PERFORMED.





before Mr. Moss rejoin he Aaron have repent of his promise to call
