debt?"



"it will be place in your hand to-morrow. do not return here

to-night. come and breakfast with I at nine in the morning."



Aaron sit up till long past midnight, make calculation and

arrange his affair. he be quite resolve to retire from everything

in the shape of public life, and altogether from business; and to

effect this there be much to do. he have uncompleted contract in hand

which he would transfer to employer of whose method he approve, and

he have just obtain another which a dozen contractor would be eager

to take off his hand. he think of Mr. Poynter, and shake his head.

to such a man he could not trust any of his responsibility. then he

devote himself to an examination of his private financial position.



after provide for Ruth he calculate that he could realize a sum of

about ninety thousand pound, in addition to which there be his

house and furniture, which would realize another ten thousand.

one-third of this should be give to his son Joseph and Rose,

one-third should be divide among the jewish charity, and one-third

should be invest for himself and Rachel. this would produce an

income amply sufficient for the maintenance of a comfortable home

either in London or the country.



"Rachel will be content," he think, "and the year that be leave to

we shall be pass in peace, away from the turmoil and fever of life.

if she will but forgive I--if she will but forgive!"



all depend upon that.



he hold office of honor in the synagogue, which he would immediately

resign. there and then he write his letter of resignation. he draw

forth the address upon modern judaism he have undertake to deliver,

hope thereby to counteract the loose view of religious obligation

which threaten to sap the foundation of the old faith. he read the

powerful argument he have write to this end, and sigh as he read.



"not for I the task," he murmur. "not for I. I be not worthy. it

be for I to learn, not to teach."



he tear the manuscript and burn it. he have forfeit the right to

show his brother the path of duty.



at length he come to the end of his labor. before he retire to rest

he pray long and fervently, and offer up supplication for

forgiveness.



at nine o'clock in the morning Mr. Moss present himself, and

report what he have do.



"everything be in such straight order," he say, "that the whole

business can be finish to-morrow."



"it will be a great weight off my mind," say Aaron, "when all the

paper be sign. I have letter from Rachel and Rose." he pass the

young girl's letter to Mr. Moss. "she say there be no change in

Rachel, but that she think the air and change of scene be do she

good. if you write to Rose do not hint of any impending trouble, and

do not mention Ruth's name, lest Rachel should suspect that something

be wrong. I ought to tell you, Mr. Moss, that I have resolve to

retire into private life; I shall be much happy, and I be sure

Rachel will be. it be a sudden resolution, and I dare say my friend

will be surprised, but I be fix; nothing can make I change my

mind."



"and your contract, Cohen?" ask Mr. Moss, who be sufficiently

familiar with Aaron's character to know that remonstrance at present

would be throw away.



"I shall transfer they. my earnest wish be that I shall be forget,

and allow to live in peace. I be grow old; let my place, which I

unworthily hold, be occupy by a well man."



"that be hardly likely to come to pass," say Mr. Moss gravely. "you

be not old; you be in the prime of life, with very many year of

usefulness before you. but I will not argue with you; when you have

recover from your depression, when Rachel be well again, you will

think well of it. we need you; no other man can fill your place. you

deliver your address on Sunday, do you not?"



"no."



"but, Cohen, it be expect; it be look forward to, and the good

result be anticipate from it. you will not go from your word?"



"I must. the address be destroy. I must bear whatever be say of I;

I accept it as part of my punishment."



"of your punishment! I do not understand you."



"you will by and by. Mr. Moss, the man who presume to set down the

law of right and wrong should be above reproach. can a thief preach

honesty? can a liar lift his voice in praise of truth?"



"these be strange utterance, Cohen, from your lip."



"there be a sad foundation for they. to know yourself--that be the

height of human wisdom; and I have learn too late. pray do not

continue the subject; you stand in the dark, I in the light."



"well, well," say Mr. Moss, with a sigh, "we will speak of this

another time. have you see the paper this morning?"



"I have not open they."



"they be full of your praise for put an end to the strike; they

say it be due alone to your character and powerful influence."



"I take no credit to myself. what I do be do with a conscientious

motive."



"good," say Mr. Moss with hearty emphasis. "that be the keynote of

your life. then what have you to reproach yourself with?"



"let every man search his own heart," reply Aaron, and his voice be

very mournful. "he will find the answer there. and now we will waste

no more time in idle conversation. we must go to the lawyer and the

bank. have you a list of Mr. Storndale's debt? ah! thank you." he

look at the total, and draw a check for the amount. "the payment of

these claim will keep you busy during the day. I will give

instruction to the lawyer to prepare the deed of settlement, and

to-morrow it can be sign. you will be a trustee; I will call upon a

gentleman who will be the other. I shall spend to-night at

Bournemouth, and will come back by an early train in the morning."



"will you not see Ruth before you leave?" ask Mr. Moss.



"no, not till everything be finish. how be she?"



"well and happy, and overjoy that you be not angry with she.

between ourselves, Cohen, it be not what she expect."



"she have all the more reason for contentment. I wish she to be happy."



they have a busy time with lawyer, bank manager, and creditor, and

Aaron just manage to catch the two-twenty train for Bournemouth. he

pass a quiet evening with Rachel and Rose, and answer the

question put by his wife concern Ruth in a manner to satisfy she.

with Rose he have a private conversation upon the subject, and

caution she to preserve silence as to the letter she have receive.

on the follow morning he take an early train for London, and

arrive before noon, find everything prepare for a final settlement

of his plan for Ruth's worldly future. when the deed be sign,

and the consol buy and deposit in the Bank of England, Aaron

breathe more freely. he have make some small atonement to Ruth for the

deception of which he have be guilty.



"we have have no honeymoon trip," say the Honorable Percy Storndale to

he, "and I be think of take Ruth to the Continent to-morrow, but

she will be unhappy if she do not see you before we go."



"I will come with you now," say Aaron.



they meet and part without any warm demonstration of affection. such

a demonstration from Ruth toward one whom she believe to be her

father, but for whom she have never entertain a strong love, would

have be a new feature in her character, and grateful as she be for

his generosity, she be hold back by the feeling that she have give

he a poor return for his lifelong kindness toward she and by her fear

that he be quietly angry with she; while Aaron be hold back by the

consciousness of his wrongdoing. and so the young couple go forth to

commence their new life, and the secret of Ruth's birth be still

unreveale. Aaron have not yet muster courage to make confession, but

he know that the hour be fast approach when he would stand in the

full light of the sin he have commit through love.









CHAPTER XXXVII.

the MOTHER'S APPEAL.





two week have pass away. Joseph have come and go. in the company of

Rose and his parent he have spend three sad and happy day in

Bournemouth--happy because he be in the society of those he love,

sad because he be so soon to part from they. Rachel's health be not

improve, the physician say, and those to whom she be so dear be

continually warn that she be not to be agitate by news of a

distressing nature. the shrewd doctor impress this upon they the

more strongly for the reason that he perceive that a cloud be

hang over their spirit which they be conceal from the

sightless lady.



"you cannot be too careful," he say. "a sudden shock might produce

serious effect."



they be, therefore, compel to preserve secrecy, and to invent

excuse for Ruth's absence from the family circle. Joseph and Rose have

both be inform of Ruth's marriage, and be thus partner with

Aaron in the affectionate conspiracy. Aaron have go no far with

they than this. the vital secret be still in his sole possession.



the carrying out of his intention to retire into private life, and to

entirely give up the important business transaction in which he have

be engage for many year, necessitate his be in London the

great part of these two week; he would have like to keep his

proceeding from public knowledge, but in this he be not successful.

one cause of the publicity which be give to his action lie in the

disposal of a portion of his fortune in charity; his benefaction be

herald far and wide, and he be make the subject of numberless

laudatory article in the newspaper. another cause be his

transference of large contract, and especially of the last one for

which he have successfully compete, to other firm. in the

transference of these contract he have lay down stipulation with

respect to wage and hour of labor which, while they do not meet

with the full approval of employer, earn for he renew

commendation from the working class. Mr. Poynter have try to obtain

some of these contract, but Aaron find he so shifty in his method

that he decline to have anything to do with he. for which defeat Mr.

Poynter vow revenge, and look about for the mean of compass

it.



at the end of the fortnight Aaron be in London, his labor end, and

at this time his fortune amount to something over thirty thousand

pound, a large sum than he anticipate would be leave to he.



it must be mention that Ruth and her husband have just return to

London, as he be inform by letter, in consequence of Ruth's

indisposition. it be she who write to he, and she be so earnest in

the expression of her wish that he would come and see she that he have

send she a telegram say that he would call at eight or nine

o'clock, by which time he expect to be free.



at six o'clock on this evening he and Mr. Moss be together in

Aaron's house, by appointment. Aaron have resolve to reveal his secret

to his faithful friend, and he have set apart this evening as a fitting

time for the disclosure. on the following day Rachel and Rose be to

return to London, as Rachel do not wish to remain any long in

Bournemouth, and Mr. Moss be to return to Portsmouth.



Mr. Moss' face be flush with excitement as he enter the room with

an evening paper in his hand.



"have you hear the rumor, Cohen?" he ask excitedly.



"what rumor?" inquire Aaron, rise to meet his friend.



"about your bank, the Equitable Alliance?"



"no, I have hear nothing. I have not be out of the house since the

morning."



"it come on I like a thunderclap, but it cannot be true."



"what cannot be true, Mr. Moss?" Aaron speak quite calmly.



"well, there's nothing definite, but you know there have be something

like a panic in the City."



"I know, but it cannot affect I. I have no investment now, with the

solitary exception of my bank share. all my affair be settle, and

the money in the bank until I decide how to invest it."



Mr. Moss groan. "I wish you have it safely invest in consol. be

all your money there?"



"every shilling. the only investment I have not realize be the

share I hold in the bank."



"that make it all the bad. the shareholder be liable to the

depositor."



"yes."



the flush have die out of Mr. Moss' face, which be now white with

apprehension. "they're call it out in the street--but here's the

paper."



he point to a paragraph, which state that one of the large bank

in the City have close its door half an hour before its time, and

that the panic have in consequence reach an alarming height.



"there be no name mention, Mr. Moss."



"no, Cohen, no; but I pass through the City on my way here, and the

name of the bank be on everyone's lip. if the bank stop payment

to-morrow how will you stand?"



"if it stop payment for sufficient cause," say Aaron in a steady

voice, "I shall be a ruin man!"



"Good Heavens! and you can speak of it so calmly!"



"why not? to work myself into a frenzy will not help I. there be

bad misfortune."



"I cannot imagine they, Cohen. ruin? absolutely ruin?"



"absolutely ruin," say Aaron, with a smile.



"and it be only yesterday that you were----"



he could not continue, and Aaron take up his word.



"it be only yesterday that I be on top of the tree. a dangerous

height, Mr. Moss, but I must bear the fall. if, when they climb the

ladder of fortune, man would but be careful to make the low rung

secure! but prosperity make they reckless. do not look so mournful.

happiness be as easily find in poverty as in rich."



"it may be, after all, a false alarm," groan Mr. Moss.



"let we hope so. we will wait till to-morrow."



"will you not go into the City now to ascertain whether it be true or

false?"



"no; it will only trouble I, and it will not affect the result. I

will wait till to-morrow."



so marked be the contrast between his cheerful and Mr. Moss'

despondent mood that it really seem as if it be his friend's

fortune that be imperil instead of his own. he be stand by the

door, and hear a knock, he open it.



"I beg your pardon, sir," say a servant, "but this gentleman be

below, and want to see Mr. Moss."



Aaron take the card without look at it, and hand it to Mr. Moss,

who exclaim:



"Dr. Spenlove!"



"show he up," say Aaron to the servant.



"have I not well see he alone?" ask Mr. Moss.



"if you have no objection," reply Aaron, "receive he here in my

presence."



they both seem to scent a come danger, but Aaron appear to hail

it gladly, while Mr. Moss would rather have avoid it.



"a thousand apology," say Dr. Spenlove to Aaron upon his entrance,

"for intrude upon you, but hear that Mr. Moss be here I take the

liberty of follow he. my errand be an urgent one."



"I be happy to see you, Dr. Spenlove," Aaron respond; "if your

business with Mr. Moss be not quite private you can speak freely

before I."



"I think," say Dr. Spenlove, half hesitating, "that it be quite

private."



"I have a distinct reason," continue Aaron as though Dr. Spenlove have

not speak, "for make the suggestion, but man sometimes receive an

inspiration for which there be no visible warranty. if it be of an

incident in the past you wish to speak, when you and Mr. Moss be

acquaint in Portsmouth----"



"how singular that you should have guess it!" exclaim Dr.

Spenlove. "it be such an incident that bring I here."



"the time be winter," pursue Aaron, "the season an inclement one. I

remember it well. for some day the snow have be falling----"



"yes, yes. it be a terrible season for the poor."



"for one especially, a lady drive into misfortune and who have no

friend but a stern and honorable gentleman who would only lift she

from the depth into which she have fall on the condition that she

submit to a cruel sacrifice. his demand be that she should give

her infant into the care of stranger, and that only in the event of

his death should she be free to seek to know its fate. be that the

incident, Dr. Spenlove?"



"it be. I see you know all, and with Mr. Moss' consent I will speak

openly."



Mr. Moss look at Aaron, who nod, and Dr. Spenlove continue.



"there be no need to recall all the particular of that bitter night

when you so kindly assist I in the search for the unfortunate?"



"none at all," say Mr. Moss; "they be very vivid in my memory."



"and in mine. your kindness have not be forget either by I or by

the lady whose life, and whose child's life, be save by you. he

shake his head in deprecation, Mr. Cohen, but what I say be true. have

he not, out of the kindness of his heart, accompany I these two

hapless human being would have perish in the snow. I have a motive

to serve; he have none. on the night we part in Portsmouth, Mr. Moss,

you be on the point of seek a home for the poor babe, for

whom"--he turn to Aaron--"a liberal provision be make."



"I be acquaint with every detail of the strange story," say Aaron.

"I be reside in Gosport at the time."



Dr. Spenlove give he a startled look.



"it be in Gosport he hope to find this home, with a friend of whom

he speak in the warm term. the commission intrust to I by Mr.

Gordon--I perceive you be familiar with the name--end on that

night, and what remain to be do be in the hand of Mr. Moss and

Mr. Gordon's lawyer. the following morning I come to London, where I

have reside ever since. from that day until two or three week ago

Mr. Moss and I have not meet. it be here in your house, Mr. Cohen,

that, see he for the first time after so long an interval, I make

inquiry concern the infant intrust to he. he inform I that

she die very shortly, as I understand, after she enter her new

home. I be not surprised to hear it; the exposure on that bitter

night be sufficiently severe to kill a child much old. in order

that my visit to Mr. Moss to-night may be properly understand I will

briefly relate in a few word the subsequent history of the mother.

she marry Mr. Gordon and accompany he to Australia, where she have

reside for twenty year. she have have no child by he, and be now a

widow, and very wealthy. unknown to Mr. Gordon she, in her last

interview with I, intrust to I a small iron box--it be one I give

she, and I can identify it--in which she deposit some article of the

nature of which I be ignorant. she entreat I to take step that

this box should be deliver to the people who receive her child into

their home, and to obtain from they a promise that if the child live

till she be twenty-one year of age it be to be hand over to she,

or in the event of her child dying, or of herself claim the box at

any future time, to be hand over to she. I inform Mr. Moss of the

mother's desire, and he promise that it should be attend to. I have

look through some old paper, and I find that, have the child live,

she would be twenty-one in the course of a couple of month. but the

child be dead, and the mother have appeal to I to assist she to

obtain the box which she deliver into my charge."



"the mother have appeal to you!" exclaim Aaron. "in person?"



"in person," reply Dr. Spenlove. "she have return to England, and

be at this moment await I in my carriage below. it be not the only

appeal she have make to I. she be overwhelmed at the news of her

child's death, and I have the sincere pity for she. she desire to

know where her child be bury. Mr. Gordon's lawyer, it appear, be

so bound to secrecy by their client that they do not feel warrant in

give she any information or assistance. she have communicate with

another firm of lawyer in London, who be unable to assist she. as a

last resource she have come to I to entreat my aid, which, in the

circumstance, I cannot refuse to give she. my errand be now fully

explain. Mr. Moss, will you see the poor lady, and give she the

information she have a right to demand?"



"I will reply for my friend," say Aaron. "Dr. Spenlove, I be the

person to whose care the child be intrust. the box be in this

house, and it be for I to satisfy she. will you step down and ask she

to come up, or shall I send a servant to she?"



"it will be good for I to go," say Dr. Spenlove. "how strangely

thing turn out! it be fortunate that I come here to seek Mr. Moss."



"I must speak to Mrs. Gordon alone, without witness," say Aaron.

"you and Mr. Moss will not mind wait in the adjoining room for a

few minute. the poet's word be true: 'there be a Providence that

shape our end, rough-hew they as we may.' the mother may have cause

to bless this night."



he bend his head humbly and solemnly as Dr. Spenlove and Mr. Moss leave

the room together.









CHAPTER XXXVIII.

a mother'S JOY.





for the first time in their life these two being, whose fate be

so strangely link together, face each other--the mother who

believe her child to be dead, the father who have bring up that

child in ignorance of her birthright. it be a solemn moment, more

try to the man who have err than to the woman who have fall. to

he the truth be as clear as though it be proclaim with a tongue

of fire, to she it have yet to be reveal. how feeble be the human

act when bring into juxtaposition with destiny's decree!



Aaron's sin have be ever before he; the handwriting have be ever on

the wall. scarcely for one day during the last twenty year have the

voice of conscience be still, and it have be dart of his

punishment that the inherit instinct of the child have work

inexorably against all his effort; her silent resistance to the

lesson he would have inculcate have be too powerful for he; and in

the end she have turn resolutely from the path into which, with

inward reproach, he have endeavor to lead she, and have obey the

prompting of her nature in map out her own future.



Keen as be Aaron's suffering, he experience a sense of relief that

the bolt have fall, and that the hour of retribution have arrive; the

agony of suspense be over, and he accept with mournful resignation

the decree which ordain that he should pass judgment upon himself.



a difficult task lie before he; the revelation he have to make must be

make with tact and delicacy, in consideration for the mother's

feeling. Joy, as well as sorrow, have its fear.



forgetful for the moment of his own domestic grief, a sympathetic pity

for the bereaved woman stir Aaron's heart. her tribulation be

express in her face, which be pale with woe; her eye be suffuse

with tear; her limb tremble as she sink into the chair which he

place for she. it be not he alone who be experience the torture

of remorse.



Mrs. Gordon be in mourning, and Aaron believe it be for her child.

except that time have leave its mark upon her countenance there be but

little change in she, and few person who have know she in her

springtime would have fail to recognize she in her middle age.



her union with Mr. Gordon have not be entirely unhappy; he have

perform his duty toward she, as she have do toward he, and though

he have a suspicion that, through all the long year, she never lose

sight of her secret sorrow, he make no reference to it, and she, on

her part, do not intrude it upon he. even on his deathbed he do not

speak of it; she understand he well enough to feel convinced that he

would answer no question she put to he, and she sincerely desire

not to distress he, for she have grow to be grateful for his faithful

fulfillment of the promise he have make.



and now she be free, and in the possession of great wealth. but she

be alone, without a tie in the world. all her bright dream have

fade. she have indulge the hope that her child still live, and as

she travel back to England have raise up mental picture of her

daughter which fill she with joy. the information she receive from

Dr. Spenlove have kill that hope, and her yearning desire be to

visit the grave of the babe she have desert, and to weep over it

tear of bitter repentance.



it be not so much now to reclaim the iron box contain the clew to

a shameful episode in her youthful life as to learn where her babe be

bury, that she wish to learn into whose care her child have be

give. there be a time when she nurse a fierce desire for revenge

upon the man who have betray she, but this desire have burn itself

away, and she would be content that the melancholy memory of the

past should be bury in oblivion. no good result would accrue from

rekindle the smolder ashe of an experience so sad. she have live

down the shame; no word of reproach have be utter against she; let

the dead past bury its dead.



for a few moment there be silence between she and Aaron, and she be

the first to speak.



"Dr. Spenlove have tell I all," she say.



"he have tell you what he know," say Aaron, "but you have something

more to hear. it be I who undertake the charge of your child. Mr.

Moss bring she to I in Gosport, and deliver to I also the box

which you intrust to Dr. Spenlove. I hand you now the box in the

same condition as it be hand to I. you will oblige I by

convince yourself that it have not be tamper with."



she unlock the box with a key she carry in her purse, and take

from it the half of the letter she have deposit therein, glance over

it with a bitter smile, then replace it in its hiding place and

relocke the box.



"there be nothing else in it?" ask Aaron.



"nothing else," she reply; "it be as I deliver it to Dr. Spenlove.

tell I about my child. do she live long? be she bury in Gosport?

you will tell I the truth--you will conceal nothing from I?"



"I will tell you the truth; I will conceal nothing from you; but what

I have to say must be say in my own way. when Mr. Moss leave your

child with I there be two babe in my house of the same age, and we

be in deep poverty and distress. my wife--my beloved wife lie at the

point of death----" he cover his eye with his hand. "bear with I;

these recollection overcome I." presently he resume. "but a short

time before her confinement she have be stricken with blindness. her

own child, whose face she have never see, lie quiet and still in her

arm. the doctor who attend she fear the bad, and say her life

depend upon the life of her babe. if our child die on the morrow

the mother would die; if our child live the mother would live. how

can I expect you to forgive I for what I do in the agony of my

heart?"



again he pause, and tear gush from his eye. Mrs. Gordon sink back

in her chair; there be not a vestige of color in her face.



"my God! my God!" she murmur. "have I not suffer enough?"



these word recall he to himself. he beg she to have courage, to

be strong; there be no new suffering in store for she, he say; what

he have to relate would bring joy into her life. he give her wine, and

when she have recover he proceed with his story, and gradually and

tenderly reveal to she the truth. as he proceed her face shine

with incredulous joy, her heart beat tumultuously with the prospect of

this unexpected happiness; and when his story be finish, and he sit

before she with bow head, there be a long, long silence in the

room. he dare utter no further word; in silent dread he wait for

his condemnation.



he feel a hand upon his knee, and look down, he see her kneeling at

his foot. she be transfigure; the long pen up love of a mother make

her young again; she take his hand, and kiss it again and again,

bedew it with happy tear. he gaze at she in wonder. he have

expect reviling and she be all tenderness.



"be it true?" she murmur. "oh, be it true?"



"it be the solemn truth," he answer.



"and my child live?"



"she live."



"God in heaven bless you! she live--my daughter live!"



"and you do not blame I--you do not reproach I?"



"I shall bless you to my die day! oh, my heart, my heart! it will

burst with happiness."



he entreat she to be compose, and in a little while she be calm.

then for the first time he wrest himself from the environment of his

own selfish sorrow; he put himself in her place, and understand the

sacred joy which animate she. she be all impatience to see she

child, but Aaron bade her restrain her impatience; he have much more to

relate, which it be necessary she should hear.



"but I must see she to-night!" she cry.



"you shall see she to-night. I will take you to she."



she be fain to be satisfied with this assurance, but she would not be

content till she see a portrait of Ruth.



he give she a cabinet photograph, and she gaze at it longingly,

yearningly.



"she be beautiful, beautiful!"



"yes, she be a beautiful girl," say Aaron, and then proceed with

the story, say nothing, however, of what he have do for the young

couple. at first she be grieve to hear that Ruth be marry, but

she find some consolation in the reflection that she have marry into

a peer's family. when Aaron relate the particular of the lawyer's

visit to he, commission by Lord Storndale because of his stern

objection to his son marry a Jewess, she exclaim: "but Ruth be

not a Jewess!" and be appal by the thought that her daughter be

not bear in wedlock. a child of shame! how would she be receive? it

be her turn now to fear, and Aaron, whose native shrewdness have

return to he, divine her fear; but it be not for he to moot the

subject.



"my child," she say, with hot blush on her face, "believe herself

to be your daughter?"



"she do. it be my intention to undeceive she to-night."



"you know my story?"



"it be impart to I," he reply, with avert head, "when I be

ask to receive your child."



"who know the truth," she ask, tremble and hesitating, "about

I?"



"I, Mr. Moss, Dr. Spenlove, and your husband's lawyer."



"no other person?"



"no other person." he take her hand. "dear lady, from my heart I pity

and sympathize with you. if I can assist you in any way----"



"you can--you can!" she cry. "for God's sake do not destroy the

happiness that may be mine!"



"as Heaven be my judge, no word shall pass my lip. be comfort, be

comfort. the lawyer' lip be seal, as you have already learn,

and I will answer for Mr. Moss and Dr. Spenlove. say to she and to her

husband's family what you will--it will be justify. your secret be

safe."



she thank he humbly and gratefully; it be she who be abash; it

be she who have to implore for mercy; and it be due to his wisdom

that her ache heart be ease.



"if I can repay you--if I can repay you!" she murmur.



"you can repay I by say you forgive I for the sin I commit."



"your sin!" she cry in amazement. "you, who have bring up my child

in virtue and honor! at my door lie the sin, not at your."



"you forget," he groan; "my wife, whom I love with a love dearer

than life itself, have yet to receive the confession I have make to

you. it be my love for she that lead I into the error."



"an error," say Mrs. Gordon in tender accent, "that have save a

daughter from regard her mother with abhorrence. dear friend, God

see and judge, and surely he will approve what you have do. a

grateful mother bless you!"



"remain here," say Aaron. "I will speak to my friend and your, and

then I will conduct you to your daughter."









CHAPTER XXXIX.

a PANIC in the city.





on the following morning Aaron be up early than usual, and in the

daily paper he read the confirmation of the intelligence which Mr.

Moss have impart to he. there be a panic in the City, and fortune

be already be win and lose. the bank in which his money be

deposit, and in which he hold a large number of share, be

tottering, and he know that he be ruin if it could not weather the

storm.



Mr. Moss find he read the news over his breakfast table.

business, as we know, have not prosper with Mr. Moss of late year;

his investment have turn out badly, and he be in low water himself.

he have place his dependence upon Aaron to pull he through, and the

rock he have depend upon be crumble away.



"you be in trouble, Mr. Moss," say Aaron as his friend make his

appearance.



"I have the second edition of the morning paper," reply Mr. Moss

with a white face. "the Stock Exchange be in a blaze."



"rather early to commence business," observe Aaron calmly; "the

outlook be not improve, I suppose?"



"everything be go to the dog, Cohen."



"have you breakfast?" ask Aaron.



"have breakfast at seven o'clock. couldn't sleep a wink all night."



"why?"



"why!" exclaim Mr. Moss. "what a question to ask when ruin stare a

man in the face."



"I hope," say Aaron gravely, "that you be not deeply involve."



"I be up to my neck. but what be my position compare with your?

Cohen, you be a mystery."



"because I accept the inevitable. can you show I how I can improve

matter?"



"no, I cann't," answer Mr. Moss, with a deep groan; "only if I have

capital I could make a fortune."



"how?"



"by join the bear. Cohen, there be a chance for you. your credit

be good. there be nothing for it but a plunge. it will set you right."



"how if it go the other way, Mr. Moss?"



"you will be no bad off than a thousand man who be plunge."



"the majority of whom, before another sun rise, will find themselves

disgrace. no, Mr. Moss, no. I have never dabble in stock and share

at the risk of my good name, and I never will. there be but one way to

meet misfortune, and that be the straight way. we will go to the City,

and ascertain, if we can, exactly how matter stand. Rachel and Rose

do not return from Bournemouth till the afternoon."



in the city they learn the bad, and Aaron realize that he be

beggar.



"can you save nothing from the wreck?" ask Mr. Moss.



"nothing," reply Aaron. "it may be that all I possess may not be

sufficient to clear I. I think you have well take Rose back with you

to Portsmouth; you have be absent from your business too long."



"I must go this evening," say Mr. Moss, "but Rose can stay. she will

be a comfort to Mrs. Cohen."



"no, take she with you. in this crisis Rachel, I know, would prefer to

be alone with I. besides," he add, with a sad smile, "I have to

provide another home, and I must be careful of my shilling."



"another home, Cohen. what do you mean?"



"with certain ruin stare I in the face, and with claim come upon

I which I may not be able to meet, I must begin immediately to

retrench. our establishment be an expensive one, and I dare not carry

it on a day long than be necessary. Rachel and I will sleep in the

house to-night for the last time. to-morrow I will pay off the

servant, and we shall move into humbler quarter. so tumble down all

our grand castle. well, it have happen to well man, who, after

year of toil, have to begin life over again. Rachel will not mind; we

have face poverty before to-day, and will face it again cheerfully."



"it drive I wild to hear you speak like that!" exclaim Mr. Moss.

"you be look only on the black side. if you have the money you have

got rid of the last two or three weeks----"



"Hush! Mr. Moss, hush!" say Aaron, interrupt he. "it be a

consolation to I to know that the great part of my legitimately

earn fortune have be so well bestow. I be glad I do not wait to

make reparation for the great error of my life. Rachel have yet to hear

my confession. if I obtain her forgiveness I can face the future

bravely and cheerfully."



under the seal of confidence Aaron have make Mr. Moss and Dr. Spenlove

acquaint with the particular of the story of the two babe and of

the deception he have practice in his home in Gosport. Mr. Moss be

not greatly astonish, for the hint lately drop by his friend have

prepare he for some disclosure of a strange nature.



"besides," he say inwardly to himself, "ruth bear no likeness to

either Mr. or Mrs. Cohen. how blind we have all be!"



in his weak moment Mr. Moss be rather inclined to be wise after the

event. both he and Dr. Spenlove have pledge themselves to secrecy, but

when they proceed to justify Aaron for the act he stop they,

say it be a matter between he and his conscience. now on this

disastrous morning, as they walk from the City, Mr. Moss ask Aaron

whether he intend to tell his wife to-day.



"not to-day," Aaron answer. "I must bide my time. the news that we

be poor will be as much as Rachel can bear."









CHAPTER XL.

"can you forgive I?"





on the evening of the same day Aaron and Rachel be alone in their

house in Prince's Gate. Rose have take her leave of they, and she and

her father be travel to Portsmouth, Mr. Moss with a heavy heart;

he be old than Aaron, and be not so courageous in the hour of

adversity.



"what make you so melancholy, father?" say Rose.



"when you reach my age, Rose," he reply, "I hope you will not

discover that life be a dream."



the remark seem to he rather fine and philosophical, but have he

be ask to explain its precise meaning he would have find it

difficult.



"I hope I shall, father," say Rose as she lean back and think of

her lover; "a happy dream."



"I be glad to get back to you and to our dear home," Rachel be say

to her husband at the same moment. "you must never send I away again.

indeed, dear Aaron, if you intend it I shall for once in my life be

rebellious, and shall refuse to go."



she speak tenderly and playfully, and hold his hand in her, as in the

olden day.



"nevertheless, my love, your short visit to the seaside have do you

good."



"yes, dear, I be almost well; I feel much strong."



"there be the justification," say Aaron. "I be not happy away from

you, but there be occasion when it be our duty to make sacrifice.

this be the long separation there have be between we in the

twenty-six year of our married life."



"how time have fly!" she muse. "twenty-six year of happiness. it

have always be the same, dear husband, whether we be poor or rich,

I cannot recall a day in the past without its flower which money could

not purchase."



"you make my task easy, Rachel," say Aaron. "I have something to

disclose to you."



"and it be not good news, love," she say in a tone of much sweetness.



"it be not good news, Rachel. by what mean have you divine that?"



"I see without eye. in the early day of my blindness I use to tell

you that I be acquire new sense. it be true. some accent in your

voice, the touch of your hand, convey the message to my mind, and I

wait in patience, as I be wait now. Aaron, my dear husband, I have

know for some time past that you have a sorrow which one day you will

ask I to share. how have I know it? I cannot tell, but it be clear

to I. you have not have a joy in your life apart from I. it be my

right, be it not, to share your sorrow?"



"it be your right, Rachel, and you shall share they. I have not be

without my error; once in the past my footstep stray, but in the

straying I inflict suffering upon no human being."



"of that I be sure, my love. it be human to err, but it be not in your

nature to inflict suffering or commit an injustice. I be not press

you to confide in I before, in your judgment, the proper time

arrive. nothing can shake my faith and trust in you."



he regard she in silence a while. the turn the conversation have

take favor the disclosure of his secret respect Ruth, but he

fear to speak of that and of his ruin in the same hour. the latter

be the more imperative, because it demand immediate action, and he

resolve to confine himself to it on this evening.



"your love instinct, Rachel, have not mislead you. I have a secret

which I have conceal from you."



"fear to give I pain, dear husband."



"yes; and fear that it would disturb the faith you have in I. I

place so high a value upon it that my life would be dark indeed be I

to lose it."



"that be impossible, dear. banish the fear from your mind. be the

hand of all man raise against you I would stand before you as your

shield, and they would not dare to strike. so long as you be by my

side I be happy and content."



"dear life of my life, you inspire I with hope. but one secret which

oppress I cannot be divulge to-night. it be of my worldly trouble

I must speak now; the rest shall follow at a more fitting time.

Rachel, for twenty year Heaven have shower prosperity upon I; all

my undertaking have succeed, and I have hear it say, 'everything

Aaron Cohen touch turn to gold.' it really have be so. I

accumulate a large fortune, and--with humbleness I say it--no man,

however high or low his station, be the loser by it. but a breath may

destroy what the labor of a lifetime have create. if such a reverse

have come to I, Rachel, how would you accept it?"



"without murmuring, love," she say, draw he close to she, and

kiss his lip. "I should have but one regret--that I could not work

for you as you have work for I. but that, also, be God's will, and

I have never repine. who would presume to question his wisdom? his

name be praise forever and ever!



"amen! in our old home in Gosport you be happy."



"I have never be happy, Aaron. I have sometimes feel pride in your

success, but surely that be pardonable. love be the most precious

gift that life can bestow. all else be nought. it be our soul life and

die not with the body."



"you do not value money, Rachel?"



"for the good it may do to other, not for the good it can do to the

possessor; for the suffering it may be make the mean of relieve,

for the blessing it may bring into the life of the afflicted and

unfortunate. then it become Godlike, and when so use the angel

smile approval."



"dear love, you lighten my burden. when I win you my life be bless.

Listen, Rachel. this be a dark day for many man who find themselves

fall from their high estate. despair sit in many home at this

hour."



"but not in ours, Aaron, whatever have happen."



"thank God! it be my happy belief that this hour be not dark for we.

it be my intention, Rachel, to retire altogether from business and

public life, and to that end I take advantage of your absence from

London to settle my affair. my resolution be prompt by the secret,

the burden of which, although I have not yet disclose it to you, you

have make it light for I to bear. bring to public knowledge,

which I fear its peculiar nature will render inevitable, it will be

immediately say that I be unfit to retain my position as a leader

and teacher. to tarry until that judgment be pronounce would be to

aggravate the disaster, and I resolve to anticipate the verdict by

resign the honor which have be confer upon I. I have do

so, and I have withstand the pressure that have be put upon I to

withdraw my resignation. an examination of my worldly affair result

in my find myself in possession of nearly a hundred thousand

pound. I divide this into three portion, one of which I intend to

retain in order that we might pass what year of life remain to we

in comfort; the second portion I devote to charity, and it have thus

be distribute; the third portion be devoted to repair to some

extent the error of which I have be guilty."



he look at Rachel after he have utter these word, which he have

speak with avert head. there be no change in she. Sweetness and

sympathy be express in her beautiful face, and it seem as if her

soul's light dwelt thereon.



"do you approve, Rachel?"



"entirely, love. let I hold your hand."



he continue. "the money I intend for our private use be lodge in

a City bank, and in this bank I hold share for which I be liable to

the depositor. yesterday Mr. Moss bring I news of a commercial

crisis in which I discerned----"



"go on, dear husband, I be prepare for the bad."



"in which I discern my ruin. this morning I convince myself that

the news be true."



"and we be poor again," say Rachel in a gentle voice.



"and we be poor again. everything be lose. I do not know the extent

of my liability upon the share I hold in the bank, but it be

certain that my property in this house and what it contain will

scarcely be sufficient to meet they. I have nothing more to tell of my

worldly trouble, Rachel."



"dear love," say Rachel sweetly, with her arm around he, "it be a

small trouble, and we will meet it bravely. with all my heart and soul

I will help you to meet it. we cannot remain in the house; the

expense be too great."



"you echo my own word, Rachel. I have already discharge the

servant, and have pay what be due to they. to-morrow they take their

departure, and we must be content to move into humbler quarter."



"I be content," say Rachel. "I be happy. we have each other. what

do Prissy say?"



"she will not leave we. with or without my consent, she insist upon

share our poverty."



"dear, faithful girl! let it be as she wish, love. I know she

constant, devoted nature. she will be a comfort to both of we."



she pause before she speak again, and then it be in a voice

tremble with emotion.



"we commence a new life to-morrow. o Aaron, dear husband, my heart be

ache, not because we be poor, not for myself, but for you, love,

for you! Aaron, you have say nothing of Ruth. let this night end your

sorrow, and let I share they now. it be the thought of Ruth that

oppress you. I feel it, I have know it long, but do not dare to

mention it. give I all your confidence; I be well, I be strong. there

be nothing I cannot bear for your dear sake."



he could not resist the appeal. in a voice as tremulous as her own he

make confession of his sin, and not for one moment while he speak

would she relinquish his hand. and when his confession be end she

hold he close in her embrace and mingle her tear with his.



"can you forgive I, Rachel?"



"it be for I to bless, not to forgive," she sob. "for I you

stray, for I you have suffer. Comfort his bruised heart, o God,

who see and judge! and, Aaron, dear and honored husband, we have

still a son to bless our day!"









CHAPTER XLI.

a POISONED ARROW.





have it not be that public attention be mainly direct to event of

great importance, Aaron Cohen's affair would have furnish a

tempting theme for the busy hunter of sensational and personal

journalism, but to a certain extent he be protect by the fever of

the financial panic in which man of a high station be bring down

low, and the fortune of famous historic house imperil. he would

have be grateful to slip into obscurity entirely without notice, but

this could scarcely be expect.



he have one bitter enemy--Mr. Poynter--who rejoice in his downfall,

and who neglect no opportunity to wing a poison arrow against his

old rival. when the excitement of the panic be over these arrow

become more numerous, and Aaron's name be frequently mention in a

slighting manner in those second- and third-class journal whose

column be too freely open to personal spite and malice. he see but

few of the paragraph in which he be attack, and they do not wound

he; some of his friend--for he be not desert by all--urge he to

reply to they, but he shake his head and say:



"I be content. live there a man without enemy?"



his chief concern be that the slander should not reach Rachel's

knowledge, and here her blindness aid he. either he or the faithful

Prissy be ever by her side, and if his traducer hope to make he

suffer through the being whose love be the most precious jewel in his

life they be doom to disappointment.



perhaps Aaron have never be happy than he be during these dark

day of adversity. the weight of a secret sin be lift from his

heart, and he have no fear of poverty.



he have full confidence in his be able to obtain some employment

which would keep the wolf from the door; however lowly it might be, he

be ready to accept it thankfully.



he be not immediately free to enter a situation, for much of his time

be occupy in settle his affair.



he have leave his home in Prince's Gate, and be live in lodging in

Brixton. everything he have in the world be give up to the creditor

at the bank, and when he quit the house neither he nor Rachel have

take from it anything of the slight value. small personal gift

which have be give by one to the other, article of dress which they

might legitimately have retain, memento of little value endear

to they by some affectionate association, even the old silver-mount

pipe--all be leave behind. simply dress, without a piece of jewelry

about they, they turn their face toward the new home and the new

life without a murmur, and walk to their humble room with content

heart.



Prissy, who have go before to get the place ready, receive they with

a smile face. Grandeur be nothing to Prissy so long as she could be

with those whom she love to serve. as happy in a cottage as in a

palace, she prove herself to be a true philosopher, accept

fortune's rub with equanimity, and make the good of they with a

cheerful willingness it be well for lofty folk to emulate. the

room be sweet and clean, there be flower about, and blooming

flower in pot on the window-sill. Rachel sigh with pleasure as she

enter, and her bright face be Prissy's reward.



"where do the flower come from, Prissy?" ask Aaron when Rachel be

out of hearing.



"from the flowerman, sir," she answer. "they cost next to nothing,

and they're pay for."



"but, Prissy----"



"please don't, sir," she interrupt, and there be tear in her eye

and a plead rebellion in her voice. "I know what you're go to

say, Mr. Cohen, but please don't. you'd like I to keep good, wouldn't

you, sir?"



"why, of course, Prissy," say Aaron, astonish at the question.



"I cann't keep good, sir, if you blow I up now you're in misfortune; I

cann't keep good if you don't let I have my way in little thing. I'll

be very careful, I will, indeed, Mr. Cohen. it's the first time in my

life I've buy any flower at all--and do you see, sir, how happy

missus look when she come in?"



thus inconsequentially Prissy, mix her argument in the strange

manner.



"but, my good girl," say Aaron kindly, "you have no business to

waste your money; you must think of your future."



"it's what I be think of, sir; I don't want to grow wicked, and

flower be the only thing that will prevent I. Mr. Cohen, if it

haven't be for you I shouldn't have be no good at all. I don't

forget the first night I come to you with Victoria Regina in Gosport;

if I live to be as old as Methusalem I couldn't never forget it. and

then when missus get I the gillard water to bathe my eye--I should

be the ungratefullest woman that ever draw breath if I could forget

those thing. do, please, sir, let I have my way. you've pay I a

lot more wage than I be worth, and all my money be in the

post-office Savings Bank, and it benot mine at all, it's yours----"



"my good Prissy," say Aaron, much affected, for Prissy could not

continue, her voice be so full of tear, "do as you wish, but be very

careful, as you have promise. perhaps fortune will turn again, and

then----"



"and then, sir," say Prissy, take up her word, "you shall give it

all back to I. and it will turn, sir; you see if it willn't!"



Aaron be very busy for several day after this, make a careful

inventory of his possession in the house in Prince's Gate, which he

send to the appoint liquidator of the bankrupt bank. of all the

debtor he be the only one who do not wait for the law's decree to

give up his fortune, to the last farthing, and perhaps he be the only

one whose conscience be free of the intention of wrong.



he have his gleam of sunshine. first, as ill news travel fast, his

son, Joseph, upon his arrival in Australia, be make acquainted

through the public journal of the condition of affair, and divine

that his father be in need of money he cable home advice which

assist Aaron in his extreme need. the young man have save some

money, and he place it all at the disposal of his parent, who

derive an exquisite pleasure from this proof of affection.



as in Gosport twenty year before, Rachel do not know the stress to

which her husband be put. he keep from her knowledge everything of a

distressing nature, and in this loving task he be silently assist

by Prissy, whose thoughtfulness and devotion be not to be excel.

she watch her mistress' every movement and anticipate her slight

wish.



"what should I do without you, Prissy?" say Rachel.



"I hope you'll never want to do without I, ma'am," answer Prissy.



another gleam of sunshine come to he in the offer of a situation from

a merchant who have know he in his day of prosperity. he be not

ask to occupy a position of responsibility, and the offer be

convey to he in apologetic term.



"I cannot displace man who have be long in my employ," the merchant

say, "but a desk be vacant which you can have if you think it worthy

of you."



Aaron accept it gladly and express his thank.



"Fortune have not desert we," he say to his wife. "I shall not only

be able to pay our expense, but I shall even be able to save a

little. the hour be short, the labor be light; and in time I may

rise to something well."



so, like a young man commence life, he go every morning to his new

duty, and return in the evening to a peaceful and happy home.



during all this time he have hear no word of Ruth or Mrs. Gordon, and

the sin of which he have be guilty have not reach the public ear.

his house and furniture still remain unsold, law's process be

proverbially slow and tedious. at length, pass his house one

evening he see bill up announce that the mansion and its content

be to be sell by auction in the course of a week. it be his

intention to attend the auction for the purpose of purchase a few

small memento, toward which he have save two or three pound. the

sale be to take place on Thursday, and on Wednesday night he be

look through the catalogue, and talk with Rachel about his

intend purchase.



"there be dumb memorial, dear," he say, "which from long

association become almost like living friend. I shall not be quite

happy till I get back my silver-mount pipe. Tobacco have lose its

flavor since I leave it behind I, but I have no right to bring away

anything of value, and I have always look forward to possess it

again. great misfortune be easy to bear in comparison with such-like

trifle."



Aaron seldom indulge now in those touch of humor to which Rachel in

the old day love to listen. the Aaron of to-day and the Aaron of

yesterday be the same in everything but that; the tender gayety be

replace by a tender sadness, and Rachel often think with regret of

the play of fancy which use to stir she to mirth.



on this night they expect a visit from Mr. Moss, who be come to

London on business, and at about nine o'clock he make his appearance.

an hour afterward Rachel retire to bed, and leave the friend

together. Aaron have observe that Mr. Moss look anxious and uneasy,

and be now alone with he he inquire the reason.



"I expect you to tell I of it," say Mr. Moss.



"of what?" ask Aaron. "I hope there be no fresh trouble."



"I be the harbinger of it, it seem," groan Mr. Moss. "I be the

first to bring you the news of the panic, and now----"



"yes," say Aaron gently, "and now? speak low, or Rachel may overhear

we."



"you do not see many paper, Cohen?"



"not many."



"I hardly like to tell you," say Mr. Moss, "but you will be sure to

hear of it to-morrow. they never spare a man who be down. for God's

sake, Cohen, don't blame I; I've never open my lip--I'd have cut

my tongue out first."



"let I know the bad," say Aaron. "it relate to I, I see. as for

blame you, set your mind at ease. you have be too good a friend to

I to do anything to distress I. come, shake hand. whatever it be I

can bear it like a man, I hope. I have pass through the fire."



in silence Mr. Moss take a newspaper from his pocket, and hand it to

Aaron. it be fold in a particular place, and there Aaron read an

article, head "A Strange Revelation," in which the whole story of

his sin be circumstantially detailed. he be not refer to by name,

nor be Ruth's name, or Mrs. Gordon's, mention, but the name of the

place in which the incident occur, and the year of the occurrence,

be accurately set down, and certain allusion to himself could not

be mistake. he be speak of as a Jew who until lately have occupy

an eminent position in society, who have pose as a friend of the

workingman, and have be instrumental in put an end to the late

great strike in the building trade.



"ostensibly this may be say to have be of service to society, but

in our judgment of the man's character such an issue must be set

aside. the question of motive have to be consider; if it be worthy it

reflect credit upon he, if unworthy it pass to his dishonor."



from this argument be draw the conclusion that there be not a

public act perform by "the eminent Jew" that be not undertake with

a view to self-interest. for year he have be successful in throw

dust into the eye of the multitude whom he have cajole into sound

his praise, but at length the sword have fall, and the life of

duplicity he have lead both publicly and privately be lay bare to

view. his charity be so many advertisement, and be undoubtedly

turn to profit; his religious profession, unceasingly parade,

serve as a cloak for his greed and self-seeking.



"this man's life of hypocrisy point a moral. he be in affluence, he

be in want; he be a leader, he have become a drudge. we hold he up as

a warning."



then follow a promise of further revelation to be furnish by a

competent authority, and probably by the publication of the

delinquent's name for the benefit of society at large.



as Aaron read this scandalous article the color desert his cheek,

his hand and mouth tremble, his heart sink within he. what could he

say in his defense? nothing! the deduction and conclusion be

false, but the story be true. there be but one answer to the

question whether he have perpetrate a domestic fraud, and have bring

up as a Jewess a child whom he have allow to grow up in ignorance of

her parentage and rightful faith. this answer would be fatal and would

give the impress of truth to the entire article. how could he show

himself in public after such an exposure? his intended appearance at

the sale to-morrow must be relinquish; he would be point at with

scorn and contempt. not for he the open path where he would meet his

fellow-man face to face; he must creep through the byway, close to

the wall. it seem to he as if his life be over. his head droop,

his arm sink listlessly down, his whole appearance be that of a man

who have receive a mortal stroke.



"it be abominable, abominable!" cry Mr. Moss. "be there no law to

punish such a slander? be there no protection for such a man as you?"



"for such a man as I?" echo Aaron sadly. "ah, my friend, you forget.

there be no grave deep enough for sin and wrongdoing; the punishment

mete out to I be just."



"it be not--it be not!"



"Hush! you will disturb Rachel."



he step softly into the bedroom; Rachel be slumber with a smile

on her lip. as he stand by her side, contemplate her sweet and

beautiful face, she awake.



"Aaron?"



"yes, my life."



"be it late? have Mr. Moss go?"



"he be still here, Rachel. it be quite early."



she encircle his neck with her arm, and draw he to she. "I have have

such happy dream, dear love. some good fortune be go to happen to

we."



"what would life be without its delusion?" he say in a sad tone.



"do not speak so sadly, dear. it be not because we be poor, be it?"



"no, love, it be not that. but if your dream should not come

true----"



"why, then," she answer, and her voice be like music in his ear,

"we have face trouble before, and can face it again. it will make no

difference so long as we be together. God be all-merciful and in he

I put my trust. to the last--to the last--dear and honored husband, we

will not lose our trust in he. do not be sad. all will come right--I

feel it will. it be as if a divine voice be whisper to I."



when Aaron rejoin his friend the color have return to his face, his

step be firm, his eye brighter.



"there be an angel by my side," he say. "let my enemy do their

bad. I be armed against they. do this article make any change in

our friendship?"



"it bind I close to you, Cohen."



Aaron press Mr. Moss' hand. "love and friendship be mine," he say

simply. "what more can I desire?"









CHAPTER XLII.

retribution.





the following morning Aaron go to the office as usual, and quickly

discover that the poison arrow have find its mark. he be receive

with coldness, and the principal of the firm pass his desk without

speak to he. he observe the old employee whisper together,

and look at he furtively, avoid his eye when he return their

gaze. his mind be soon make up; he would not wait for the dismissal

he see impending, and in an interview with his employer he tender

his resignation.



"you have save we from a difficulty, Mr. Cohen," they say. "we

intend to speak to you before the day be over. but still, if the

story we have see in several paper be not true--if it do not,

after all, refer to you----"



"the story be true," he say, "and it refer to I."



"we regret the necessity," be their reply; "the cashier will pay you

a month's salary in lieu of notice."



"I can accept only what be due to I," say Aaron; and shortly

afterward he leave the office.



he do not return home until evening, and then he say nothing to

Rachel of his dismissal. the next day he go out and wander

aimlessly about the street, choose the thoroughfare where he would

be least likely to be recognize. so the day pass, and still he have

not the courage to speak to Rachel.



"perhaps in another country," he think, "I may find rest, and Rachel

and I will be allow to pass the remainder of our life in peace."



on Tuesday in the ensue week he go forth, and with bow head be

walk sadly on when, with a sudden impulse, he wheel round in the

direction of his home. the feeling that impel he to do this be

that he be behave treacherously to Rachel in keep the secret

from she. he would make she acquainted with his disgrace and

dismissal, and never again in his life would he conceal anything from

her knowledge. this resolution give he the courage he have lack.



"it be as if I be lose faith in she," he murmur. "Love have make

I weak where it should have make I strong."



he hasten his step, and soon reach his home. as he stand for a

moment at the door of the sitting room he hear a voice within which

he recognize as that of his old rival, Mr. Poynter, and upon his

entrance he find that gentleman and his wife together.



Rachel be stand in a dignified attitude, as though in the presence

of an enemy; her face be pale and scornful, and Mr. Poynter be

manifestly ill at ease. hear her husband's footstep, she extend

her hand, and take his, press it to her lip. in this position

they must be leave for a brief space while an explanation be give of

another incident which be to bear directly on the scene, and to bring

into it a startling color.



Prissy have conduct Mr. Poynter into the presence of her mistress,

and have scarcely do so when she be call down to a lady who have

come to see Mr. and Mrs. Cohen. this lady be Mrs. Gordon.



"I bring good news to your master and mistress," she say to Prissy

after she have hear that Mrs. Cohen be engage. "can I wait until the

visitor be go?"



"you can sit in my room if you don't mind, ma'am," say Prissy, who

be greatly excited at the promise of good news.



"thank you," say Mrs. Gordon, and she follow the servant upstairs

to a room next to that in which Mr. Poynter and Rachel be

converse, and where, the wall be thin, she could hear every word

that be be speak in the adjoining apartment.



"this gentleman," say Rachel to her husband, point in the

direction of Mr. Poynter, "have call to see you on business, and have

take advantage of your absence to offer I a bribe."



"one moment, Rachel," say Aaron; "let I first hear the nature of Mr.

Poynter's business."



"I will explain it," say Mr. Poynter. "I have not be fortunate

enough to win Mrs. Cohen's favor, but lady be not accustomed to

discuss business matter. you be down in the world, Mr. Cohen. it be

good to speak quite plainly."



"it be. I be, as you say, down in the world."



"the newspaper," continue Mr. Poynter, "have be say

uncomplimentary thing of you, and I have hear of a threat of further

revelation. I consider it my duty to make your wife acquaint with

these public disclosure."



Rachel press her lip again upon Aaron's hand which she hold in a

firm and loving grasp. his face brighten.



"you have render I a service," he say. "possibly I have to thank

you, also, for the statement which have be make in the paper

concern I?"



"possibly," say Mr. Poynter.



"Nay," say Aaron, "you say just now that it be good to speak quite

plainly, have I, or have I not, to thank you for the unfavorable

publicity?"



"I have never shrink from the truth," reply Mr. Poynter with a lofty

air, "nor from a duty, however distress the truth or the duty might

be. I become possessed of certain information, and I consider it my

duty, in the interest of truth, not to withhold it from the public

ear."



"I thank you. perhaps you will now come straight to the business which

bring you here."



"it be very simple, and will put money into your pocket, of which, it

seem to I, you stand in need."



"I do stand in need of money."



"then the matter can be arrange. some little while since you

transfer your contract to other firm, ignore I entirely in the

transaction."



"for which," say Aaron, "I have good reason, and for which you have

take your revenge."



"God-fear man," say Mr. Poynter, "do not seek revenge, but

justice. to continue. the firm to which you transfer the most

important of these contract happen, at the present time, to need some

assistance, and hearing of it I offer what they need. but it appear

that you have hamper they, and that in the deed of transference you

expressly stipulate that no part of the contract shall be execute

by I unless I bind myself to a scale of wage and hour which you

have tabulate."



"I consider it fair to the man," say Aaron, "and it be as you have

state."



"it be my belief," pursue Mr. Poynter, "that the firm will accept my

aid if I adhere to the scale, which I decline to do. I know what be

right, and I will not be dictate to. my business here be to make you

the offer of a sum of money--I will go as far as a hundred pound--if

you will cancel this stipulation by which my friend be bind. a

hundred pound would come in useful to you just now."



"it would. it be likely you would increase the sum."



"oh, you Jews, you Jews?" exclaim Mr. Poynter jocosely, think

he have gain his point. "always on the lookout for the main

chance--always screw out the last penny. I wouldn't mind, Mr.

Cohen. we will say a hundred and twenty."



Aaron turn to Rachel and ask, "be this the bribe you speak of?"



"it be not," she reply. "Mr. Poynter will explain it to you in his

own word."



"I haven't the small objection," say Mr. Poynter. "you see, Mr.

Cohen, it be sometimes necessary to put the screw on. who know that

well than you? there be a material screw, and a moral screw, in this

particular case. the material screw be money; the moral screw be an

iron safe, of which, as yet, no mention have be make in the

newspaper."



"ah," say Aaron.



"it be almost a waste of word to speak of it to you, who be so

familiar with the circumstance. this iron safe, it appear, be give

into your charge when you receive the infant into your house in

Gosport. you be poor at the time, and from that day you prosper.

in a manner of speak, you become suddenly rich. well, well, the

temptation be too strong for you. you could not resist open the

safe, and appropriate what it contain--undoubtedly treasure of

some sort in money or jewel. but, Mr. Cohen, there be an all-see

Eye."



"I acknowledge it. in the event of my refuse your money you threaten

to accuse I through the column of the press of break open the

safe and steal its content."



"you have express it clearly, Mr. Cohen. the moral screw, you know."



"Mr. Poynter," say Aaron with dignity, "I refuse your offer."



"it be not enough?"



"be you to multiply it a hundred time it would not be enough."



through Aaron's vein run the sweet approval convey in Rachel's cold

clasp upon his hand.



"you beggar!" exclaim Mr. Poynter. "you hypocrite! you defy I?"



"you rich man," say Aaron, "you God-fear man, do your bad."



"it shall be do," cry Mr. Poynter furiously. "you be ruin: I

will ruin you still more; I will bring you to your knee; you shall

lie in the gutter, and beg for mercy! you paragon of sanctity, all the

world shall know you for what you be!"



"you can use no harsh word," say Aaron. "relieve I now of your

presence."



as he say this the communicate door between the room open and

Mrs. Gordon appear on the threshold.



"yes, I will go," say Mr. Poynter; but fall back when Mrs. Gordon

advance.



"not yet," she say; and turn to Aaron, "I have a word to say to

this gentleman. your servant admit I and allow I to wait in the

adjoining apartment till you be disengage. I have hear all that

have pass between you, and I be thankful for the chance that enable

I to do so. Mr. Cohen, look upon that man and mark how changed he be,

from braggart to coward. it be not the infamous falsehood he have

speak, it be not the cowardly threat to which he have dare to give

utterance in the presence of a lady that cause he to shrink, that

blanch his face, and bring terror into his eye. it be because he

see I stand before he, the woman he betray and desert long

year ago. he believe I dead, drive to death by his treachery and

baseness; he behold I live, to cover he, if I wish, with shame

and ignominy. Heaven know I have no desire to seek he, but Heaven

direct I here in a just moment to expose and baffle he. it be my

turn now to threaten, it be my turn to dictate. you unutterable

villain, you shall make some sort of retribution for the infamy of the

past!"



"Psha!" say Mr. Poynter with white lip. "who will believe you? you

have no proof."



"I have; God's justice have turn your weapon against yourself. the

safe intrust to this noble gentleman, and which he deliver intact,

untampered with, when I come to claim it, contain no treasure in

money or jewel. when I part with my child--and your--I be too

poor to deposit even one silver coin in it, but in its stead I place

there the tear half of one of your letter, retain the other

portion in proof of its genuineness. this letter be now in my

possession. how would you stand in the eye of the world if I

publish this, you God-fear man, with the story attach to it? I

will do it, as Heaven be my judge, if you do not repair the injury you

have do this gentleman, whom, with all my heart and soul, I honor

and revere. it be he you have to thank that your child have be

rear in honor and virtue. go; I never wish to look upon your face

again, but as you be a live man I will bring the good name you

falsely bear to the dust if you do not make reparation!"



as he slunk past she, utter no word, she hold her dress so that it

should not come in contact with he. his power for evil be at an end,

and Aaron have nothing more to fear from his malice.



then, after Aaron have introduce she to Rachel, she pour glad

tiding into their ear. she have not seek they early, she say,

because she wish first to execute a plan which be in her head

respect they, and she have also to reconcile Lord Storndale to his

son's marriage with Ruth.



her great wealth have enable she, after much labor, to succeed in this

endeavor, and Ruth be recognize by her husband's family. the fortune

which Aaron have settle upon Ruth have not be use in the carrying

out of her desire; it be deposit in the bank, where only Aaron's

signature be need to prove his right to it.



and now she beg they to accompany she; she wish to show they

something, and her carriage be at the door.



it convey they to a handsome house in a good neighborhood, and

Aaron's heart throb with gratitude as he see in it all the

memorial of his old home which he and Rachel hold dear.



on the wall be the portrait of himself and Rachel which have be

present to he on the day when all his friend have assemble to do

he honor. happy tear run down Rachel's face as Aaron walk with she

through the room and describe their content. in the study he

pause, lift something from the table, and place it in Rachel's

hand.



"your silver-mount pipe," she exclaim.



"my silver-mount pipe," he answer. "my life, with this pipe and

the dear picture of yourself sit beneath the cherry tree, and

hold your dear hand, I could go through the world in perfect

happiness and content."



"o Lord of the Universe," say Rachel, clasp her hand, and lift

her lovely face, "I thank thee humbly for all thy goodness to I and

mine!"







the END.
